THE RED PAGE.
I am a hawker, and I hawk. I peddle laces,
boots, buttons, anything, from Port Darwin to
Port Davey, and back again by way of Albany
and Bundaberg. Order from me a ton of ice-
cream at Georgetown, or' a crocodile at Kalgoorlie,
and you shall have it. Terms, cash in advance ;
delivery any time from Monday week to the Day
of .Judgment ; but 1 guarantee delivery. As a
rule I have no time for literature : it is merely
literature. Hawking is Life. I tell you that
lying near my' horses in a dried-up Diamantina
waterhole, from which I had just dug out the last
quart of mud to make tea th it Scotch Tart never
equalled, and watching the stars glimmer over the
baked plain, I have had emotions higher than
Shakespeare’s. To be sure, Marinoopna’s head was
on my knee ; and she is the best gm I ever had.
Inspector Will shire, who saw her when we were
on the Fink ; River, was mightily taken with her ;
and Wiltshire is a judge. My dog’s head was on
the other knee ; and his eyes are as soft as Mari-
noopna’s. And I felt ... if I could only tell
you !
• • * •
Who am I ? It is no business of yours. Maybe
I was born of poor but unferm mted parents:
maybe I am the descendant of a highly-flavoured
race who habitually ran because the arm of the
law was gesticulating after them. I have danced
at your Government Houses ; I have dined at
your threepenny restaurants ; and —I have re-
flected that your Governors are far less important
than the kitchenmen at your threepenny restaur-
ants. The Governor is a male, fascinating but
useless ; the kitchenman is a male, useful but un-
fascinating. Take away the Governor, and the
metropolitan earthquake of traffic recurs daily as
usual ; take away the kitchenman, and the metro-
polis becomes chaos crying for its dinner.
* • * *
You may think me preju diced ; for I myself
was once kitchenman in a threepenny restaurant.
I took the billet proudly ; for I remembered how
St. Francis was scullion in a monastery, and
scrubbed the saucepans—seraphic St. Francis, who
called the flowers liis brothers and sisters. They
worked me fourteen hours a day for seven days a
week, and paid me os. for the week. But I got
“ board and lodging I fed on the haricots and
the braised steaks ; and slept- -where dial I sleep ?
By the Lord, I don’t know; ani/ichere. They
even tried to ram me into a waste-pipe to rest.
My arms worked like trombones as I played the
symphony of saucepans ; I had no time to'dry the
dishes with a cloth ; it was smack them through
the hot water to dry themselves. I lived in an
atmosphere of grease and grief ; stew mental and
Irish ; steam temperamental and culinary. Ah,
it is a soul-damping trade, this slavery of the sink-
hole ! Yet I have met people tender-hearted
enough to cry over the funeral of a cigarette, and
they never cried over me. When my last molecule
of hereditary pride impelled me to refuse to sleep
luxuriously in the waste-pqie, I was sacked, and
walked about the streets in a weary of days
and nights, waiting for windfalls. My life became
so full of ups and downs that I fancied myself
private secretary to a see-saw.
* * * •
Last spring, on the Russell River, I camped
near the biggest orchid I ever saw in my life.
Honest Jack O’Leary, who was with me, said he
could smell its perfume for a mile and three-
quarters ; and it looked so big that he might have,
though Jack’s sense of smell has been so culti-
vated in smelling out people who won’t pay their
rates that it isn’t a fair criterion. Anyway, my
brain was full of memories of the cheap Melbourne
restaurant; and I smelt nothing but beef-a-la-
mode.
* * * »
The cheap Melbourne restaurant is a phenome-
non unrivalled in its way. It is a dingy theatre
of the threepenny-bit, wherein takes' place an
awful drama of over-feeding. Fancy three courses
all the year round for threepence a meal ! Could
the shade of Luoullus arise, it would immediately
evaporate with incredulity. If I could invent a
patent pig, I would teach him to grunt a fantastic
grunt at some of the frequenters of cheap restau-
rants. Ihess threepenny epicures have as much
sympathy for their fellow-creatures as hot water
has for a bar of soap. Outside each cook-shop
there is generally a board on which is written the
bill-of-fare, comprising perhaps twenty different
dishes. The customers pass by this board, sit
down in the restaurant, and make the waiter call
out. to them the items of the menu. He does so
in a loud sing-song, like a curse that can’t stop.
He seems to utter some prodigious word, which, if
written, would go all round the wall of a workhouse.
He declaim > “ Stewedsteakstewedmuttonstewed-
lamb, ” &c., &c., with such loud rapidity that you
feel he would be clever enough to recite an elec-
toral roll in the time it would take a baby to fall
over a saveloy. He is training himself for recita-
tive in a cantata in hell.
* • • »
The customer dines, as he can dine nowhere else
in this frying-pan world for the money. Then,
like a gasping pair of gorged bellows, he slouches
out mto the street and leans up against a verandah -
post to recover. He lights his pipe and looks
sleepily before him, as if nothing could interest
hmi now—not even the resurrection of his grand-
mother’s old mop-stick, which he used to play
with when a boy. Over the way is a Hungry
Man with a face like a blighted dumpling. He
also leans against a post and makes vain efforts to
enjoy a famine. He inwardly curses the man who
has dined ; he prays that the base banquet of this
bloated crocodile may prove as indigestible as an
antediluvian idol. The Full Man takes no notice;
he may have bowels of compassion, but they have
been congested with threepence. The Hungry
Man continues to curse. 8 J
* * * *
If I had had time to smile, the credulity of our
customers would have amused me. They listen
to the extensive bill of fare, and do not know that
in the kitchen is a Magic Saucepan, out of which
come twenty different kinds of dishes. Some
lumps of beef are smacked into this utensil and
stewed, lhen, by black saucepan magic, its con-
tents are served up as stewed steak, .beef a la mode,
braised steak haricot steak (with a few carrots)
|lle or pudding (with a little
paste oi dough). The restaurant-keeper goes and
chalks up these dishes on his board! and damns
the expense. The scraps that remain from dinner
are paised througn a sausagi-maehine, and figure
on the menu at tea-time a< “ minced veal.” 'The
stale bread is made into puddings of extraordinary
il' Sometimes, to economise space, the cook
bo Is the plum-pudding in the soup. The curses
that frequently resound m a kitchen are enough to
unstiffen the neck of a holy image ; especially
b tter an 1 prolonged is the curse directed against
too customer who sends in for some meat for his
tML u ? wi UJU f h - WI Y do 1 talk about these
things ! Why do you ask me ?
* * » *
Yes, I know your Australia—no man better ;
and I love this sea-girt virgin sprawling under
the southern stars. I know your cities—your
warm genial Australian cities—what crowds and
life-tales pass through them 1 Some, happy as
sun Input on a toy-shop window; some, godly as
the gilt edges of a good girl’s prayer-book ; others
wicked enough to make a town-hall clock wring
its hands in depair. Melbourne, like a stuccoed
sultan in a seraglio of town halls; Adelaide, that
precocious young paradise of amateur angels •
Bnsliane, city of lovely lightnings ; Rockhampton
and lownsYille, like parched mermaids beneath
an ocean of sunlight ; Perth, a handmaiden point-
ing to the great golden spare-room; best of all,
Sydney, the shepherdess of human cities, on a
shore where the sea has wandered inland and lost
itself in loveliness. All health and sunshine to
Australia, and may her evil critics be sterilised !
i e 18 , new-life land, the oasis-country,
the welcomer of all wanderers.
* * m m
The two most typical figures in Australian life
are the sundowner and the larrikiness. The sun-
downer works, hard at being out of employment,
and walks through the country in order to run
i7ri°f l- >rov i f 4 ony - The larrikiness arrays her-
self like a cr.Vexed kaleidoscope in a tit, and her
language is c dculated to make the stoniest-
hearted peach biu ;h itself off the tree. Yet she
can love, and battle for her love like a prize-
fighting duck. She may be as wicked as a cap-
sized angel, but she has wonderful touches of
human n iture. She.sympathises deeply with her
femn.eoomra’os wh‘ii they return from a forced
uat to Cockatoo Island prison. She meets the
boat that brings them from their brief incarcera-
tion at ti o’clock in the morning ; away go a l the
girls to the bnck-parlor of an adjacent hotel; tb -y
rejoice over rum or beer ; then they go home, swop
clothes or hats, and quarrel over their bargains in
the back-yard when the afternoon comes.
* * » *
The larrikiness eats fried fish in bed, and when
her larrikin wakes up in the morning he finds he
has been sleeping on the bones and the stopper of
a vmegar-bottle. Then he curses all the curl out
of her fringe, till she says, “Nark it; you are
ratty, and plays a fantasia of finger-nails on his
cheek. Sometimes at night, after a brawn supper
bought in a Rocks eating-house, she arises in a
frightful nightmare and performs weird in nuta-
tions with her larrikin’s straw hat, shockin'" the
moon, when there is any mo%. Then she Tiiun-
deis back to bed, and lies down like a horizontal
horror beside her paramour, who snores as if ha
had been born in an organ-pipe out of tune.
* ' * * *
When her youthful charms fade, the larrikiness
is often so hard-up that she has to sleep out all
night. Perhaps one early morn she espies an
elderly man who looks as if he hal money and
wis at the fag-end of a nocturnal spree. For fun
he takes her into a hotel. She is so poor that she
is determined to captivate him, so, while he is
drinking and talking to the landlord, she sneaks
into the back-yard to black her boots. The land-
lord comes and yanks hur out. She has another
drink, and sneaks ba ,*k again. This time the
landlord finds her talcing a rouged rag from her
stocking and daubing it over her features till her
face looks red enough to take a first-class prize in
a tomato-show. She is “ making up ”in her
simple, humble, determined way,
* * » *
Much of the bush of Australia may be compared
to a panorama of poles. The wind blows through
the gaunt eucalypti, and, like harps, they moan
back, crying for rivers and waters unknown ; the
axe beats time to the slow, hot anthem of air; and
men and women drink tea as if in a previous exist-
ence they had lived through millions of years of
drought on a waterless planet. As a rule, the
people are kindly and cheerful ; but some of them
keep themselves alive by working their animals to
death; the men cultivate the soil, and the horses
cultivate a fasting capacity superior to Succi’s.
Then there is the laughing-jackass ; he alights on
a branch, scratches his ear with his left wing,
shakes his tail, and cachinnates enough to widen
a cracked joke into a miniature earthquake. Who
knows what the bird laughs at ? The native hear
is an oddity; he climbs up a tree at dusk, and,
like a little fool in fur, lifts up his voice to let
people know where he is. He sings a vesper song
wherein seems mingled the purring of a cat, the
braying of a phantom donkey, and the grunting of
a spasmodic pig, ending with a forlorn suspiration
like a crazy eookmaid sighing through a nutmeg-
grater.
* * • *
Don’t tell me the Bush is the abode of misery
and desolation. I know better. These things are
there ; but they are not all the Bush, are not even
most of the Bush. I know your Henry Lawson
and admire his talent'; but when he talks con-
tinually of the accursed country out-back—
Bah ! A town-man who made one trip into
the back-blocks live years ago, and has
stuck to the cities ever since 1 I tell you
the .. Bush is beautiful and terrible ; but
she is more beautiful than terrible. She has
days and nights of (1 lory. If your painters could
only paint the spirit of Australia with her frown-
lnn eyes and smiling lips—Australia, the mother
of heroes. lor there are heroes among your
drovers and horse-breakers—men who in obscurity
display courage and da.Ji that the most daring
cavalry leader known to history never surpassed.
Did you ever meet Sydney' Bob the Rider ?
What, never met the most famous and capable
bush-horseman in- Australian history! Young
m in, your life has been as much wasted as a con-
signment of patent stoves to Gehenna.
* » * »
For heroism, go to the Bush. And for charac-
ter ! You people write as if Australia was made
up of Spimfex .Tim and Wattle Bill. Why don’t
you tell the stories of the fine old erratic “mullet”
of N.S.W. northern rivers? Why don't you
describe that tribe of long, gaunt timber-
getters and back-country teamsters, hewing logs
away in the gullies. When the rains set in and
tne teams can’t haul, or the bars block the har-
bors and the mills stop, their man-and-beast
perishes ” would stiffen Bill and Jim, of Bourke
and Thargomindah.
* * * *
Go and write up the remnant of the old “ Horks-
borough” natives. Why, I remember enough
good stories about them to cover a bale of
newspaper. Do you know Tom Joyous, or
Dick? Young man, I blush for your ignor-
ance. They’re identities of th ■ W iter the
Brisbane Water, of course—Brokea Bay— the
Water. The “Old Man’’has engag'd, sacked,
and re-engaged them as skippers of trading
scows for over thirty years. Tom is quiet, soft
spoken, and at times very emotional. He has
never ventured beyond the Water. Dick has
traded to the Hawke and the ’Buecra. No one
within the memory of even Al. Settfee, who
caught the heavirig-line when Captain Cook came
ashore, has ever met Dick perfectly solemn. He
ci'it read or write. Grand head; tremendous
logician; magnificent memory. An Honest
Man —the noblest work of God. Fine-
looking fellow ; always neatly dressed, clean
white shirt, no collar; never wore a collar
in his life; lie always wears calf-skin elastic-
side boots, with very small high heels, which
make him walk like a hen going to communion
service. Dick- never had an accident of any kind
with a vessel; he is the safest man at a bar-
crossing on the coast. When he gets too happy to
get round town in comfort, he makes his way to
to ; foot of Market-street and takes the coaster’s
cab.
* ♦ * *
Years ago, you know—or you don’t know—when
trade was better, the coasting skippers never
dreamt of walking anywhere about Sydney ; they
always rode in cabs, and invariably paid gold coin
fares. With the decrease of trade the coasters’
cabs have dwin lied down to one. You can often
see him dow t at the Pyrmont Bridge. Perhaps
it is because o ie shaft [joints in the air and the
other is turned dangerously close to the ground,
and one spring is as rigid as cast-iron and the other
like, wet leather, there’s a kind of roll in the
motion that is particularly soothing to the jolly
mariner. Or, again, the charm might be in
the strikingly nautical arrangement of the harness ;
or in the angularity of the horse, which affords the
wayfarer considerable opportunities of observing
the inner workings of quadrupeds ; or in the crazy
seat in which the driver (whose head comes
straight out from the middle of his chest) hangs
by a hair and one worn bolt to the back of the
vehicle. Anyway, it’s a fact that a jolly
coasting skipper of the old school will pass fifty
nice cabs to enjoy the luxury of reclining in the
wet chaff cushions of the coaster.
• * * «
As soon as Joyous gets aboard the cab he removes
his boots; passes his hat out to the driver, who
carefully stows it under his seat; and lights a cigar.
In idle moments,-waiting for freight, etc., or when
he knows the Old Man is anxious for him to leave
port, he journeys to a well known coasters’ pub. at
Pyrmont, and seated in the bar with his boots
off he’ll play the concertina for hours. I
was with him there about three o’clock in the
afternoon, one day, when the Old Man’s agent
found us. Dick had charge of a big steam scow,
and had been on his best behavior for nearly a
month. The agent told him that a log freight was
ready at the Water, and he had better leave at
four o’clock. He put away the concertina, hauled
on his boots, hailed his cab and drove to the wharf,
where jay the craft. I came along too.
* » * *
At about four o’clock we came dashing down to
the agent’s office, and Dick said it was no
damned use trying to get away that afternoon.
The engineer was the only man'aboard; the rest
of the crew had gone away and got drunk with
the crew of another scow. Couldn’t find any of
them in any of the usual pubs. The agent’s
orders were imperative : the vessel must leave.
“ If you can’t find those chaps, Dick, we had
better make out their discharges and ship another
crew. The Old Man will be mad if you don’t get
away to-night.” So they made out the discharges
and proceeded to the shipping-office to fix up
another crew. They wanted six A. B. ’s, and it
was simple enough to get them. There’s a little
waiting-room at the end of the shipping-office
long-room, and if you look in there any day you
will observe a dozen or so lackadaisical jokers sit-
ting round a deal-table stabbing it with pen-
knives.
* » * *
The old messenger roared, “Six hands for a
steam-scow, coastwise, wanted.” Five or six big
red-faced, yellow-haired flatheads strolled in.
‘ ‘ Come on, you , ” roared Dick. * ‘ Here’s
the articles. Who’s cook ? What ?
Twitcherbitch ? Who the hell could remem-
ber that? Call yourself Johnsen. You the
mate? Right O; you’ll do; that’s O.K. Sign
on.” An official mumbled over the formula,
“Six months Sydney the port of shipment to be
the port of discharge sufficient without waste.”
Then with a pen formed of the rod from a chair-
back, with a J nib inserted and grasped right by
the nib, he signed “Dick Joyous.” Dick put a
cross, and then the crew —Otto Johnsen, mate;
Carl Johnsen, cook ; Gustns Johnsen, A. 8., and
so on—signed on. One Britisher, a little shrivelled-
up looking chap, signed “Charles Jones, fireman,”
and the crew was complete. Dick hailed a
second cab. He and three of the crew and myself
got into the coaster, the rest into the other, and
away we drove to Pyrmont.
When we arrived at the wharf the old hands
were aboard. They had learnt from the engineer
that the skipper wanted to start, and had got
everything ready. They were old cronies of Dick’s
—Trilby Jack, Woodbine Charlie, and so on—and
every one of them could carry a gallon of liquor if,
as they explained, you stowed it well down in
the hold. Dick, with his stilted, dignified walk,
strolled aboard and glared wrathfully at the old
hands as they clustered round the gang-plank.
“You’re sacked, you ; you’d better get
ashore.” Trilby Jack acted as spokesman. “No
fear, we ain’t going ashore. If you bring that
—— cargo of flatheads aboard we’ll chuck ’em
overboard.” “God damn it, here’s your dis-
charges, so you’d better get. I’m goin’ to start.
Come aboard, you ——
* * * *
The new crew jumped aboard. The old hands
blasphemed for a while, and then squatted round
the hatch. Some of the new men had taken their
coats off. One went to put his down in the
quarters. Trilby Jack threw it overboard. The
fellow, a big Swede, rushed at him. Dick had re-
moved his boots and was swinging the wheel over
ready to turn. He rushed at Trilby and knocked
him down the hatchway. Then they all went at
it. For about twenty minutes skin and blood
fl w. At last the engineer, the only man onboard
who hadn’t a black eye,- came ashore and got into
the coaster, who had waited to see the fun, and
drove to the agent’s. The agent advised bringing
the vessel round to the Quay and getting the
water-police to put one crew or the other ashore.
The engineer hurried back. They were all too
bashed and battered to fight much, so he cast off
tii i ropes, went ahead, and got round to the Quay.
When they made fast all hands came ashore to
Danny Lynch’s and had a drink. Then the new
hands were bribed off with a few shillings, and the
shiji went on her way in peace.
* • *
Now, there’s hundreds of yarns like that wait-
ing for you; and you chaps ought to be able to
dress them up a lot better than 1 can. I’m only a
hawker. Let’s get out of this and have another
look at the Harbour. I never get tired of looking
at your h irbour. It is a valley of violet waters

JUBILEE YEAR.
Established 1819.
Provident Society.
With the close of the year 1898, the Australian Mutual Provident Society will complete FIFTY YEARS of its
existence. Members are invited to make this a RECORD YEAR by taking out additional policies themselves, and by
inducing their friends who are not already assured to become members. ALL POLICIES ISSUED before
the end of the year will share in the division of profits to be made as at 31st December next. Amount of Cash Surplus
divided amongst the members during the 49 years ending 1897 was £7,726,170. The Cash Bonus distributed amongst
the Policyholders for the single year 1897 was £415,235. EVERY YEAR A BONUS YEAR.
Accumulated Funds, £14,479,578. Annual Income, £2,094,706.
Directors I The Hon. Sir J. P. ABBOTT, K.C.M.G., M.L.A., Chairman ; THOS. LITTLEJOHN, Esq., Deputy-
Chairman ; A. W. MEEKS, Esq. ; BENJAMIN SHORT, Esq. ; J. T. WALKER. Esq. ; JOSEPH ABBOTT,
Esq. ; R. J. BLACK, Esq. General Manager and Actuary: RICHARD TEECE, F.1.A.,F.F.A., F.S.S.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Conversion of Crehazi Brown.
Quite tall he was, and dark and staid, he hardly
ever joked,
And when he did ’twas but a simple, saintly little
wheeze;
He did not swear, he did not drink, he prayed for
those who smoked.
And had a tiny cough, and drooped a trifle at the
knees.
Gehazi oiled his ebon hair, and brushed it very
flat;
His suit was black, his cheeks were lank, he smiled
as if in pain ;
He’d turned his eyes to heaven with such constant
ardor that
They took an upward squint, and never came in
line again.
He’d missions to the fallen, burning brands he
loved to seize.
Slim spinsters called him handsome, and they
spoke of him as pure.
He managed all their Temperance Tents and good
societies,
The Purists’ Band, the Ladies’ Guild, the Mothers
of the Poor.
And when a mission aiming to regenerate the
dears
In curls of gilt who serve behind the wicked city
bars
Was started, he was chosen to invade their vicious
spheres,
And point them to a higher place beyond the
twinkling stars.
He went where men most congregate, and there,
with manners meek,
Besought the barmaids all to leave the haunts
where sinners herd.
Then whispered horrid tales at members’ meet-
ings once a week—
The women called it shocking, and they never
missed a word !
Gehazi said his mission was progressing very well,
But rumors reached the ladies —it was whispered
far and wide
At Brighton in a dog-cart he’d be cutting quite
the swell,
With a peroxided female flaunting boldly by his
side.
The hopes of frosty spinsters to the point of Zero
sank;
They looked well into matters, and emerged with
faces glum.
The funds of five societies were missing at the
bank,
And cash in hand consisted of a single battered
“thrum.”
They called upon Gehazi some sufficient cause to
show,
But none of them could find him, though they
sought him near and far—
He’d wandered to a better land, where good
levanters go,
With Flossy Jones, a frisky girl who served
behind a bar.
SILAS SNELL.
Out Back.
Where the billy and water-bag droop in your hand
And the swag feels heavy as lead,
Where the sun beats down with a cruel hate
And the crows croak the lay of the dead,
Where the flies in myriads swarm around
And the red dust marks the track,
Where “ the Golden West ” is a mocking lie—
That’s the glorious land “ Out Back ” !
Where the rolling downs are a desert plain
With never a blade of grass,
And the starving rabbits in thousands sit
As the weary travellers pass ;
Where the road that leads to the setting sun
Is only a camel-track,
And the crystal spring but a dry clay-pan—
That’s the glorious land “ Out Back ” !
Where the station-hand is a half-crazed man
Who talks to himself and horse,
As he follows the track of a straggling sheep
On the sun-dried watercourse ;
Where “ the forest monarch of lordly mien ”
Is a half-starved, cowering black,
And the squatter king but a banker’s slave—
That’s the cursed land “ Out Back ” !
N.S.W,
F. J. MURRAY.
Markin’ Lambs.
“ All aboard !" The call’s surprisin
When the mornin’ star is risin’,
And it’s ’ard,
With the blanky, bleatin’ chorus
Of the woollies waitin’ for us
In the yard—
jn the sanguinary, dusty markin’-yard.
Bolt yer brekfus-chops an’ damper,
An’ to graft, or be a tramper
Off the track.
“ Whip across an’ block ’em, Bowser ”
“ Speak up Smooger ” —“ Damn you, Towser !
Fetch him back —
Bring the crimson, cross-eyed, wool-bound breaker
back ! ”
Through the force-pens, through the race, an’
Into catch-pens—take yer place, an’
Don yer dress;
Mine’s a chaff-bag, yours a white-skirt,
And the squatter sports a night-shirt—
Nothing less ;
Shepherd’s blood-stained “ Fancy Dress ” an’
nothing less.
Fore an’ hind legs held together,
Dumped on rail to cry of “ wether !*
Or “a ewe !”
Clip go markers—knives are gleamin’—
“Tail off, Gulliver!” —blood’s streamin’
On to you,
Hot an’ sticky, blood is squirtin’ on to you.
Let them out, then count the tails, an’
’Eap ’em up across the rails, an
Rush the dams ;
Thro’ the race again, me hearty—
That’s the way we act Macarty,
Markin’ lambs ;
Jes’ the very way we do it, markin’ lambs.
N.S.W.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.

any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 22, 1898.
The State v. Australia.
There is no more important phase of the State right
question than that dealt with last night in the resolution
concerning the alteration of provincial boundaries by the
Federal Government. There can be no right more vital
to the existence of a State than that of preserving its
own integrity, If this goes, everything else that may be
retained is of but secondary consequenoe. The Common-
wealth Bill does not guarantee State integrity, and it is
to provide for this omission that the resolution affecting
alteration of boundaries was brought forward. The pro-
posal of the Government is that the boundaries of no
Staf.e shall he with unless with the consent of
its own people. To that extent the question is put
amongst the list of those covered by State rights. The
mistake was made in giving too much power to the
Federation, aqd too little to the province immediately
concerned, Tlye Convention treated it. as paramountly a
Federal question, whereas its more vital aspect is that
touching the State, Under the resolution passed last
night Hi' B is reversed, It remains for the Federal
Government to enact a law for a change of boundaries
but it can only do so with the concurrence of the State
whose territories are to be trenched upon. This is a
prudent safeguard, which may prevent serious troubles
as the future produces its developments in these colonies.
And there is no State more interested in having such a
guarantee than New South Wales.
As soon as Federation is accomplished the two impor-
tant provinces of Riverina and the Barrier will, to a great
extent, cut themselves off from this colony. Commer-
cially they will come under the domination of Victoria
and South Australia, and the centres which dominate
their trade may he expected to largely influence their
politics. There may be no reason to suppose that any
advantage would be taken of this by the Federal Govern-
ment to s/'cure an alteration of boundaries by which the
present colony would suffer any injustice. Even were an
attempt made to do so under ordinary circumstances, we
might safely rely upon the Imperial Government to frus-
trate it if New South Wales thought the matter of suffi-
cient moment to warrant an appeal to that power. But
while all this is admitted, there st ill remains the fact that
the Bill, as it stands, contains a temptation which, for
the sake of all parties concerned, it is better to have out
of the way. We speak not now on behalf of New South
Wales exclusively. The guarantee of State integrity does-
equal justice to all. It leaves none at the risk of bavin"-
its territories whittled away for the benefit of others, and
ren lers it impossible to use any such threat for purposes
of political coercion.—Sydney Telegkapii, October 14.
* * « *
In the above statement Sydney Telegraph,
the organ of the parochial party in New
South Wales, puts a very important matter
about as clearly as it can be put—from the
strictly parochial standpoint. In the Draft
Constitution—the one accepted by a majority
in New South Wales, Victoria, Tasmania,
and South Australia —it was provided that
no province should have its boundaries
altered in any way without its own consent.
That was a large and a very foolish conces-
sion to parochial prejudice, still the clause
dealing with the matter could be altered in
the same way as any other clause of the Con-
stitution if a very large mijority decided
that it was absolutely unworkable. The
alteration demanded by the minority in New
South Wales is that the guarantee offered to
each province against any alteration in its
boundaries shalL be declared to be absolutely
and permanently unalterable under all cir-
cumstances. The New South Wales As-
sembly accepted this idea by a large majority,
and it is quite probable that the other pro-
vinces will do likewise. Yet it is a miserably
unjust and near-sighted idea, and contains
probably more evil- possibilities for the
future than any other item in the Draft
Constitution. The fact that Barton’s voice
was lifted in opposition shows that Barton
is at least a far-seeing statesman. The fact
that it was not lifted a great deal louder and
more persistently than it was shows that
even Barton hasn’t half the foresight that a
Federal leader ought to have.
* * * *
ihe case in this matter has hitherto been
put only from the standpoint of the pro-
vincial capitals, and the provincial majorities.
The Bulletin desires now to put the case
for the minorities as shown in a few in-
stances :
Southern Riverina : This district forms part of New
South Wales, thoug-h its nearest, most convenient and
most cheaply-reached port is Melbourne. A great part
of it is absolutely denied any railway communication
with its natural port ‘2OO miles or so awav, and has to pay
freights for 400 miles to Sydney in order that its trade
may profit the traders and middlemen there. Then it
has to pay 400 miles extra sea-freight to Europe. Another
part of it has no railways at all, because the trade won’t
pay for a 500-mile railway to Sydney, and a short, cheap
line to Melbourne is refused it by the Sydney majority
on pure dog-in-the-manger principles.
VVestern, N.S.W. : Has its natural, cheap, and con-
venient. outlet at Adelaide. It could be developed by
short railways connected with the S.A. system, but most
of it has to do without any lines at all because the Sydney
majority says it must trade with distant Sydney for the
benefit of the Sydney middlemen.
North and Central Queensland : These districts are
governed from Brisbane, at the remote end of the pro-
vince. Their railways, by the exaction of heavy rates,
are made to yield, on the average, a handsome profit
over and above interest and expenses. The South has
cheap rates which result in an enormous loss, and the
North stands its share of the loss. The Central lands are
sold in immense areas to help cover the loss on the South’s
railway business. The North is rapidly becoming an
Asiatic province, but it can’t rid itself of the curse, largely
because the Tory vote of the South won’t let it do so.
The South suffers very little itself from the Asiatic curse,
and its policy is that it doesn’t care whether the North
does or not.
The Westralian Goldfields : Are refused a convenient
trade-outlet to Esperance Bay, and are burdened with
enormous railway charges by the long line to Perth, which
is twice the distance of their natural port, because the
Government party has property there and wants to keep
the city boom going. Also have to pay over 1000 miles
needless sea-freight to the East through going round by
Perth. Have about one-tenth their fair share of Parlia-
mentary representation, and no visible show of redress.
* » * *
In all these cases, and many others which
will probably arise later on, the minority has
a fair claim to be heard. None of these dis-
tricts had any voice in the arrangement by
which they were attached to the capital cities
to which they don’t naturally belong. Of
course, according to the strict logic that the
majority can do what it darned well pleases,
they have no right to kick. Neither, in the
same way, had the Cuban minority when
the Spanish majority (Cuba belonging to
Spain by exactly the same right that North
Queensland belongs to South Queensland)
decided that it should be harried by
Weyler ; or the Christian minority in
Turkey, when the Moslem majority decided
it should be shot or burned ; or the nigger
minority in the Southern States when the
white majority decided that it should be a
spanked and unpaid serf ; or that very small
minority, Dreyfus, when the great French
majority decided that he should remain in
prison without fair trial. The case is not so
aggravated, but the logic is just the same.
* * * *
Under the proposed system there will al-
most certainly never be any sub-division of
the unwieldy provinces of Australia by
peaceful means. The only way they can be
peacefully sub-divided is by the assent of the
State Parliament. It is always the minority
that wants to secede because its in-
terests are sacrificed to those of the majority
and it is exploited for the majority’s bene-
fit: If it, becomes the majority and
cap secure secession then it won’t want
to secede ; it can simply start exploiting its
old tyrants. And the majority never be-
lieves in letting the minority go ; it is the
boss Pharaoh, and the other crowd are the
Israelites. If the proposed policy had been
in force 50 years ago, the almost certain re-
sult would have been a gory civil war. For,
Under it, Victoria, which was woefully neg-
lected and misgoverned by the old auto-
cratic bosses of Sydney, could nor, have got
separation without the consent ofNew South
W ales and the latter province never showed
the remotest sign of giving a voluntary con-
sent. Then when the great gold time influx
made the South for a while vastly more
populous than the North, the former would
probably have ceased to want separation. It
would most likely have simply shifted the
capital of the whole province to Melbourne,
and started misgoverning the subject dis-
trict, of New South Wales from there, and
reduced Sydney t,o the status of (foulburn or
Newcastle. And that proceeding would
probably have driven New South Wales to
assert itself with the first weapon which
came to hand. These are the results which
the proposed policy would have produced in
the past, and, as Australia’s history is only
just started, it will probably produce quite
as bad results in the future.
The Telegraph, in stating the purely pro-
vincial, as against the Australian view of the
case, takes Riverina and the Barrier as its
favorite examples. In doing so, it admits
that they will naturally trade with Victoria
and South Australia, if they are allowed the
much-vaunted privilege of commercial free-
dom. Also, they will naturally take their
politics from Adelaide and Melbourne ; and
it tacitly admits that they may want to belong
politically there also. And its own idea is
all about the wrong which will be done to
the other part—the Sydney part —of New
South Wales if it isn’t allowed to retain them
against their will—if. like the Southern
planter of old days, it is forcibly deprived of
its nigger. The idea that these districts can
have any right to a hearing in the matter is
wholly ignored. The fact that Australia in
general has any right is also ignored. Yet,
it isn’t to Australia’s interest that North
Queensland should remain largely unde-
veloped through Southern mismanagement,
or that there should be a howling desert at
Esperance Bay, where there would be a large
commercial city but for the fact that it is
sacrificed to Perth. Reckoning in North
and Central Queensland, Eastern and
Northern Westralia, and certain small dis-
tricts of New South Wales, the prosperity of
nearly half Australia is liable to be sacrificed
simply in order that Perth, and Brisbane,
and Sydney may gain thereby. And if Aus-
tralia in general has no right to interfere,
what is Australia there for ?
* » • *
Of course there is no reason to suppose
that the New South Wales legislators par-
ticularly want to bind North and Central
Queensland in perpetual bondage to the
South, or the W.A. goldfields to the sand-
groping oligarchy at Perth. They would
probably prefer that Queensland should split
up, for if it doesn't the single province may
grow to such dimensions as to make New
South Wales only the second partner in the
Federation. All they want is to make sure
that their own nigger—Riverina and the
West—is safely chained up, and to be certain
that he will always have to trade with Syd-
ney however much he may lose by the pro-
cess, and that he -won’t have any beastly
rights of his own in the matter of deciding
his own destiny. And as the grievance of
Riverina and the West is, comparatively
speaking, only a small one at the worst, it
would have been much better and more
honest if New South Wales had simply
demanded a perpetual guarantee for its own
autonomy, and let the other provinces take
their chance. It might not have looked so
well, but it would have been better for Aus-
tralia. North and Central Queensland, ne-
glected and exploited and nigger-infested by
the policy or neglect of the ruling gang in
Brisbane, have a genuine grievance. And the
proposal to sacrifice the interests of that and
other vast territories for ever, or as nearly
so as any constitution can get to, merely that
the New South Wales provincial minority,
while seeking to prevent a small patch of
their own territory from finding the cheapest
and most convenient trade-outlet, may look
not quite absolutely selfish, is a mean and
low-down transaction.
» * • *
The Bulletin’s own idea is that it would
be much better for Australia if it were
divided into thirty states instead of six. It
would lead to a more general diffusion of
population, and to the growth of a
multitude of reasonable - sized towns in-
stead of three or four bloated cities and
a wilderness. Therefore it holds that any
region which wants to secede from the State
to which it is attached should be allowed to
do so, provided it can show a certain mini-
mum population, that the population is
reasonably unanimous in the desire for
secession (the unanimity to be ascertained by
a poll to be taken under Federal super-
vision), and that the Federal Legislature can
see no reason to believe that the severance
will injure the interests of Australia in
general. But then this paper reserves all
its pride and ambition for Australia, and so
long as Australia prospers it doesn’t care a
straw that one State should be bigger or
more rampant or inflated than any other.
If any place in Australia can be made richer
and happier and more prosperous by sever-
ance from its present State, and the loss to
the remainder of the State is less than the
gain, then the balance is clear profit to
Australia at large, and the severance
should be permitted. If the preservation
of the alleged rights of “our” State or
any other State to have its boundaries
kept inviolate means a shilling of loss to
Australia, then this paper votes without a
moment’s hesitation that these boundaries
should go into the melting-pot forthwith.
No State has any rights whatever, if these
rights involve a wrong or a loss to the
country at large. The Bulletin has said
all this before, but a thing has to be said
many times before the public begins to have
a faint recollection that it heard something
about it. And as this question probably re-
presents the worst danger in front of the
coming Commonwealth, it takes the chance
and says it all over again while there is still
a hope that the reiteration may lead to good
results.
The Federal City and Other Things.
New South Wales Assembly last week decided,
as one of its suggested amendments in the
Federal Constitution, that the capital of the
Commonwealth ought to be in N.S. W ales.
This doesn’t necessarily represent much pro-
gress; any Government in the Seven Provinces
or in Fiji or Papua could get its Legislature to
feel quite convinced any time that the Federal
capital should be in its province ; the work of
resolving that we should have something valu-
able, and the other fellow shouldn’t have it, is
one about which all parties are beautifully
unanimous. The Assembly decided that the
want of an arrangement for fixing the Federal
capital in N.S. W. was one of the great reasons
why N.S.W. rejected the Commonwealth Bill
on 3rd. June —which statement is a good deal
of a sham and fraud, for N.S.W. accepted
the Bill and agreed by a considerable majority
to leave the fixing of the capital to the
Federal Parliament. Also it held that if the
capital is fixed in N.S.W. that province will
probably reconsider its decision and accept
the Federal scheme—which is also a gross mis-
representation of the facts, seeing that N.S.W.
has already accepted, only Parliament calmly
nullified the popular decision, and, figuratively
speaking, stuffed the ballot-box, and cooked
the returns, and threw the Referendum over-
board. The Assembly decided, by a large
majority, not to imperatively demand that the
Federal capital should be on the unprotected
sea-coast at Sydney, and the exact part of
N.S.W. where it is to be is left for future
decision. But the Reid Government is out on
the personal and undisguised grab, and demands
to have that capital in its own province, some-
where—Sydney probably preferred.
* * * *
Curiously enough, the Cornstalk Assembly
says not a word about the Federal railway pro-
blem, which is the inevitable concomitant of
the other question. It is not the barren honor
of possessing the capital which is in question
so much as the profit to be derived from it.
The Federal city will probably grow, in a very
few years, to be the third or fourth in Australia
so far as size is concerned, and some day it
will inevitably be a place of great size and
wealth and importance. The reasons why The
Bulletin has constantly advocated the creation
of a new capital in the centre of Australia,
somewhere on the highlands beside the M‘Don-
nell Ranges or thereabouts, are that it would
draw this new population to that part of the
country which wants population most, and
would do something for the lean hog instead of
pouring all available blessings on the fat one.
Also it would “develop the interior,” which
every politician is very anxious about in theory
and cares nothing about in practice; and by
planting population and wealth there it would
make it possible and profitable to build rail-
ways right across Australia—a work which is
otherwise likely to be postponed for a century
or two. Likewise, in such a locality the
Federal Government could become sole land-
lord at a very cheap rate of a good-sized
Federal province, and retain all the unearned
increment, and realise a splendid future revenue
out of its ground-rents, and make the grandest
experiment in the way of land nationalisation
which the world has seen for many centuries.
N.S.W.’s demand that it should have all the
extra wealth and population to be derived from
the ownership of the Federal city, because it
has already more wealth and population than
any of its neighbors, ends all these splendid
possibilities. But it starts a new problem,
which is likely to cause a sea of troubles.
* * * •
Said problem is as follows: If N.S.W. is to
have the Federal capital what kind of com-
munication does it propose to allow the rest of
the Commonwealth to have with its own capi-
tal? Of course, it is impossible to plant the
new city in any place where all the provinces
will have an equal chance to get a share of the
trade. But it might be placed so as to give
two or three provinces an equal show, or so as
to snatch all the trade for Sydney alone. Sup-
posing it is planted so as to be fairly equidis-
tant from Sydney, Adelaide and Melbourne:
N.S.W. has already emphatically demanded
that the provincial system of railway manage-
ment shall be continued, and that any province’s
right to shut out the trade of any other province
by its railway charges shall be practically un-
limited. Therefore, the other provinces naturally
want to know if N.S. W. will construct its share
of a direct railway to each of the other capitals,
and if it will undertake to make fares and
freights equal between the Federal city and all
the three provincial capitals. Obviously, if the
absurd and ruinous system of provincial rail-
way control is to be maintained, the province
which owns the Federal city can annex all the
trade and profit thereof. It can do this simply
by refusing to give the new city direct railway
communication with any capital except its own,
or by charging prohibitive freights, except on
the lines that connect with its own ports.
When the selection was left to the Federal
Parliament it was safe to assume that it would
require the province in which the capital was
located to give all reasonable guarantees that
it wouldn’t cut off the rest of Australia from
free communication with its own political
centre. But if one province demands the capi-
tal to begin with, then it is - fair to ask what it
proposes to do in this matter.
* * * ¥
This is a problem which no other Federal
Government has had to face. In the United
States, Canada and Mexico the railways are
private property, and the private companies
build them to make the shortest and cheapest
line from the place they leave to the place they
want to go to. There is no question of pro-
vincial boundaries or prejudices ; no desire to
cut off one city from communication with
another lest “our” trade shouldn’t come to
“our’’port; and no building of 500 miles of
railroad for the sake of mere local animosities
if 200 will do equally well. In Argentina and
Switzerland (where the principle of State
ownership is just being adopted), the Federal
authority is the owner of all State railways,
and again provincial jealousies and petty ani-
mosities are shut out. Australia is the one (pro-
spective) Federation of the crowd which is fool
enough to seriously contemplate leaving if.'


State railways, on which national development
mainly depends, under separate provincial con-
trol. Therefore it is the only one where this
' And the question l’ onerf
great importance. It tne i euet c ,
were located, say, in the west or south-west ot
N.S. W., and it was furnished with the most
direct possible communication with Sydney,
Melbourne and Adelaide, it would grow o e
great inland commercial centre, as well as a
political metropolis. If it was simp y > e a _
[own on the end of a sawn-off line leading to
Sydney, it would be nothing in particular
And, considering the ways of provincial policy,
the latter is w’hat it most certainly wi e, 1
provincial railway management is let on 1
present basis.
• * * •
The Reid Government makes no secret of its
desire to abrogate that wise proviso in _ie
Draft Constitution which enacts that tne
Federal city shall be located on purely federal
territory. If that proviso is abolished, it
follows that the revenues to be derived from
the capital will be the property of one State
only, instead of belonging to all in common.
The capital will be built up by the expenditure
of the money of all the federated States; anr
if the land values which they all help to create,
and the incomes which they all help to P a y
to public officials, and the incomes derived
through trading and other pursuits in the city
which owes its existence to Federal expendi-
ture, are taxed for the benefit of one State
only, that State, the richest in the Confederacy,
proposes to do a very hungry reach-out at the
expense of his poorer neighbors. If the capital
is placed on Federal territory, this difficulty
will be averted, but the railway problem
remains. The Reid Government makes very
little disguise of the fact that it wants the
Federal city, in any case, so near to Sydney
that it will be entirely surrounded by N.S. W.
territory. The Federal Government will then
be able to construct lines inside its own little
scrap of territory, but it can’t connect its capi-
tal with any of the other provinces except with
the permission of the subordinate State, which
blocks the way. The Federal city problem and
the railway problem are very much mixed up
in many ways. Obviously, any State which
starts, as N.S. W. has done, by demanding pos-
session of the capital, all on its own, should
also explain what it proposes doing with the
capital when it gets it. -
Those “Sacrifices.”
The debate in N.S.W. Assembly on that pro-
posed Federal amendment which demands that
the capital of the Commonwealth shall be in
N.S.W. territory, was a long, shriekful reitera-
tion of the old lie about the special “ sacri-
fices ” which the province is making for the
sake of Federation. It was declared over
and over again that the capital must be in
N.S.W. as a small measure of compensation for
those sacrifices, which nobody could define,
because N.S.W. has, so far, flatly refused to
make any sacrifice at all; and the Assembly
took quite a s'erious view of the subject. This
same attitude on the part of a small section
of N.S.W provincialists is about the worst
stumbling-block in the way of Federation.
The whole story of these alleged “ sacrifices ” is
this: N.S.W. keeps its railways, its public
lands, and all its other assets, just like the rest
of the provinces. It keeps its debt also ; if any
other province has a bigger debt N.S. W. doesn’t
go shares any more than anybody else. It
agrees to pay exactly the same rate of taxation
as the other provinces, and not a penny more.
If it pays more in the aggregate because it has
more people it gets as much more for the same
reason. It keeps its rivers. Despite the fact
that much of the upper waters of the Darling
come down from Queensland, and that prac-
tically all of the Murray, till near the place
where it quits N.S.W. territory, is made up by
the influx of streams from the Victorian Alps,
N.S.W. claims the absolute ownership of both
rivers, and the Federal Convention made no
serious effort to dispute the claim. It is dis-
tinctly provided that the Federal Parliament
can make no law which will deprive N.S.W. of
the reasonable use of these rivers, and unless
it wants the unreasonable use of them that
should be sufficient.
* • * •
As regards the struggle of Victoria for the
Riverina trade and that of S. A. for the trade of
the W T est and South-West, N.S.W. makes no
concessions whatever. These remote places, be-
ing far nearer to Adelaide and Melbourne than
to Sydney, only trade with the latter city under
compulsion ; the commerce is, as far as possible,
forced Sydney wards by the construction of roads
and railways in that direction only, and by the
levying of high freights on trade which
goes away from Sydney and low ones on that
which comes towards the N.S.W. capital.
N.S.W. got distinctly ahead of its principal
rival, Victoria, on this point, for it induced the
Convention to prohibit the carrying by any
State of goods from another State at specially-
reduced rates, and thus clause 103 abolishes all
the vast system of reductions (ranging from
5 to 66 per cent.) by which Victoria now carries
off the Western and South-western trade. At
the same time N.S.W., according to its own
railway officials’ reading of the clause, re-
tains its right to cut rates in the Riverina.
It is done by a simple application of
the “zone system.” For instance, a six-ton
truck of chaff is carried at these rates:
100 miles, £1 Bs. Id. ; 200 miles, £2 Bs. 6d. ;
300 miles, £2 18s. 6d. ; therefore it can be
carried from Sydney to Junee, half-way to Mel-
bourne, for £2 18s. 6d. The same truck, coming
from Melbourne, would pay £2 Bs. 6d. to
the Victorian Government for the 200 miles to
Albury and .£1 Bs. Id. to the N.S.W. Govern-
ment for the first 100 miles on the N.S.W.
rails to Junee ; therefore for the same distance
the VicLorian would pay, not £2 18s. 6d. but
£3 16s. 7d. If the railway arrangement in the
Draft Constitution holds good, N.S.W., in-
stead of giving up, in a commercial sense,
large tracts of her territory,” as alleged by
Premier Reid, will, to a great extent, recover
large tracts that are already lost, as the worst
form of Victorian competition will be shut out.
As regards the tariff question, N.S.W. makes
a very good bargain. It is at present r leetra e ,
the other provinces are mainly .
They definitely give up one-half of their policy
by establishing interprovmcial Freetrade. s
for the other half, the Federal Parliament has
to decide, but the Keid policy wins the first
half of the battle to a dead certainty. JN.b. W.,
with its huge land-revenue to fall back on, is
financially safe in any case. The other P lo '
vinces, having no such land-revenue and being
dependent on customs revenue instead, take the
risk of insolvency if the Reid policy is carried in
the Federal Parliament. And if that policy is
carried, and the other provinces go broke,
N.S.W. doesn’t promise to assist them by a
single penny per annum. The only sacrifice
N.S.W. makes is the concession to the small
provinces of equal representation in the senate,
and on that point Victoria loses very nearly as
much as its neighbor. All these things, of
course, are no reason why N.S.W. should not
have the Federal capital if it can get it ; but
the eternal wail about the special “sacrifices
made by N.S.W. in the cause of Federation is a
very mean and maudlin way to get it. Besides,
the old brassy lie grows wearisome. Apart
from the bogus “sacrifices,” the capital is
claimed as the inalienable right of N.S.W.
because it is the “ oldest and richest and most
populous ” of the provinces. As regards its
age, N.S.W., Victoria, Queensland, and part
of S.A., and the Northern Territory were all
lagged together on the same day, and the whole
territory, as a British possession, is the same
age. As regards the other things, the Federal
capital will bring population and wealth wher-
ever it is located, and if the right policy is to
bring these where they are least needed there
is no more to be said.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The “Age’s” Beautiful Dream.
The Victorian House of Landlords cast out
another democratic measure or two last week,
and the Age again weeps a little about the al-
leged impregnable position of that great un-
paid snag. If the powerful Syme organ, instead
of sitting on the public soul like a cubic mile
of wet blanket, and depressing the Victorian
people by fairy-tales about the impregnability
of the 48 old Bogies, would tell the people that
the House of Landlords is only a house of
cards after all, it would do a lot more good.
And if it would show them how that ancient
snag could be blasted out of the way, and
would crusade against it with the same ferocious
vigor that it showed when it pursued Speight
in the old days, the snag certainly could be
shifted. Nowadays the Age tells the public to
wait and hope. When Federation comes the
local Parliament will have less to do, and
in the natural order of things a Bill will
be passed reducing its numbers, and alter-
ing its constitution generally, and then
the long-delayed reform will happen along,
quite naturally. Which is about the weak-
est kind of comfort imaginable. The
Council may agree, when Federation comes, to
a reduction of the numbers of both Houses, and
again it may not. Quite probably it will say
that it is an unpaid House, and obstructs re-
form gratis, and therefore it doesn’t need to be
reduced, but that the Assembly, being a paid
institution, had better be cut down one-half in
the interests of economy. Quite probably, too,
it will insert an amendment making its own
franchise still narrower, and increasing the
property qualification for members, and making
itself a more bigoted and awful House of Land-
lords than ever, and will offer to pass the
reduction measure on these terms, and no
others. The idea that Federation will make
the present 48 dreadful old proprietors volun-
tarily surrender one iota of their authority is
more wildly improbable than Rougemont’s dog
anecdote.
Sarg'ood’s Snuffle.
Frederick Sargood, who, when any progres-
sive measure is before the Victorian Council, is
the boss Solomon Eagle of that sad congrega-
tion, was very, very deeply grieved at the pro-
posed abolition of the multiple Property Vote.
He asked the Council, as the boss Tory always
does, to consider the awful risk of putting the
w'harf-loafer on the same voting-level as great
minds l.ke Pitt, and Peel, and Oladstone,
and the like. And then, by way of setting the
matter right, Sargood implored the House to
give a double vote to any boor, or publican, or
swindler, or illiterate bottle-gatherer who had
Property, and leave the man who has only
Mind and no Property down on the intellectual
level of the wharf-loafer aforesaid. This is the
sort of thing which makes the Vic. M.L.C. look
such a mean, degraded, crawling hypocrite as he
unfortunately does—though it may be that ap-
pearances are deceptive, and he is only a preju-
diced and benevolent numskull after all. He al-
ways begins by asking why the man with a great
Mind—the scientist, philosopher or philanthro-
pist-should be left, by the pernicious One-
man-one-vote idea, on the same grade as the
unclean lingerer on the Yarra bank. And,
having worked up his fellow-fatmen by this
tearful tale of w'rong, they all quietly plant the
scientist and philosopher and philanthropist
right down on the same grade as the dirtiest
loafer in the crowd, and give the extra vote to
the person who owns a slum property let to
Flossies and Chinamen. Even in its professed
yearning to give an extra vote for thrift the
Council is a very great fraud. If a man has
been so thrifty that he has reduced himself to a
skeleton, and has thereby acquired £30,000 in-
vested in State securities, the Council doesn’t
offer him an extra vote —not much. But if he is
a drunken spendthrift who has inherited a house
which is let to a Chinaman and the Maly Ann
to whom the Chow should be married but isn’t,
and he only keeps that house because it is so
strictly tied up under the will that he can’t sell
it —then he qualifies for the double property
vote advocated by F. Sargood. The Vic.
Council never inquires about the Thrift of the
dual voter. It doesn’t care a farthing either
for Thrift or for the intellectual person whose
degradation to the voting level of the wharf
loafer gives it such a severe grief ill the stomach.
It always comes back to the old point and gives
the extra vote for bricks and mortar, no matter
how acquired. Its one god is bricks and mor-
tar ; the rest is mere snuffle and hypocrisy.
Beak Vagaries.
At the quarterly meetings held last week the
two Sydney licensing-benches gave further
proof of their determination to defeat the in-
tention of the Legislature with regard to
Australian wine. Many years ago Sir Charles
Dilke wrote that Australia was destined to
become the vineyard of the world. As tending
to show that the prediction will be fulfilled
when the law is improved and, what is more
important, intelligently - administered and in-
terpreted, the oldest and, perhaps, the largest
firm of London winedealers, Sandeman, whose
continental dealings are colossal, recently in-
structed their Sydney branch to give the
hitherto despised “ colonial ” article a
trial. With that view, several new re-
tailers’ wine - licenses were applied for,
but proceedings in court showed that the
licensing-benches were in no way inclined
to encourage a native industry. The Sydney
magistrates have made a law of their own that
no more new wine licenses shall be granted
except to restaurant keepers who wish to supply
the liquor with meals, or to grocers who sell by
the bottle. A substantial baker was accordingly
refused, and, in another case, the bench de-
creed that, while grocers were recognised, they
drew the line at greengrocers. Owing to a
vagarv of the law itself, one applicant was
refused because his premises were assessed at
,£49, whereas the Act prescribes an assessment
of <£so, though he promised to improve Ins
premises so as to increase the assessment, ihe
absurdity of the situation is shown by the
nature of the perfunctory objection put forth
by the police on such occasions, namely, “ That
the proposed license is not justified by the
reasonable requirements of the neighbourhood
and that so many hotels are within a certain
radius.” But Bung’s own solicitor, Mr. Bull,
showed that not one hotel in a hundred sold
native wine, which should have squelched that
objection in one act. However, the bench up-
held the absurd objection in most cases. The
Bulletin hopes tliatM.L.A. Crick will carry
out his expressed intention of bringing the
matter before N.S.W. Parliament.
Gov. M'Gregor’s apology for the New Guinea
concessions is to hand, and very lame it is.
The doughty Administrator is particularly
entertaining when he asseverates again and
again :
Not the slightest step was taken by the Administrator
in regard to the syndicate without the usual and neces-
sary concurrence of Queensland.
“Concurrence of Queensland” signified, of
course, concurrence of “Ugh” Nelson and
nobody else that we can find any record of. In
answer to the late Premier Byrnes’ strictures
“ On the Administrator must also be laid the
blame for not having the other colonies con-
sulted ” — M'Gregor remarks that he never
knew they had to be consulted. Unfortunately
for this last statement we have all a very clear
recollection of the wily Papuan Governor’s
presence at a certain conference of Premiers
down South, where he was much interrogated
about the future prospects of the Possession by
the heads of certain Governments that had no
business to do anything at all beyond putting
their beastly hands in their vulgar pockets.
In another remarkable passage
admits that he discouraged previous appli-
cations for land, inasmuch as the granting
of concessions “ could only have resulted in the
creation of open racial hostility,” etc., etc. . . .
If that reason applied when an Australian
syndicate wanted to buy land, why doesn’t it
apply when an English syndicate wants to buy
land ? Altogether, the document is very pretty
reading !
John Collins, the man whom N.S.W. Judge
Gibson sentenced to three years' imprisonment ,
for the theft of half-a-tin of baking-powder,
given to him as rations, has been released, after
serving 20 months of his sentence. Evidently,
Mr. Gag Gould has really left the Ministry for
Justice. Collins’s case has always appeared
to The Bulletin one of the most disgraceful
on the long list of N.S.W. judicial miscar-
riages. He was employed to erect three
miles of fencing for an out-back selector, and
was to be paid a small sum and furnished
with rations. The selector stopped the job
before completion (owing, says Collins, to the
difficulty of getting materials to the ground) ;
they squared up ; and Collins, as he alleges,
returned the remains of the flour and baking-
powder given him. The selector and Collins
apparently parted good friends ; but eight days
later Collins was arrested—charged with
having stolen the remains of the baking-pow-
der in the tin. Collins says he returned the
tin ; the selector says he didn’t; the tin was
never found ; and Judge Gibson actually
awarded three years’imprisonmentinthis appar-
ently wretched squabble, where there wasmerely
one man’s word against the other, and the whole
cause of the trouble wasn’t worth 6d. ! Was
there ever such asinine administration of jus-
tice outside N. S. Wales? The Bulletin
considers that the community owes Collins a
re-hearing of his case, and would very much
like to deduct the costs of such a re-hearing
from the irritable Judge Gibson’s salary.
+
The N.S.W. coal-weighing difficulty is gradu-
ally coming to a head. All along it has been a
remarkable proceeding and full of instruction
to an alleged self-governing community. The
law, enacted by the combined wisdom of the
people’s representatives in Parliament, enacts
that all coal must be weighed as it comes from
the mine, and the miners be paid for the exact
amount extracted. So far, no one has taken
any notice of the law, except by letting it slide.
The mine-owners simply ignore it. The
Government has made no attempt to enforce
it ; that GoD-fearing institution took it upon
itself to tacitly cancel the law on its own re-
sponsibility. It didn’t profess that the Act
was bad and attempt to procure its repeal ; it
simply allowed the employers to break it at
their own sweet will, and winked at their
doing so. In fact, it, to all intents and pur-
poses, conspired with the mine-owners
in their illegal acts ; if it didn’t con-
spire actively it did so passively. Now
it is announced that the long-neglected
statute will begin to be put in force at.some
date a little way ahead, and the Government
will then cease to be a passive accomplice. Ah
of which is very interesting. The law for
giving a poor man three years’ gaol for taking
6d. worth of baking-powder is in force all the
time. Why shouldn’t the law which is sup-
posed to protect him from possible fraud by
the mine-owner be in force all the time also ?

According to the Government returns just is-
sued, Victoria, at the end of 1897, had 52,701
persons employed in factories as compared
with 50,883 in N.S.W. Since 1894, when
both provinces had about struck bottom,
the employes in factories have increased by
11,428.’ The increase in N.S.W. during the
same period has been 8132, and most of this
has been an apparent increase only, caused
by the change of system in ’96 when places em-
ploying only four hands were first reckoned as
factories. The two provinces now estimate on
practically the same basis and have done so for
two years ; but if they had done so all along
the number of N.S.W. factory employes in
1894 would have been about 46,000 instead
of 42,751, and N.S.W.’s increase for the three
years would be about 4000 against Victoria s
11,000. In his last electoral campaign Premier
Reid stated that N.S.W., by reason of its
Foreign-trade policy, had 5000 more hands em-
ployed in factories than Victoria, but that was
merely the podgy man’s usual inaccuracy. The
province with the smaller population and the
miserably deficient coal supply—though coal is
the great essential in almost all manufactures
still keeps persistently ahead of the one with
nearly four times the area, with the larger
population and the abundance of coal and the
50 years start, and all the other advantages.

The idiots of the Tory press who used to rave.
about strikes, labor-in-politics, and agitators
“driving capital out of the country, are
seldom heard from nowadays. Yet one occa-
sionally bobs up to remind us of the good old
faith. For instance, Hobart Mercury, be-
moaning the lack of manufacturing industries
in the Land of Sleep-a-lot, remarks
Sad to say, but true, the selfishness of the labor-classes,
the constant hunting and harrying of capital as the enemy
of labor, has much baleful influence in turning away
“ enterprises of great pith and moment.”
Hobart hasn’t seen an eight-hours’ demonstra-
tion for nearly 10 years, no strikes ever happen,
no aggressive labor organisations exist, starva-
tion wages are meekly accepted, the average
worker habitually raises his hat to his boss and
fairly grovels before priests, parsons, and (ye
gods !) M’sP. The “enterprises of great pith
and moment” ought surely to happen under
these circumstances, but, somehow, thev don’t.
The fact that the Dukes of Clarence and York
planted trees at Kernell, Point Solander, the
south head of Botany Bay, will probably
make the “ naicest ” people support the
N.S.W. Minister for Lands in his action
in reclaiming, and dedicating as a public
park, the spot whereon Cook first put foot
on the shores of Australia, much more than
the historical associations that cling to this
classic ground. It is noteworthy that Clar-
ence’s tree is dead, like its planter. York’s
still lives, and has a wind-tortured being. By-
the-way, it is proposed to resume at present
only ten or twenty acres. • But it should be
remembered that, besides such ancient memo-
rials as Cook’s first landing-place on Aus-
tralian soil, Banks’ and Solander’s botanising
arena of first research among Australian plants,
Forby Sutherland’s burying-place—grave of
first white man, so far as we know, interred in
Australian earth, there is in the neighborhood,
and about a mile south of Point Solander, the
most interesting spot to Australians (and, inci-
dentally, to Australasians) to be found on Aus-
tralian soil, to wit, the spot whereon Capt.
Cook hoisted the Union Jack for the first time
on the shores of the Great South Land, thus
taking possession of a continent, and uncon-
sciously laying the foundation-stone of what
hereafter will' be Federated Australia. The
resumptioh should include this almost more
than historical locality.
For a marvellous transformation-scene, com-
pare the reception of Hori Grey’s one-man-
one-vote speeches in the ’9l Federal Convention,
and the reception of Edward Braddon’s de-
fence of one-man-more-than-one-vote in Ade-
laide at the opening of the ’97 Convention.
Compare, too, the old man’s plea, received with
guffaws, for one-man-one-vote in one House in
’9l, and the little-contested adoption of the
principle in respect of the two Houses in ’9 l.
Also compare the actions of those who cheered
the Grand I'. l Battler in ’9l with their action
in ’9B in cursing the makers and supporters of
a Bill which gives a more liberal franchise thap
even Grey ever asked for. a

Some remarks which The Bulletin made
a few weeks ago about the Incriminating Docu-
ment in melodrama receive further endorsement
from the weird ways and proceedings of “My
Jack”—now in possession of Sydney Lyceum.
“My Jack” has the Document breaking out ali
over it, or, rather, the Birth Certificate, which is
brother to the Document, and an even more
illogical stage fiction. It has somehow come to be
a recognised legend of the “boards ” that no man
can prove his identity and come into the property
—now unlawfully collared by the wicked baronet—
unless he has his birth certificate. Also, that
there is only one copy of it extant—therefore he
fights like a bag of lions to recover it, and pursues
it all over the earth, instead of simply going to
the registrar’s office and getting another. Also,
when he has the certificate, he comes into the pro-
perty immediately. In real life you can get a
copy of anybody’s birth certificate for 2s. 6d., and a
barrow-load of them assorted for £lO, and they are
valuable to wrap up meat in; but on the stage it is
only necessary to show the document to the proper
authority—who is generally the policeman—and
he hands over the estate at once. The amount of
property which changes hands in this way on the
edge of the pavement on a wet night in London is
tremendous. Also, he sentences the uncle and
the fraudulent solicitor to a long term of imprison-
ment right there, and then plods On his way trying
doors in the rain at 275. a week.
* * * *
“My Jack " is a long and bitter struggle
for the certificate, by Benjamin Landeck, in five
acts. The certificate is cast up by the sea once
along with a dead drowned woman who lies on the
beach with the sunlight playing on her wet hair
after the eels have got her soul. A Greek of
reprehensible character gets it and keeps it for
many years, while the depraved nobleman rages
all over the globe in search of it. It is buried
secretly at dead of night, like Sir John Moore, and
is accidentally found again, and the finder is
murdered for the sake of it, and then it
isn’t on his dead body after all. The real owner
carries it about carelessly for a while and gives it
away ; and finally, the Greek (every stage Greek
is an awful character) explains all about it while
perishing frequently in a red red desert by the
Nile, and the red sun listens to the story, and the
giraffe makes the quickest retreat he can in the
distance. Then the birth certificate being restored
the hero goes home, and on its sole evidence
he seizes the property with a strong hand
and appoints himself to be baronet in place
of the other man. The scene is laid partly
in the weary old Soudan, where Rignold and
Baker and Diver and Norman and Plimmer
and Bland Holt and Holloway and many other
stage characters have rallied round the old
Flag so often that it 1 is about worn out
now, and there are holes in it large enough to
throw a goat through; and where every breeze
turns over the leaves of some big warlike drama or
other. Also it is partly on the deck of a man-of-
war where the British sailor revels in dance and
song and leads an idyllic existence; and partly in
a lighthouse which is blown up ; and partly out on
the wet, wet sea, hanging on to a spar ; and partly
everywhere.
** m &
Dante, who commenced to rather doubtful
business at Sydney Palace—the community being
dubious about conjurors and one-man shows, and
the Palace being a luckless house at the best—has
now settled down to be a solid success. The
gorgeous little theatre is full every night, and
seems likely to remain so for a while, and the
Professor spreads himself in new marvels so
rapidly that the public has indigestion trying to
assimilate them. All his tricks are neat and
clever, but some of them are actually flabber-
gasting. In the presence of a committee of well-
known, affluent, bald, respectable citizens, he
plants a youth in a chair on the stage and wraps
a curtain round him, and in about ten seconds it
is withdrawn to reveal that youth securely tied
hand and foot. Then the wretched victim has
his mouth filled with water as a guarantee that he
doesn’t blow on anything, and five coins balanced
on his .fingers to prevent him grabbing at any-
thing ; nevertheless, when bells and trumpets are
dropped over the curtain the trumpet is blown
upon and the bell rings furiously, and both are
hove ten feet in the air. The committee snatches
furiously at the curtain, only to find a bound
boy dozing in the chair with his mouth still
full of water and the coins still balanced on
his fingers. After about a dozen attempts the
committee retires sulkily. Probably the most
remarkable rio : of the list, however, though not
tj is prettiest, is in old card dodge with variations.
It is the ancient device of a pack of cards on a dish
under a glass cover, out of which pack certain
cards selected by the audience rise one by one, and
show themselves, and then drop in a ghostly
fashion into the dish. Dante’s variation is that he
lets anybody in the audience hold the dish, and
that person’s eyes bulge out till umbrellas could be
hung on them trying to discover the spring, and
the hair attached to the card, and the other
properties. But he never discovers anything,
except that the cards rise up one by one. An
individual who was once an expert in the conjuring
biz. himself held the dish one night, and he got no
BuStS 0f ths “ T >“
