** * *
Sydney Criterion re-opens on Saturday
under the management of Alfred Dampier, with a
new play, and a new company, and new scenery.
Sydney is very much overcrowded with shows just
now but The Bulletin trusts that there will be
standing* room for the new venture somewhere.
** * *
Warren and Bristol’s “great Eastern
Variety and Circus ” came to Sydney unobtru-
sively last week and pitched its tent on the usual
site adjacent to Redfern railway station. It is a
very respectable circus as circuses go; about as
great as could be expected, and moderately Eastern
—quite sufficiently so to satisfy anyone who isn’t
an unreasoning hog for Orientalism. Professor
nstols end of the show is somewhat discounted
Dytheta ct that Sydney has seen most of it before,
at the time when the Prof, occupied the Criterion
stage, and the balmy odour of horse smothered the
traditions of the old Brough and Boucicault days.
Still it is good enough (for those who like horse)
to stand many repetitions. The present writer’s
verdict m the Criterion days was that it was very
clever and very wonderful, and that he was weary
to death of horse, and, except in the cause of duty
wouldnt take the trouble to fix his faded brown
eye on the most astonishing mule that ever per-
vaded the earth. Even if he heard that Al-Borak
and Bucephalus and the Centaur were all grazing
on his lawn, he would simply impound these
historic animals (by deputy) and send in a bill
for the grass. And in this frame of mind he
simply desires to say again that the present circus
is a very good circus indeed; and that Professor
Bristol s animals are without exception the clever-
est and best-trained set of horses and mules that
he ever saw or met or heard of. And he devoutly
trusts that they will meet with the great prosperity
they deserve m Scandinavia ; and that the busi-
ness they will do in Peru will be simply enormous,
be doesn t say this in any ill-feeling or disparage-
ment; he is simply tired of horse, and if there is a
profitable engagement sticking out in Egypt or
Guba he wouldn’t ask any horse to miss it on his
account.
** * *
Miss Ada Reeve has quitted the Tivoli,
and Biondi will be gone before these remarks
appear m print. Miss Cora Caselli, who has
brought a graceful leg and a magnificent high-kick
all the way from London, fills the gap to some
extent however, and Lottie Moore and the
tremendous electric organ are still present, so
there is nothing particular to complain of. The
organ is a bulky mystery which breaks out in ex-
plosions all. over the place, and arouses great
enthusiasm in the soul of the infant upstairs.
** * *
John Coleman and the rest of the new
Alhambra co. have galvanised that old riotous
haunt into something like prosperity again. Time
was when you could lose most of your clothes in
the Alhambra inside five seconds if you even
looked disrespectfully at a local “gun” on Satur-
day night, but those days have passed away, and
the ancient house is almost a quiet Christian
establishment now. Coleman himself is good
value for the money, and his name alone should
drag the enterprise along for a while.
** * *
If Brough made a lob of money in India
the profit was fairly earned. The co. had to work
hard, and the managerial portion of it—i.e., Mr.
and Mrs. 8.-—must have slaved like niggers.'’ At
Calcutta, where they boomed for three months
and put the financial success of the tour beyond
all doubt, it is the rule for visiting companies to
play three nights a week only. The Broughs
played six nights a week, changing the bill every
Wednesday and Saturday. In Hongkong, they
did equally big business, and barring a brief “off”
period m Shanghai the show was coining money
throughout a season of some 150 nights altogether.
At the Melb. Bijou the Vic. Operatic
Society is giving a sweet, clean and crisp musical
performance of “The Bohemian Girl,” a better
performance in its semi-grand opera character than
one would expect from an ordinary professional co
these times. “ The Girl, ” regarded as a story for
grown-up people, is about fifteen times more ridicu-
lous than Maritana, ” and no Australian manager
is hjply to produce it at vast expense ever again.
In the hands of the Y.O.S. amateurs the book of
the opera becomes a painful joke, although they
are a smarter dressed and less angular crowd than
amateur actors in general. If it could assist the
maintenance fund of St. Vincent’s Hospital to tell
the V.O.S. warblers that they score a dramatic
success in this old-fashioned show, The Bulletin
would stretch a point—several points— to flatter
them; but seeing that “The Girl” travels solely
on the musical score there is no reason for over-
stating the truth. All the principal parts and
choruses were well sung on Saturday, and with
the exception of a tiresome minuet the perform-
ance didn’t provoke any bad language in front.
Horace Stevens, J. Goudy, Miss Millie Chapman,
William Neilson, A. W. Pitt, and Miss Layton,
took musical honors at the first attempt. During
this week the cast will be sometimes different in
three places, but even if the new people are a little
weaker than the originals “The Girl” will be a
deserving effort, and the balance-sheet ought to
show a profit for the Hospital aforesaid.
** * *
The season at Melb. Royal has begun to
hang out signs of approaching dissolution. On
Saturday a misguided management put up “The
flying Scud ” in a sick and sorry style, with the
idea of catching a horsey public on the hop. Two
previous shows at the Royal had some expensive
teatures to recommend them, anyway; also they
were rather new and peculiar in their foolishness,
rioucicault s drama is neither new, nor spec-
tacular, nor foolish, and the people who
play it are expected to give the author a chance,
the author has a pretty bad time at the Royal.
Jt. D. Haygarth is an effective Nat Gosling, but
the rest of the co. ranges from moderate to worse.
Charles Maurice, the Franco-German manager,
whose foreign accent and cheerful self-assurance
qualified him for the first character he undertook,
tt't , s * ars ” himself as Mo Davis, the Jew.
Unfortunately, he doesn’t shine.
** * *
Another leading lady has been indisposed—
Miss Ada Ferrar, this time. Her part in “ Under
tne Red Robe ” was filled for two or three nights by
Miss Terriss, the little lady who used to gnash her
teeth at the “Chrwistian” girl in “ Sign of the
Oross. Save for her Jewish turn of the tongue,
which compels her to say “ trweasure, ”
prwomise, ” &c., Miss Terriss would have
been an improvement on Miss Ferrar. She
was not so stagey a heroine, moreover she
tended to wake up Julius Knight. “ Red Robe ”
goes rather more buoyantly than at first, and may
be further enlivened by the substitution of a
younger man for old Gladstone in the part of
Louis the servant—but no matter. On Saturday,
the lirm will stage “Lady of Lyons” at the
Princess s on account of an alleged feminine yearn-
ing to behold Julius Knight as Claude Melnotte
before he withdraws his fatal beauty from Mel-
bourne. Three performances of “L.0.L.” will
close the season, and on Wednesday (26th) the new
Comic Opera Co. make a first appearance at the
Princess’s in “LaPoupee.”

Business at the Opera House abafceth not,
and Peggy Pryde stands where she did. Like-

A well - informed correspondent: “ The mystery that
of d ! sa PP earance .Louis Poussard, the 11-

Saturday when the bright little fellow returned to
Sydney with the devoted nurse who took him away three
fishm,rs e e for th a tm h 1 a S - pl ? did wouldTal’unseL
hsn nurse—that modern instance of the constant service
of the antique world-have been for Ponssard’s f a vnU° P
novelist, Balzac ! And Elizabeth Boutcher was not only
the foster-mother of the son, she was stove - lighter,
washer and wringer, cook, errand-maid, and general
lion’s provider to Poussard himself. Her w eekly wage
was only PI, but, unfortunately, she did not draw this,
and when the old artiste died, all she held was a piece of
paper acknowledging that £230 was due to her. It is not
the fact that Poussard left nothing. Including a little
money in the bank, his violins and furniture, and money
owing to him, he was worth about £4OO. But the faithful
housekeeper never troubled about her wages. Her whole
thought centred in the child she had reared from infancy;
and she took him to Melbourne, in the hope that R. S.
Srnythe, her employer’s oldest friend and manager, would
arrange with the guardian, appointed eight years before,
for her to have the child with her. That was impossible.
But Srnythe did his best. Despatching the nurse back
to Sydney, he sent the boy to a station on the Murray,
thinking by this temporal separation to render the final
parting easier ; and he then visited Sydney to press on
the executors the exceptional claim of Miss Boutcher.
Talkipg about benefits, let M. Poussard’s many artistic
friends give a benefit for his unselfish and unworldly re-
tainer. Where could there be found a better cause ? ”
“Buskin”: “Stroller” is quite right. With one ex-
ception, all Australian actors copy, slavishly, the imported
mummer. Barrett, the raucous, is in Scotland, but I
heard him at a Sj'd. matinde only t’other day. Cart-
wright is also far away, but he can be seen and heard at
two Sydney theatres any night. But there is an ex-
ception. Australia possesses one native-born actor
(possibly the best she’s got at present), who copies no one,
walks naturally on the stage, and talks as if he were a
man, and not a phonograph. This rarity lets the wordsconie
out of the mouth that God gave him and doesn’t harpoon
them with his nose, after a prevailing fashion. Judging
by samples to hand he is fully up to compete with any
English actor, and do a lot better than most that are now
blazoning their antipodeeds. To disclose the name of the
only natural Australian actor w'ould be to invite an
avalanche, therefore it must be left in oblivion. Queerly
enough, Tub Bulletin, that earnest “barracker” for
all Australian products, seldom mentions the name of the
only native actor it is not wearisomeness to listen to.
Therefore note those who are constantly mentioned, and
the one who isn’t is the missing word. [N.B. — The Bulle-
tin gets lots of letters like this. Generally, the “ only
actor” writes them himself in a poorly-disguised hand.]
A woman lately lost her husband by an accident; and
to give her a start some kindly-disposed persons raised
£5O for her by means of a benefit performance at an
Australian theatre. This lasted her two weeks, £25 of it
going in a tombstone for deceased, although it was all she
had between her children and the Benevolent Asylum.
How characteristic of the best old aboriginal kind of
woman !
Australian artistes who have gone to England declare
that it is impossible to get a hearing there unless one is
prepared to pay about £5O down to an agent, who will
then “ run ” you, and afterwards square the press not to
give you unfavorable notices and so on, ad infinitum.
Arthur Deane affirmed that ’Gustus Harris was kindness
and hospitality itself to him on every point but one—he
flatly refused to hear him sing. After three months’
weary waiting Mr. Deane contrived that Sir ’Gustus
should hear him accidentally, after which the great man
at once gave him an engagement.
Are the street minstrel and pub. serio-comic recognised
as legitimate members of the “Perfesh?” These night-
pavement performers are to be daily seen sunning them-
selves at Poverty Point. They are equipped with Talent’s
conscious smile, and the more-or-less clean shave; while
with arms haughtily folded across their geui-laden shirt-
fronts, they exchange business aboriginalities and other-
wise obstruct the side-path. Of course, should they prove
to be bona fide “ pros. ” this scribe anticipatively apolo-
gises and withdraws any offensive observations prompted
by an inadequate understanding.
The second revival of a certain comedy was expected, at
time of writing, to yield an interesting libel case. An
actor who had taken the name-part a wdiile ago, ventured
to say (or so the story goes) that his successor couldn’t
play it “for sour apples,” whereat the other fellow was
naturally indignant, and threatened legal proceedings for
defamation of artistic character.
Dead—John Hennings, veteran scene-painter, all-round
clever man, and right good fellow. Came hither in the
fifties, and had ever since been connected w’ith the Aus-
tralian stage as scene-painter. He and Pitt were con-
temporaries. Hennings was for years in partnership with
Coppin, Stewart and Harwood as lessees of Melb. Royal.
They all saved their coin save Hennings, who came out
best, after all—since the others dropped their hoards
when the banks broke in the Boom. Hennings, the best*
natured of men, would nevertheless chuckle over this
fact, and point the moral, corner-stone of Bohemian

don’t “pla3 r up to one another” with enthusiasm. The

and the public pays to see them do it, and, if they were
true artists rather than petty-minded mummers, they
would do it. Being merely mummers, they never get
away from the thought of their private antagonism, and
they act accordingly.

Long Tunnel (Vic.) Sept, yield, 21430 z., bring-
ing the half-year’s average to loz. per ton. Divs. to date,
£1,208,400, equal to £503 per share, and there is a reserve
fund of over 110,000, and development-work shows many

20440 z. Both returns above the average —cause, the half-
yearly clean-up. The N. Long Tunnel has still over 500 ft.
to sink before it can reasonably expect to get into the
same country. South Long Tunnel, after a long bad time,
has struck gold, and has recently acquired some ground
from the parent mine, giving it an increased prospective
value. New Loch Fyne, another big producer of this dis-
trict, has had a big drop—monthly output about one-half
what it was, bringing down divs. and price of shares, and
causing many people to curse themselves for not jumping
at the offer of £2l a share made for the mine about a year
ago. Working its tailings by cyanide so far shows profit.

Stated as a humorous fact in Ballarat that the
manager of the Black Horse mine is also the proprietor of
a local sausage and baked-pertater stall. According to
latest reports, the “ Horse ” is panning-out splendidly —
seven sausages to the pound, or thereabouts.
* * * *
The new battery of 40 stamps lately started at
the Galleymont gold-mines is one of the finest gold mill-
plants at work in N.S.W., and is well worth inspection by
parties contemplating erection of similar machinery. The
ore is fed automatically through rockbreakers and feeders
without shovelling, and the whole is most substantially
built. The engines are a fine pair of horizontal compound
type, driving the battery with ropes. Mr. M. Eissier,
the managing engineer, speaks in the highest terms of
the construction and design of the plant, which was built
by the Austral Otis Engineering Company, of South
Melbourne.*
**. • *
Lord Kilmorey’s Largs Bay harbor scheme, to his
great chagrin, was blocked in S.A. Leg. Council owing to
an irregularity in connection with the deposit of £25,000
required under the conditions. The failure of the scheme
may be a blessing in disguise as its opponents had become
so strong that its defeat was pretty certain. The Bill
provided for a Govt, guarantee of £30,000 a year for 10
years and valuable grants of foreshore frontages. Kil-
morey introduced the Bill in S.A. Leg. Council owing to
the strong opposition threatened in the Assembly.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
When Melbourne sets to work in real earnest
to amalgamate its 20 municipalities, it will come on some,
surprising bits of information. For instance, the 20
municipalities spent among them last year £32,8/7 in
salaries alone. Melbourne city, with 17,830 ratepayers to
look after, spent • 12,081 in salaries. Prahran, South
Melbourne, Fitzrov, Richmond, St. Hilda, and Col-
lingwood, with over 43,000 ratepayers, blevved only £9944
in salaries among them. If Melbourne city would cast
its glittering Fitzgibbon traditions overboard it would be
a good beginning in the way of municipal reform.
* * # *
Perth (W. A.) city council offered 4 per cent.,
the other day, for a loan of £BO,OOO at par, and only got
£26,500. Since then it has placed some more fragments
of the loan here and there, but the episode is another sign
that the slump is bad in W.A.
• * * •
The Permanent Trustee Co. of New South
Wales—the joint-stock trustee which doesn’t do the
Pacific Slope with the property of the widow and the
orphan, and which offers the security of £975,000 uncalled
capital as a guarantee that, if some official does embezzle
the orphan’s patrimony and loses it on a horse, the loss
will be made good—sends along its balance-sheet for the
year ended 30th September, 1898. This is the eleventh
year of its existence, and the net profits (£2250) are the
best up to date. They compare with some previous re-
turns as follows :
September, 1892 .. £147 September, 1896 .. £1035
„ 1893 .. 1358 „ 1897 .. 1671
„ 1894 .. 764 „ 1898 . 2250
~ 1895 .. 1007
Up to 1891, the third year of its existence, the Permanent
Trustee 00. was a losing speculation, the business, in
those early days, being insufficient to cover working ex-
penses. Since it turned the corner, however, its progress
has been very steady. It paid its first dividend of 4 per
cent, in ’95 ; another 4 per cent, in ’96, and 5 per cent, in
’97. This year another 5 per cent, dividend is paid
absorbing £1250, and the other £lOOO, with previous
balance, leaves 4.2251 to credit of profit and loss. The
profits are equal to 9 per cent, on the small paid-up capi-
tal of 4 25,000, but the co. is well-advised to keep its
dividend down to 5, and it should continue leaving a
large margin between profits and dividends for many
years to come, and start to build up a solid reserve out of
the balance of the profits. At present, the Permanent
Trustee Co. has no reserve. Its £5 shares are only 2s. 6d.
paid-up, so they carry the enormous liability of
£975,000, and if some unlooked-for loss or disaster comes
along, and there is no reserve to meet it, this liability of
£4 17s. 6d. for every 2s. 6d. paid is full of possibilities for
the shareholders. Moreover, a trustee co., like a govern-
ment, and a bank, and a rat-trap with a bit of cheese in
it, and many other things, has to live on public confidence,
and a strong reserve does much in the way of inspiring
that valuable object.
* * • •
The Royal Bank (Melbourne) for the half-year
ended 30th September, 1898, shows a still further improve-
ment. The net profit is £4OBB, and compares with some
previous profits, as follows :
March, 1893 .. .. £2510 March, 1896 .. £2099
September, 1893 .. 1901 September, 1896 .. 2044
March, 1894 .. .. 3258 March, 1897 .. 3398
September, 1894 .. 2875 September, 1897 .. 3625
March, 1895 .. .. 2136 March, 1898 .. 3864
September, 1895 .. 2038 September, 1898 .. 4088
The profits are equal to £5 9s. per cent, per annum on the
written-down capital of £150,000, which is remarkably
good as present rates go, or £2 14s. 6d. per cent, on the
original paid-up capital of £300,000. A half-year’s
dividend at the rate of 3 per cent, per annum uses up
£2250, and the other £lB3B, added to previous balance,
leaves £5976 at credit of profit and loss account. Under
these circumstances, it is certainly time the Royal was
starting to build up a new reserve, by way of replacing
the £170,000 of the shareholders’ money which dropped
overboard in the late crisis. This little institu-
tion, the only Melbourne bank of issue which
didn’t suspend in 1893, begins to wear a decidedly pro-
sperous aspect. Its deposits (£280,575) are £38,000 more
than they were six months ago, and are larger than they
have been since September, 1893. The cash reserve
(£86,522) is equivalent to over 27J per cent, of all liabili-
ties to the public, which is solid enough for all purposes
and rather above the average. Barring the absence of
any reserve-fund, and of any reserve of State securities as
a desirable second line of defence, the balance-sheet of
the little Royal is an eminently satisfactory document in
every respect.

“Ordinary”: Colonial Bank preference shares
are probably a good investment at present rates if you can
afford to wait some years for a return. Considering the
low prices now ruling the return is likely to be a very
good one in the end, but it may be long before it arrives.
• * * *
The depositors of the Premier Permanent Build-
ing Society (Melb.) wisely rejected the scheme for the
sale of all their remaining assets to an English co. Said
assets were alleged by the liquidator to be worth, after
liberal deductions, £173,600, and the co. offered only
£113,750 for the lot. Also, it only offered a part in cash
and the balance b3' gradual debentures at very moderate
interest, and the security for payment of these debentures
was to consist, apparently, in the fact that the purchasing
co. had a paid-up capital of just £30,000. In face of these
things the P.P. depositors resolved to keep the whole
£173,600 worth for the present, and think of forming
themselves into a small co. for owning the property, and
collecting the rents, and realising the bricks and mortar
when the proper time comes. Most of them are weary of
the very name of the Premier Permanent, but the grab of
the English shindykit was too vast and impudent for even
a very tired depositor to endure.
* * * *
The Trustees, Executors, and Agency 00. (Mel-
bourne) has made a call of 10s. per share. This co. has
got into a serious difficulty over the deposit of its clients’
money in hanks which have since reconstructed and
locked up the cash. According to a certain clause in the
Act, any trustee co. doing business in Victoria was em-
powered, among other investments, to “deposit any
moneys of which it has control . . . with any banking co.
or banking corporation having a subscribed capital of at
least £300,000 ; a paid-up capital of at least £150,000, and
a reserve of at least £50,000, and which does not, as part
of its ordinary business, buy and sell land or shares or
other property.” This looks clear enough, and the T.E.
and A. 00. invested largely in fixed deposits accordingly.
But after the smash came the Victorian Courts decided
that the clause only referred to current accounts, not to
fixed deposits, and that the trustee co.’s were liable for
any loss incurred by their clients in the fixed deposit
business. As the Trustees, Executors, and Agency Co.
pathetically states, it is thus compelled to take over from
its clients £'60,000 worth (nominal) of deposit receipts
which are now worth only £30,000. Hence the call of 10s.
per share = £30,000.
The question is left—what receipts are they
that are only worth on the average 10s. in the £ ? The
clause prohibiting deposits in banks that dealt in land
should have made it impossible for the Trustees E. and A.
Co. to drop its clients’ money in any of the mass of mush-
room institutions which are dead and gone. Among the
legitimate hanks the City of Melbourne, Mercantile, and
Federal are the only ones which went actually broke in
Victoria. If the money, or a part of it, was in these, then
the co. ought to state not merely that its £60,000 worth
of deposits have a “ present market value ” of only
£30,000, but also that a lot of them will never have any
better market-value than they have now, and that the
consequent loss is going to be faced and written off. If
the co. had no clients’ money in these three banks, or in
some of the utterly rotten land banks, then it is difficult
to see how its deposits can be worth only 10s. in the £
all round, for reconstructed Victorian bank deposits now
range in value from 15s. to 20s. 2d. in the £. Moreover,
they all seem reasonably certain to be paid in full by-and-
bye, and they pay fair interest in the meantime, so
there is very little to complain of after all. So far as Tiie
Bulletin can see, there is no way in which the deposits in
question can be down to 10s. in the £ as an average unless
a good part of them are in banks that are utterly dead.
And, in that case, it should be clearly stated that there is
a permanent loss to be faced, and the shareholders should
be informed of the probable amount thereof, and how it
is to be provided for. Anyhow, if the co. did risk its
clients’ money in either the Mercantile, Federal, or City
of Melbourne, it says little for the management, for they
were all small affairs of very moderate repute at the best.
The Trustees, Executors, and Agency Co. has a
nominal reserve-fund of £40,000, but £32,000 of it is re-
presented by the badly-depreciated securities of the
Australian Deposit and Mortgage Bank, and apparently
no attempt is made to square up the depreciation by writ-
ing these securities down to their present value. Yet if
the co. proposes to face the situation by writing-off the
present loss, whatever it may be, it ought to have a general
cleaning-up at the same time. And if it doesn’t propose
to write-off the loss —well, what does it propose doing ?
* * ♦ *
The Coalmines Act regulating the weighing
of coals at the pits and otherwise determining contested
points was passed by N.S.W. Parliament two years ago.
The clause relating to weighing was, nevertheless, treated
as a dead letter by Govt., and when, after a year and a
half, pressure was applied, the Govt, found that the
Supreme Court required to be consulted. In 1898 the
Supreme Court decreed that the clause was operative and
clear for all purposes. Then again Govt, held back.
Finally, on the application of more pressure the Govt, has
decided to put the clause in operation in February 1899.
That is, in the third year after it was passed by both
Houses. How does that compare with the oft-repeated
Govt, statement that while the law is law it must be
strictly enforced ? Would not that clause have been en-
forced years ago had it, instead of favoring the miners,
favored their employers ?

* » * *
“ P.” : Now, I thought Bedford was no longer
“ Randolph the Reckless.” What does he mean, then, by
recalling, even in verse, Waukaroo ? It’s a reckless scari-
fying of a tender spot in the memories of thousands. For
Waukaroo (it was in the days when Randolph blossomed
into a mining expert at Broken Hill) was the centre of the
tin-boom. Some hundreds of leases were taken up, some
scores of co.’s formed, some hundreds of thousands of
pounds “invested,” and not a ton of tin was put on the
market. To-day there isn’t a single inhabitant; there
isn’t a more forsaken spot in all the droughty west of
N.S.W. It’s name was always the best part of it; but B.
might have let us go on forgetting it.

“E.W.”: Anyone who knows about N.S.W.
railway freights will fully endorse “ J.P.’s” remarks. To
drag a truck of stuff 501 miles costs £7 10s. less than if it
was taken only 424 miles. Sugar is also charged higher
for the 424 miles. Jam (a local product) is carried at the
rate of 1965. 4d., while canned milk, currants, etc. (im-
ported articles), are carried at the rate of 141 s. One
truck (say) of N.S.W. produce pays as follows Potatoes
and corn 315., oats 315., with 20 per cent, added (why ?) ;
brari, pollard, and wheat 20 per cent, added (why?).
Why don’t they charge a freight without any added
percentage ? The railway, instead of being a benefit to
some towns, is indeed a curse. The Commissioners make
periodical visits, but the only satisfaction one gets is—
“ Haw—the subject of freights—haw—is too large—haw,
haw—to enter upon—haw.”

The directors of Cobar Great Western are ask-
ing the shareholders to take up shares in the Federal
G.M. Co., which property they acquired in making addi-
tions to the Cobar plant. The winding-plant was taken
away, and the battery and (perforce) the mine left behind.
Now proposed to re-open with a co. of 5000 shares issued
as paid up to 12s. Chappie, boss of the Great Western,
gives a very encouraging report, but the chances are that
the whole of the money would be frittered away before
the co. knew where it was. Anyhow, the writer advises
the Great Western directors to devote their time to the
G.W. They are not so overburdened with mining ability
as to have any to spare for the Federal Co.
* * * *
Lake George Mines: Extraction of sulphide
ores abandoned, involving the closing-down of one of the
world’s finest and most complete copper pyritic smelting-
plants. Hundreds of men thrown out of work, for the
more destitute part of whom work has been found by
Govt. Proposed tq cyanide the gossans overlying the
sulphide zone—a doubtful proposition, and a short-lived
one, anyway. A horrible collapse in values—from 60s.
and 705., at which figures many thousands of shares were
sold. They can now be got, also in thousands, at from lOd.
to Is. 2d. Though probably the cheapest-worked copper-
mine in the world, allowing for distance from a railway
and other conditions, it was too low-grade to pay. It
started at about 3 per cent, copper ; then, as the output
increased anil the lower levels were opened, it fell to 2 per
cent. Even had it remained at that a fair profit could
have been paid ; but when it dropped to lj per cent, the
co. cried go. And for this unfortunate mistake in spend-
ing big money on a poor mine N.S.W. copper-mining has
to bear some of the burden.
* * * »
The interim report issued by Mt. David Co.
clearly shows that this N.S.W. mine has pulled itself out
of the mud more bv good luck than good management.
First crushing* were a great disappointment, and when
some heavy contributing shareholders found the co.
encumbered with a debt of £BOOO, and fully realised the
extraordinary way in -which it was floated; many nasty
things were said and some big threats made. A new
manager came along, and practically found a new mine.
He has taken out upwards of £20,0.10 i i gold, cleared off
the big debt, and left a substantial balance towards divs.
For every ton crushed, he says, three have been de-
veloped, and speaks well of the future generally; and,
inasmuch as a good mine acts like charity in the covering
of sins, the Mt. David crowd now seems to be a happy
family. Writer regrets having to say anything that
might disturb this harmony. By the statement of
accounts, over £5OOO has been spent on battery account,
about two-thirds of it on a new 20-head mill, which is up,
but has never been used. This report says : “ For various
reasons the completion of the erection of the 20-stamp
battery has been delayed.” What are the reasons? Has
not. the mill been put in a place where it is useless, and is
it not a fact that, much as the mine has been developed
this year, it is not yet ready for 30 stamps ?
“ Mac. ”: That Mt. M'Donald yarn is slightly
“off.” The man got drunk at Milburn Creek pub., and,
coming home to the Mount, lay down near a quartz-built
forge, at a copper-mine. In the morning, seeing the stone
was golden, he traced it, and the first crushing from “ the
Block claim ” went 6oz. to the ton. The temperance
moral still remains.

Coolgardie miners are kicking against the use
of cyanide tailings for stuffing stopes, alleging that pot.
cyanide poisons the atmosphere, so that miners have
occasionally to knock off and go to the mouth of the drive
for fresh air.
* * • »
George Brookman, chairman of the last meet-
ing of Coolgardie Mining and Prospecting Co., held in
Adelaide last week, mentioned that originally the syndi-
cate consisted of ten contributing shares of £l5 each and
five fully-paid. These shares now represented, in one
form and another, a capitalisation of £9,275,750. The
share-distribution represented £3,421,000, and £950,000
cash. The co’s working the ground pegged out by the
original syndicate had produced 17 tons of gold, equal to
£24,000,000. The meeting expressed satisfaction at the
management of the syndicate, and it was agreed to give
the liquidator an extra £l5O. We should think so. The
writer adds a few facts. George Brookman was the pro-
moter of the syndicate. He had made some money at
Broken Hill and by running a store in Adelaide. W.G.,
his brother, had tried to make some and had failed. He
was one of the prospectors. Pearce was the mining man
of the crowd —for it was a common or garden sort of expe-
dition, anyhow. Charles De Rose, a brother-in-law of the
Brookmans, joined them. He had been working at the
Broken Hill smelters. They were all shareholders,
the balance of the shares being held by Adelaide men,
except Colin Templeton, who, the writer thinks, held
one, and soon sold at a small profit. The prospectors made
for Hannan’s, which had just broken out, and, being un-
able to peg anything in the immediate vicinity, and hav-
ing no money or inclination to buy anything, they made
towards the lake. Here, acting under the advice of
Pearce, they pegged leases galore on ground now occupied
by the Great Boulder, Ivanhoe, Lake View, Associated,
and one or two other mines. From time to time the
syndicate had to be enlarged to keep things going. It
was in these enlargements that some men, to their ever-
lasting sorrow, dropped out ; and it is to the great credit
of George Brookman that he carried through all these
changes without involving himself or the co. in one law-
suit. That is a big thing to do when the history of other
great, though much smaller, mining concerns is remem-
bered. The millionaires of the £250 syndicate are the
two Brookmans. Whether by good luck or judgment, this
very ordinary party of prospectors pegged out the richest
mines ever located by one crowd in the world. Or if they
didn’t The Bulletin wants to hear of richer ones.
* » » *
It is long since a Westralian mine was floated in
Melbourne, and the last that was has been knocked end-
ways—the Kanowna, promoted by one or two persons who
hail scooped the pool before Mt. Lyell slump. They lifted
the shares to £1 and thereabouts, and it was not hard to
find a man in Collins-street who said they would go to £5.
They are now down to 55., and what they will go to no-
body knows. The new mill and the first crushing started
recently, since which the mill has shut down for want of
water, and the market has shut up for the want of con-
fidence. Same old moral: always sell before the crushing.
• * * *
The number, size, continuity, and richness of
the lodes worked by about a dozen Kalgoorlie mines finds
no equal in any known part of the world. Westralia had
a boom with mines that produced no gold, and now,
having a number of mines producing gold by the ton, it
has no boom. So bad has been the stump that, in the
face of tons of gold, regular and even yields and divs.,
it is doubtful if the market has taken the right measure
of values. Over a year ago, when the Associated Mines
had not reached one-fourth their present developments,
the shares were about their present price ; and a few
months ago, when the richness and permanence of the
mines were established beyond any possible doubt, the
Bottomley collapse sent them back to less than half their
present value. The first and second levels on these phe-
nomenal ore-bodies were so rich, and the formation so
strange to Australia, as to create suspicion of values
deeper down. The first shaft to reach 400 and 500 ft. was
that on a mine that had no surface gold. The others
were in no hurry to go down. Some were afraid
to sink. The lodes are going down as rich as ever.
Probably some of the rich chutes will be carried
into adjoining claims by the strike they are known to
have, hut lasting richness has been established. The
marvellous development which took place recently in
Lake View mine is richer than anything before seen in it,
and is probably the reason for Manager Callahan leaving
so mysteriously last week. He was first going to Perth
for a few days, then to Albany, then to Colombo to meet
Whittaker Wright, who was coming as far to save time.
Now stated that he is going right on to London. He has
got the mine in shape to produce at least 10,000 oz. a
month for years to come. The Boulder keeps up its
3000 oz. a fortnight. The Associated, when it gets the full
plant going, should turn out as much as the two. The
Ivanhoe is doing over 5000 oz. a month. The Brown Hill
will, perhaps, have a shorter life than the other big pro-
ducers of the field, but the first yield from the new plant
indicates from 6000 to 7000 oz. a month for some time, at
any rate. The Horseshoe is running 20 stamps on rich ore
and should have about 4000 oz. a month. The, Persever-
ance is now well-controlled in the shareholders’ interests.
The Kalgurlie seems to be the coming big producer of the
camp.
* • * *
Esperance (W.A.), which should be a large and
prosperous commercial town, has dried up. It has a good
harbor, and it is the natural outlet for Coolgardie, Kal-
goorlie, Norseman, and other goldfields. But the Govern-
ment resolved to build the railway to Perth instead—it
was about twice as long a railway, and needless extra
mileage cost an enormous amount to build, and the extra
interest is a heavy burden, and the extra freights over all
these needless miles are a tremendous handicap. Then
almost all the trade is with the eastern provinces, and
Perth is 1000 miles further from them than Esperance, so
there is 1000 miles of sea-freight added on as well, and the
whole thing is like Sydney having to import its potatoes
from Melbourne via Brisbane, and then bring them
back by rail. Through this disgraceful effort of
the ruling gang to boom Perth and their pro-
perty there, Esperance has practically faded away. A
recent telegram reported: “Nearly all the leading
business people are closing their establishments. Amongst
those who have announced their intention to close are
Mr. Bond, tank manufacturer, who is closing both his
Esperance and his Norseman factories ; Mr. Howard, tin-
smith ; Mr. Dickinson, jeweller ; and Mr. Elston, stationer.
On Saturday the Chronicle announced that its publica-
tion would be temporarily suspended. Messrs. Parker
and Co., tailors, are also closing. Messrs. Wills and Co.,
merchants, will close in another month, and many others
have decided to shut their business houses here, but have
not yet formally announced their determination to do so.
About half the'buildings in the town are already empty.
Nearly all the people are leaving for Adelaide, Mel-
bourne or Sydney. But few of them are going to
Norseman. The population of Esperance two years ago
was 1500, but now it is merely about 400, and by the end
of the year it is considered that it will be reduced to less
than 200.
• * * *
Prosperous Perth ! Recently a resident called
for tenders for a job of building a shed; worth about
£5 to the successful tenderer. Over 140 tenders were re-
ceived. As it would take a good man three weeks, the
contractor made about £ll3s. a week, and had to provide
his own nails, &c.
• * * *
Just at present, the ghost of C. H. James is
understood to be applying for a certificate in Melb. In-
solvency Court, and,' although the hairy boomster’s
wraith has no apparently improper purpose to serve in
asking for a “clean sheet,” there is certainly no good
reason why the spectral applicant should get it. James
went bung under peculiar circumstances, and when the
Law ordered him to come forward to explain some of the
circumstances, and throw light on certain settlements,
the Law’s order was persistently disobeyed. James, in
fact, was a scandalous insult to the Law during the last
year or two of his life. The mere fact of his happening to
be dead doesn’t qualify him for a certificate. Why this
attempt to whitewash James’s ghost ? The matter is to
be carried to the Full Court at considerable expense, and
the F.C’s learned opinion will be worth hearing.

Although Victoria has probably produced more
alluvial gold than all the other Austraiias together, it has
been left for Bull to find nearly all the money needed to
prove the existence of well-known leads into the deeper
and more expensive ground. Several Bull co’s are at
work, and the principal—the Vie. Gold Estates—has a
working-capital of £lOO,OOO, and is preparing to lift up to
five milliou gals, of water a day. Three other co’s are
combining to provide electrical power for all.

* * * *
Tiie closing scenes at Mt. Deddick, Vic-., are
pathetic. The railway scheme is still agitating the souls
of shire presidents and councillors of little hamlets like
Orbost, Cunninghame and Buchan, and the schemers are
making catspaws of the whole boiling. Considering the
large number of men working on the field— i.e., six— it
looks like a screaming farce, but the public loves to be
gulled. The field’s name is suggestive of a funeral, and
its demise is approaching. There is a postal service over
the mountains twice a week to a field where nearly every
lease is jumpable, but the owners know that nobody out-
side of a lunatic asylum would attempt anything of that
sort.
* » • •
Another attempt being made to resuscitate
Clarkville (Vic.), just across the N.S.W. border. It had a
mild boom some six years back, when inspection revealed
some fine bodies of quartz, little or no gold outside of a
few very short chutes, an abundance of water likely to
cause some trouble at small depths, and a magnificent
forest of timber.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
SOCIETY
The drought has settled down again on
N.S.W., and that province proposes to pray and
humiliate for rain next Sunday. That is to say,
it will stalk haughtily to church in its best clothes
and its most insultingly-expensive hat—or be
driven there by its proud, Sabbath-breaking
flunkey. If N.S.W. believes there is anything to
be got by humiliation then there is no reason at
all why it shouldn’t humiliate. Let it put on
its oldest clothes, and go to church humbly bare-
foot and in its worst hat, and let its wife and
daughters go barefoot also—with their hair hang-
ing down, and let everybody be really and obtru-
sively ’utnble all round. A community which
professes to believe it can avert a great calamity
by being ’umble, and yet flatly refuses to be ’umble,
is simply reckless.
» * * *
S.A. barmaids anticipate being extin-
guished at an early date, as O’Malley, M.P.,
wants to remit his abolition of barmaids proposal
to a Referendum. This can only have one result.
The average woman regards a barmaid as a home-
wrecker, and the S.A. female vote will therefore
go solid for abolition.
* * * *
At a recent South Australian agricultural
show, the ladies’ hack-race (lady riders only) was
won easily by a very tall, ungainly female, whom
nobody was acquainted with. The judges took
stock of her suspiciously. They discovered, among
other things, that she wore large bluchers, and had
tweed pants under her skirt. She wouldn’t take
off her veil, so they couldn’t inspect her back-hair
properly, but they asked her to step over and col-
lect her prize, and the way she fell over her skirts
as she stepped made them more doubtful still.
One judge thought of asking her to light a match,
to see if she would strike it on the back of her
skirt, but a brighter intellect asked her for her
ticket, and when she tried to put both hands in
her pants pocket in search of it, they told her she
was a man and a fraud, and bade her light her
pipe and get out. Then she gathered up her
skirts and “ scooted ” for it.
* * * *
From time immemorial woman has been
looking under the bed for a man, and hasn’t
found him. The other night a Melbourne cab-
driver looked under his bed for a woman, and
discovered her at the first attempt. She flatly re-
fused to come out, so the cab-gentleman dragged
her out and tried to stand her up, but she insisted
■on going at the knees, and flopped every time he
let go. Then he carried her into the yard, and as
she declined to shift any further he stepped out
for a policeman. When they came back the
woman had returned to the house and was under
the bed again, and as she hung on to the bed-post
with both hands, and the constable dragged with
all his might at her heels, she was very much in
the position of a lady on the rack. She warned
the constable that if he kept dragging like that
she would burst, but the officer held on and at last
tore her away. Then she used language. The
man under the bed generally ran as soon as dis-
covered, but the woman under the bed evidently
means to take the "bed with her when she departs.
* * » »
The MeagKer-Nortdn cowhide episode is
not the first of its kind in Sydney. Many years
ago the smellful Major Mudie, of Castle Forbes,
in his (now rare and expensive) book, “ The
Felonry of N.S. Wales,” sneered at poor old Judge
Kinchella for his deafness and other infirmities.
The blood of the Kinchellas was aroused, and a
son of the Judge, meeting Mudie in George-street,
gave him a very bad thrashing. The Major sum-
moned his assailant and had him fined. In 1860
Dr. Lang in his paper, The Colonist, made some
rough remarks about the family of Capt. Malcolm
McDonald, and the latter severely flogged the old
doctor in Hunter-street. Dr. Lang prosecuted his
assailant, who was committed to Quarter Sessions.
Before acting-Judge Holroyd he pleaded guilty
and was fined <£s, through which unpardonable
leniency Holroyd lost his chance of a seat on the
bench.
* * * .
A historic building will be offered at
auction in Nov. On Macquarie Fields estate near
Liverpool, N.S.W., are two large brick buildings,
formerly the residence of Gov. Macquarie. As
Macquarie left in 1821, these must be about as old
as the country can boast of.

“Tan. T.” to Bulletin: —Was in a
country chemist’s shop, the other day, when a
man came in for some arsenic. Questioned as to
what it was wanted for he said it was to put upon
a cancer on a cow whose milk went to the local
butter-factory. The chemist advised him to shoot
the beast. Some people allege that N.S.W.
Health Act unnecessarily interferes with the
dairyman’s occupation, but the fact is that inspec-
tion is altogether too lax in most country districts.

An old lady and her daughter were walking
one very hot day to Sydney Coast Hospital, when
Chinaman comes along in sulky, and seeing ex-
haustion of pedestrians, pulls up. “ You go Little
Bay?” “Yes, John.” “ Too faar ” (sympatheti-
cally). • “Much better you lette me lidee you
Little Bay.” They thank him, and as his sulky
will only hold two, the young woman suggests
that John shall take the matron, who is badly
exhausted. “No planky fear! ” says John.
“Old woman no good. Y"ou young and plitty.
You lettee me lidee you all round and get plitty
flower, O, welly ni ! ” And then he had to drive
on disgusted.
» * * *
An opinion gaining ground on the tiresome
Sunday pub-opening question takes the novel
form of advocating a special licence for Sunday
selling. Thus many pubs would remain closed,
and those that opened would contribute an extra
fee to the Treasury, while those caught selling
without the special Sunday licence would be
heavily fined as sly-grog sellers instead of lightly,
as at present, as mere breakers of a regulation.

Dear Bulletin. —A warning to
teachers who meditate taking State ser-
vice in W.A. The specious promises
that lure don’t pan out beyond .£125 p.a.
If you lay to your book and don’t bite
when you’re bitten, a favorable “re-
port ” might get you ,£l4O ; but the only
power outside Tophet to raise you above
that is one Cyril Jackson, and he—well,
we know him over here. Longest hours
in Australia; stiffest programme ; lowest
pay.—Y.
Vic. Full Court has remitted to Melbourne
City Court the question of expelling the “gay
houses” of the metropolis. This means prac-
tically a hint to Mayor M‘Eacharn and Panton,
P.M., to reverse their decision allowing Lonsdale-
street to continue the same old Lonsdale-street of
yore. But luminaries like these, with the back-
bones of stone-walls and with strong convictions
of their own infallibility, will have little difficulty
in reiterating their decision if they feel that way.
Such men, since they can hardly commit Judges
Williams, a’Beckett, and Hodges for contempt,
may, with perfect ease, find for the defendant on
the facts in a way that no Full .Court even will
care to interfere with.
* * * *
The Scotch “ expert ” who recently stated
that, in his opinion, the best way to reclaim a
drunkard is to flog him within an inch of his life,
is probably an old navy-surgeon. An ex-R.N.
sawbones, formerly medical officer to a big Austra-
lian gaol, held the same opinion ; and, though he
wasn’t allowed to flog, he had a way of douching
drunks that left little to be desired in severity,
and prevented many a toper from getting run-in
again. Also, it sent some to the lunatic asylum
and others to the grave.

Tradition dies hard. The other day Mary
Richards was committed for trial by the Ballarat
Bast (Vic.) magistrates on a charge of stealing half-
a-pound of butter, valued at sixpence. Which is
worthy of the cruel days of George the Third.
The recent big blaze at Redfern, Sydney,
once more, demonstrated the iniquity of building
alleged dwellings without a rear exit. Many
people narrovvly escaped cremation through houses
catching fire in front, and rear-escape being cut
off by high broken-bottle-topped walls.

Parson Kirby, of Port Adelaide, who is
always writing about the Social Evil, or some
such attractive theme, has found a new subject.
He wants to seduce the S.A. Acting-Governor in-
to taking a title, and thus pours out the rancid
grease •’—
Nobody is more popular, and worthily so, than His
Excellency the Right Hon. Chief-Justice Way, D.C.L.,
who is also one of Her Majesty’s Judges in the Supreme
Court of Appeal for the whole Empire of Britain. Now
it is an open secret that on more than one occasion the
right hon. gentleman could have received the honor of
knighthood, but declined. That was all very well when
he dwelt alone in splendid isolation and single misery,
but now that a kind and gracious lady has smiled upon
him, and he has attained to connubial bliss, it seems to
me a lack of the fitness of things that the husband should
abound in titles of honor and the wife be called plain
Mrs. Way. Do you not think, sir, that the ladies of Ade-
laide should put on their best bonnets and interview the
Right Hon. His Excellency the Acting-Governor and ask
him to agree to be knighted, and that, having secured
his consent, the ladies might then, in the same splendid
bonnets, visit the Premier, and ask him to bring the
matter before the Queen, and secure an early knighthood,
so that we all might have the pleasure of saying “ Lady
Way.”
Some peopffi affirm that the parson was “put up ”
to write in this odious vein so as to make the
way smooth for Way’s knighthood.

equally guiltless of humor and Latin.

B. MOTJNTCASTLE & SONS, Brisbane, Q’land.
A deputation of parsons called on the
N.S.W. Lands Department the other day, to ask
that the churches should be empowered to take up
lands here and there all over the province for the
preacher’s horse to graze upon while the preacher
himself is administering ghostly comfort to the
adjacent parishioners. In other words, the
churches desire to become non-residential selectors
on a very large scale. The tale the deputation
had to tell was a trifle complicated, but the bones
of the narrative, when they had done bleaching in
the sun of inspection, were these : The Cornstalk
reverend, in by far the biggest part of the coun-
try, has to go round his parish on horseback in
order to admonish the pastoralist and the cockatoo.
Many of his flock are so stony broke that they can’t
give his horse a feed when he arrives (how they
feed the preacher himself doesn’t appear), and
apparently they are even so hard-up that they
can’t let his horse stand in the paddock. Be-
sides, in many cases, there is very little
except rock for the horse to eat if it is
allowed to stand in the paddock. Therefore,
the churches want to take up paddocks for the
preacher's horse to stand in while its owner is con-
soling the agricultural cockatoo. The situation is
a trifle hazy, for, in the dry season, when things
are so awful that the cookie can’t stand the
reverend’s horse an oat or two, there will be no
feed in the church paddock, and probably no water
either, and the good man’s saddened beast, will
simply have to stand there and wither in the
blaze of the sun. To all intents and purposes, it
might just as well stand in the selector’s paddock
or evaporate on the highway. According to the
deputation, “the minister does not like to cast
himself on the hospitality of those who have so
little to give that they cannot spare food for the
horse,” but, if the country is so dry that the
animal can’t snatch a mouthful off the face of the
earth for itself, it is difficult to see how any grant
of land to the church will improve its condition.
And, if the preacher doesn’t mind casting himself
with a thud on the selector’s hospitality, he couldn’t
make a much louder thud by casting his horse also.
As a rule, it is much harder for the selector to find
room for a reverend inside his little bark shanty
than to let a horse have the use of the globe out-
side. The preacher’s horse looks a rather emaciated
excuse, somehow.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE. .
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

An inquest at Gulgong, N.S.W last week
produced as witnesses two girls, of 11 and 13
years, who stated they had never seen a parson,
and knew nothing about God or heaven or hell.
Happy mortals ! Er —beg pardon, dear berro-
thah ! Yes, it is, as you say, a truly lamentable
state of ignorance not to know all about the devil
and hell; and—we will now take up a collection m
aid of the Chinese inland missions.

At Sydney, Mrs. Quong Tart, of a daugh-
ter. Scotch papers, please copy.
Among what the Reid Cabinet call minoi
reforms in court procedure,” to be submitted to
the present Parliament when business permits, is
a short measure dealing with juries in felony
cases. A N.S.W. Judge cannot discharge a jury
in a felony trial, however clear it may be that
they will not agree, until they have sat 12 hours,
and'he cannot accept a verdict or discharge the
panel on Sunday. It thus happens that if jurors
are locked up on a Saturday they may have to
wait till Monday for release. In England the
Judge can call the jury into court after a reason-
able tune, and if, on questioning the foreman and
other members, he is satisfied that they are not
likely to agree, he can discharge them and turn
tjie prisoner over for a fresh trial. The Cabinet
favors giving this discretion to N.S.W. Judges.
It would, at any rate, stop Saturday-night scan-
dals.

Melb., again bobs up with a demand for Sunday

He’d lay the dust with lots of water,
And plant two rows of shady trees,
That Grundy and his wife and daughter
May go to heaven at their ease.
Aid. Carter aforesaid thinks the streets should be

kirk. Fancy going to Sheol on a water-cart! And
again :
This proposition very well
The divers tastes should suit;
We’d then see bound the rough and swell,
For heaven one and one for hell,
And by the self-same route.
“Constance Clyde”: Your
"‘Red Page” dictum which
awards the palm of genius to
the small woman is worthy of
comment from the fact that
novelists invariably do the re-
verse. The story-writer makes
his large woman original and
strong-minded, his little hero-
ine timid and clinging. In
real life the contrary is the
case. Four-foot-ten has all the
independence, while the woman
who really wants to be a cling-
ing vine is the sort of woman
that if she did cling would
send the male support to earth
with a dull thud. As for
genius in the way of story-
writing, Charlotte Bronte, with
some score others, was small;
George Eliot was large. Per-
haps the moral is that the
little woman is, in general,
cleverer, more vivacious, has
more of the essential genius
temperament, but that when a
large woman has brains, she
has them, and no mistake.

“Hermes”: Was recently on a ’bus
crowded with women, when a cab ranged up and
the jehu poured out a torrent of filthy observa-
tions about a wild burst of immorality which
Bussy and a lot of Chows and certain other devils
like unto himself were alleged to have had a few

to complain to the Transit authorities would in-
volve being “dealt with by the push ” later on.
In S.A., the Governor of the province
makes an annual inspection of the Stockade —the
one general prison for long-timers—and hears com-
plaints and applications. It was about the last
official act which Gov. Buxton performed before
quitting on his English jaunt.
* * * *
Two mad-drunks recently took charge of
the town of Forbes (N.S.W.) for over two hours,
galloping too and fro, swinging stirrup irons, and
filling the air with curse till the scared population
shut itself in and through the key-hole of its barri-
caded door watched the wild men of the west lay
out with a stirrup-iron the only living thing that
dared to remain in the street—a greengrocer’s horse,
to wit. In the bad old days of the Men-who-
Went-Before, it would have taken a lot of search-
ing to find a community that would allow two
drunken ruffians to terrorise it for two whole hours.
* • * *
Apropos “Scotty’s” reference to the in-
dignation of the Connemara man, when told that
the ancient Irish wore saffron-colored shirts. The
50th Regt. was stationed in Sydney, when Dick
Lee, boss joker on a weekly satirical paper, stated,
in answer to a correspondent, that it was known
in the service as the “Dirty Half Hundred.”
Also, that this nick-name had been applied to the
regt. because the soldiers only washed twice a year,
and the officers once a quarter—-with the added in-
formation that only the colonel and adjutant wore
socks when on duty. When the soldiers read
this, they sent a fatigue party to remonstrate ;
"and it did its work well. The printer was
chucked down stairs, and his compositors kicked
after him ; after which the office was wrecked.
Then the remonstrants got hold of Lee, and
ducked him in the horse-trough opposite the old
Exchange. The following week an apology was
published, to the effect that the writer had con-
fused the 50th with another dirty regiment. And
after that the paper died.

JACK’S TROUBLES.
“ What’s the matter, Jack ?"
"Boo-hoo, mother. I’ve bin fiddling — boo-hoo—ever so long with
this horrid old blouse, and — boo-hoo —I can’t get it on.
Boo-oo! ”
"Never mind; you’ll never be a man if you cry like that.”
“ Hoo-boo! I don’t think I ever shall, mother. I’m no use — boo!
I shan’t ever be able to be a cabman —I shan’t be able to
be anything. Oh — booo —I shall have to be a plain man
and do nothing, like father.”
plain English were given by representatives of a
variety of nationalities. Dutch artist Van der
Velden orated like a politician barracking for a
portfolio; conductor Hazon explained that he no
speaka ver mooch; and Sun Johnson, of the
Chinese Herald, gave out that Bohemia was a
land situate somewhere in the Orient. The jour-
nalists present were so overwhelmed by frequent
references to “ literary men ” that they hung their
heads in blushful modesty—or else reserved them-
selves for the next Bohemian shivoo, which will
happen in a few weeks time.

* * * *
The chairwoman at Miss Tilley’s woman-
hood-suffrage meeting in Sydney t’other night gave
it as her opinion that, so far from being recruited
from the ranks of unhappy wives and disappointed
spinsters, the league was generally composed of
women nappy in their marriages. It is a solemn
fact. The woman who has to help hubby’s fal-
tering footsteps from the adjacent gin-house, and
take off his boots, and chase away snakes, is gener-
ally too much occupied to worry about the wrongs
of her sex in general. It is the good, kind, easy-
going husband who gives a hand with the dishes
at night, and doesn’t object to wheeling Mamie in
the go-cart, that is responsible for the mischief.

At Sydney Hospital Dispensary last week
a mother, with an unruly brood, caused much
amusement by filling the dark, cavernous passages
withfrantic yells to Jersey (his hair was fiery red)
to keep quiet, and threats to whack Carington’s
ultimatum, and loud requests to Kintore to pass
Hampden’s feed-bottle out of the basket.
* * * *
Sydney dailies, referring to a recent
George-street burglary, observe that “ the robbery
was characterised by a certain amount of daring,
inasmuch as the shop is kept brilliantly lighted
throughout the night.” This is a non sequitur.
The thief dreads the light, but the burglar of
more than average observation soon discovers
that the police delegate their duty to gas or
electricity, and that places lit up at night are
practically undefended. Wherefore, worthy
tradesmen, veil your premises in outer darkness,
provide a promising crop of spring-guns, and
chance shooting the friendly policeman who
“ didn’t know it was loaded,” as he tries the door-
fastenings in his early-morning tramp.
* * * *
A touching item for the education of
parents. A young man in our street was hope-
lessly mashed on the girl next door, and, as usually
happens, the maiden was grateful, and did her
best to aggravate the ailment; but her pa, a
prosaic Scotch person with a passion for “siller,”
hoped for a more profitable son-in-law. So the
fond pair spooned surreptitiously in the back
garden over the dividing wall and through cracks
in the fence. One night the old man charged
down on the lovers when the tabooed suitor had
his right arm wound round the girl’s neck to
shield her from the blighting blast, and the two
males fought a busy little mill all over the cabbage-
patch, whilst the girl stood by bewailing her sad
lot. Result: the maiden contracted a bad batch
of influenza germs, and a pulmonary complaint
followed, and came within an inch of killing her.
The doctor told the old man that the hasty re-
moval of the suitor’s arm from the neck of the
damsel had induced the complaint, and he charged
the disgusted old Scotchman £53. May this
warning not be thrown away upon Australian
parents !

Melbourne breweries have struck a new
way of ad vertising their connection with “tied”
public-houses. They provide nice new mats for
the front-door—or wherever most noticed—with
the name of the brewery worked thereon. These
mats, by the way, are made by prison labor, at
Pentridge.
“ Bures ” : Bulletin
says that a mixture of Jew-
ish and Irish blood is a good
cross. A while back, whilst
living in an inland town-
ship, I became friendly with
the local tobacconist —a Jew.
Yarning with him one day,
he suddenly changed the
conversation by remarking,
“ I’ve been going to ask you
several times how are you
bred. Your name’s un-
doubtedly Sheeny.” Told
him my father was Jewish
and mother Scotch. With
hands stuffed in trouser-
pockets, and pipe tilted up
towards his hat-brim, he
walked about four steps
away, then stopped, and
with lowered head surveyed
me critically. Finally, ap-
parently as the result of much
cogitation, he said, “My
word ! but that’s a terrible
thieving breed, aint it? ”

A man of humorously
cynical turn t’other day called
at Melb. Post-office to ask if
any letter rested there for
him. The sweet young crea-

was reading. She slowly
rose, running a finger along
a line in the page, evidently
absorbed in the interest of
the book. “My dear young
lady,” said the applicant,
“I am truly sorry to drag
you from that interesting
story. Mine is such a trivial
business. But I can boil-
down the yarn for you if
you like. The wicked noble-
man, who seeks the ruin of
the beauteous and virtuous
maiden, is shot by the game-
keeper, whose wife, you know
—but I pass that over. The
heroine weds the sailor boy,
who turns out to be the
wicked nobleman’s son, and
inherits his title and estates.
As for the comic tinker and
the money - lender my
name is Brown, Thomas
Brown. No letters ! Thanks.
Good morning.”

The Approaching Hot Weather.
Now that the hot weather is rapidly approach-
ing in this colony we want to lay in a big stock of
Bile Beans, as these little beans have a peculiar
effect on people who are troubled with the heat.
They appear to brace one to face almost any
degree of heat. This is caused by their action on
the liver and bowels, for if these organs are
inactive a great quantity of waste matter
remains in the blood, and all sorts of serious
complications follow, such as bad heart’s ac-
tion. But if Bile Beans are used during the
trying hot weather there will be very few deaths
from heat apoplexy. For all such ailments as
Indigestion, Headache, Piles, Debility, Languid-
ness, Lack of Energy, and Physical Tone, that
tired, weary, worn-out feeling, and want of desire
to move, Bile Beans will prove an undoubted
specific, for they have been used for years by the
first physicians in America with most excellent
results, and to-day their sale is probably greater
than any other pharmaceutical preparation in
America. As the price is only 9Jd. per box, and
they are such an undoubted remedy for hot-
weather ailments, they will undoubtedly score as
great a success in Australia as elsewhere.*
