A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, October 18, 1898.
My Dear Myef.—
Don’t you think it a little bit artless of the
jfrational Council of Women to describe its en-
deavours to further the teaching of cookery in
imblic schools as a “ subject that more particularly
nterests women ’’ ? If the National Councillors were
all “unappropriated blessings,” who had never
ministered to the wants of a hungry hubby, there
might be an excuse for this naive remark, but most
of them are matrons who know the suffering an
ill-coolted dinner can cause a man, to say nothing
of his wife.
Cookery in public schools wants a big, strong,
helping hand. Our servant girls all come from
public schools, in spite of their veiled hints about
“ma being a lady” and “ladies’ schools” and
“better days,” and to give them a sound, sensible
education in roasting and boiling would be to turn
the servant question topsy-turvy, for the servant
trouble nearly always starts with the cooking. No
self-respecting “general ” ever swallows a slur on
her method of boiling potatoes, or rolling “piestry.”
“Mrs. Othermissus never come outinthekitching
interferin’, and her pertaties always pleased every-
body else, ” and she gives notice and goes, and the
next girl boils potatoes, but can’t boil a leg of
mutton, and so the servant question is swelled by
one more weary querist.
The deputation waited on Mr. Hogue, Minister
for Public Instruction, lately, with a view to
snaring his support. Quite a large gathering of
chiffons went into the sacred room, where the
minister received them with a rose in his button-
hole, and a scare in his eye that betrayed his new-
ness to deputations of this gender. Mrs. Renwick
played spokeswoman and put the matter briefly.
The National Council wanted all the public school
children to learn cooking as they learned their
Euclid and Algebra. Then sweet Rose Scott rose
up like a Machiavelli and drew pictures of good
dinners until the Minister’s mouth must nave
watered. And she did so hope that the cooking
would be sensible. Jam sandwiches and sponge
fingers were all very well for women, but for
men—and left the Minister to fill up the pause.
I thought the cause must be won then, but perhaps
a culinary speech is a little tantalising to a mere
man. Anyway, Mr. Hogue made a guarded reply,
and said he would do what he could, and compli-
mented the Councillors on their practicality and dis-
trust of jam sandwiches, arid left them wondering
how much forrarder they were. Which is quite
the correct impression for a Minister to leave on a
deputation, don’t you know.
How the hash-house would prosper under the
proposed regime ! For if resurrection pie can ac-
quire different grades of horror in the hands of
different slaveys, why shouldn’t it be made a
well a thing of moderate beauty? There’s no doing
away with it, you know. Not in the present lively
state of things, at any rate. A Sydney woman
advertised for board and lodging the other day and
received no end of offers from respectable neigh-
borhoods like Darlinghurst at eight shillings per
week. Their pie couldn’t offer much hope of
resurrection, but even then the mystery of such
things baffles one.
Another sign of the times lies in an ad. from S.
M. Herald : “ Wanted small office boy, must use
typewriter and shorthand.” From man to boy.
From boy to small boy. The only other line of
retrenchment Gouli can foresee will be ‘ ‘ small girl. ”
I have just been looking at a portrait of De
Rougemont, and surely that’s the phiz of old hum-
bug. Eyes that have driven deep, extraordinary
lines into the high forehead in their long efforts to
keep up a sincere expression, while the yams
flowed forth, —all the garrulous old story-tellers
I know have just such eyes. _ Louis Becke
is stirred to a scornful article in the Daily
Chronicle, in which he wants to know, on behalf
of Australia, who are the two English girls De
Rougemont rescued from our aboriginals, and
why Australia has never heard of them before.
Which reminds me that an English novelist
once tried her hand at a somewhat similar yarn,
and laid on her colors with liberality that puts De
Rougemont in the dark altogether. I speak of
Helen Mathers, authoress of “Cornin’ thro’ the
Rye,’’-who let her fancy air itself in a 3vol. novel
called “Sam’s Sweetheart.” Eheu! They sail
from Bathurst in that book, I remember, and used
to drive from Bathurst to Melbourne for a pound
of sweets. And there was a beautiful black-gin-
heroine who called herself Sweetheart, and turned
out to be an English girl, daughter of a duke, who
had been dyed by the wicked Australian blackies
with nut-juice. They took it all for gospel in
England, but the Sydney School of Arts sub-
scribers used to vent their outraged feelings in
agonised pencil-notes on the last page. “ This
book is dammed rot ” was about the mildest com-
ment.
The latest dusky arrival in Sydney is a small,
bright, little 10-year-old Fijian princess, who has
come to live with Prof, and Mrs. David,, of
Funafutan fame, until she is old enough and wise
enough to go to Sydney University to study
medicine.
Hostesses at garden parties wishing to be con-
sidered right up to date might originate amongst
their guests a competition search for the planet
Yenus. Our beautiful neighbor (approaching
conjunction) is now so brilliant that it can easily
be found at 3 p.m., due north, in the full sunlight.
Very few people can say they have seen a star in
broad daylight.
The whole world has as yet not found its way
about the new Markets. I made a voyage of dis-
covery up there, the other day, and noticed that
the chief visitors were dogs and friends of the
shopkeepers, and women with large assortments,
of children. The tiled floor shrieked to be swept,
and the general air was quite out of keeping with
the domes and carvings and that golden key and
gaudy opening ceremony. Greengrocers and
fruiterers flourish freely, but everyone’s wares
suffer from sunburn. However, now that awn-
ings are promised, and water is being laid on, the
market people say that biz. begins to look up. I
was much taken with Singer’s room, decorated by
Phil Goatcher with a wheel-pattern of dull red
and gold pickings on a dull green wall. A com-
plicated ceiling held up every soft blue and yellow
and green and chased them all with gold, and
people waiting for their Singer can feast their eyes
on all sorts of nice harmonies.
Phil Newbury’s first Sydney Bobular (according
to a Frenchy trying to put on a little Australian
slanguage) drew a nice, comfortable house to the
Town Hall on Saturday night. And the greedy
glutton who never gets enough bang-dash, and
curly-wurly trills, and straight-out shrieks for his.
money came away with a satisfied smile and a full
feeling in that part of his anatomy marked
“ Music, One Shilling.” Phil, in good voice, can
give points to all our other tenor visitors, and if
he hadn’t played down as much as he did on Satur-
day the back-seats would have been much offended.
For the back-seats know what they want, and'
they’ll have it, or they’ll never come again. So-
they get it, all the head notes, and the sostenutes,
and the sotto voces, and the made-up turns that
finish away in some undiscovered part of the vocal:
region.
On the whole, the concert was remarkably good.
After Newbury, the chief note of musical culture
was lent by Madame Spada, who carolled “ Bel
Raggio, ” and wouldn’t take an encore. A happy
little sketch by Miss Rose Musgrove and Mr.
M‘Nicol Turner quite revolutionised the ordinary
concert programme. Minuets, skirt and step-
dancing, and a kissing duet were thrown into this;
travesty of a Christmas pantomime, Miss Mus-
grove acting with lots of go and grace.
Everybody will be sorry to hear that a certain
handsome, full-chested baritone is having a bad
time with paralysis of the throat. Over-work of a
kind he was not used to is the cau&e, and there is.
a sad question whether the boards will ever ring
again with that fine voice.
From Paling and Co. comes the final burst of
this season’s music—and a good burst it is. Two-
songs of Ugo Biondi’s, “Dear Little Saint” and
“ The Continental Waiter,’’show what a long way
beyond them Ugo goes. On one’s own piano these
songs haven’t a kick in them. The “Victor
Waltz,” by Ludwig Schliebner, is like so many,
many waltzes, except in its dreamy second move-
ment ; and the “ Mia Bella Gavotte,” by Theodore
Tourrier, is so like so many more Gavottes. A
gentle little tinkle that wouldn’t scare a fly is the
well-named “Tyro ” Gavotte, by F. May Lewing-
ton. Maud Fitz-Stubbs’ “Orlando Waltz” an-
nounces on its cover the ninth edition of her
Premier, which proves that somebody buys local
music. W. H. Glen sends Mdme. Kellerman’s
new song, “Dear Old Home,” introduced here by
Mr. Burns Walker. The music does its best, but
never gets over the words, which ring with such
moral lessons as “True love, it is better than
gold,” and “Be manly, be brave, and be true.” A
different kind of moral lesson—a much more per-
suasive and friendly one—is preached by a giddy
waltz with a picture of a bottle on its cover. I
was just beginning to play this over when I
realised that it was an ad., and hadn’t the heart to
go on with the champagne, in music.
I have received from Messrs. David Jones and
Co. an artistic little booklet, “Shopping by Post.”
Country cousins can keep themselves up to date
with the aid of this guide to fashion, and no back-
block belle need have any doubt of what’s worn,
with all these pictures of hats and frocks and capes
and stockings to tell the story so carefully.
Verily the way of the reformer is hard.
Some soft-hearted University woman recently
complained that the inmates of Newing-
ton Asylum didn’t get enough beauty sleep
o’ mornings. So they agitated, and persuaded
the authorities to give the old ladies an extra
half-hour in bed. Result, much chagrin and
hauteur among the old ladies, who now decline to
wait for the bell, and insist on their right to rise
at the old hour, quite satisfied with having dis-
turbed the asylum routine.
Talking after the recent big fire at Redfem, a
fireman said that he rushed into a room where a
woman was in her bed, in order to save her.
Turning on him, she indignantly asked how he
dared to come in. He told her that, if she wasn’t
out quick, he’d have to drag her out, so he waited
outside the room door while she climbed into a
wrapper. They were scarcely in the street when
the flames burst into the room. Even then, the
lady wasn’t quite sure whether she oughtn’t to
have reported the fireman.
A benevolent-looking old librarian, at the recent
Sydney congress of bookworms, mentioned that he
had known a young man who changed his book
once a day for three years—all fiction, too. But
what the librarian couldn’t quite understand was
the devotion of the young man to the golden-haired
Gwendolines of fiction, when he had but just mar-
ried a young and pretty wife of his own. And
the old gentleman sighed a far-away reminiscent
sort of sigh, and left the conference to puzzle
it out.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Acting-Gov. Madden ‘and suite’ have left
Govt. House and taken rooms at the Grand Hotel.
The official residence had to be given up that it
might be got ready for the returning Brazen
Image, and as the Madden suburban residence is
let for the season, there was nothing for it but to
eat the temporarily vice-regal morning egg and
munch the official toast at the hotel in Spring-st.
“ By the way, a flag has blown from the tower
of the Grand Hotel since the Maddens have been
in residence there, but the terms for other boarders
have not at present been increased. Doubtless
the Madden arms will be hereafter placed over the
hall door —moustachios rampant, waxed : a bald
head proper, and the motto ‘ I like 'em plump.’
‘ ‘ Society rolled up to that Children’s Hospital
matinee at Princess Theatre (Oct. 13), while over
and above legitimate Pure Merinos came hordes of
would-be ‘ naicest, ’ anxious to please ‘ dear Lady
Madden ’ by support of the cause so precious to her
‘kind, motherly heart.’ Said heart, by the way,
was solaced at the matinee by the society of all the
owner’s daughters, down to Baby Beatrice with an
•ostrich-tip plantation on her white satin straw hat.
“ Small girls no longer minimise their apparent
ages by baby-bonnets, patty-pan hats, pinafore
frocks and touzled thatch. At present their long-
ish skirts are gathered neatly on to close-fitting
waist bodices, and, what with smooth hair and
•sober-minded millinery, the fashionable child of to-
day suggests her mother’s maiden aunt cut short,
rather than a belated baby. Probably the thick
ankles and would-be saucer-eyes of grown-up
Greenaway girls sounded the knell of the pictur-
esque juvenile. At all events, Missie of the
moment is dressed to look precise and ‘ lady-like.’
"‘Miss Leslie Madden’s flowery muslin, worn at
the matinee, was built with small chemisette of
pink satin, and dog-rose sprays lay flat on her
white chi)) hat. Sister Ruby sported pink roses
on the tilted side of her Tudor cap ; and, unwilling
to hide her adorable neck ‘ under a bushel, ’ substi-
tuted turned-down lace tucker and pearl necklet
for a dress collar.
“At the matinee, Mrs. Maesmore Morris trotted
weary miles carrying tea-cups. Stage beauty in
Melb. apparently can’t command a sovereign a
smile, or twice as much for a word. The fragile
belle in question gave good value in tea, cake, and
attendance for every shilling she took, and was
taken as calmly by her customers as the ordinary
bazaar-syrefi. She seemed extra-ethereal that hot
afternoon in black and white muslin and floating
pink sash-ends. Even the stole capes falling over
her sleeves failed to make her look a comfortable
armful, but when she tripped on a step in the
dusky d.c., the ensuing thud was surprisingly
sufficient and satisfying. Florence Terriss, too,
in one of those Tussore washing silks and snowy
cuffs, did good tea-tray service, and, seen at close
quarters, proved to be far more shapely and restful
than she appears on the boards, where she’s so
dreadfully animated as to convey the impression
of a bundle of angles, joints, muscles and vitupera-
tion.
“A squad of Cliveden Clarkes patronised the
matinee. Miss C., with Honiton lace applique on
her green frock and rose-trimmed hat, brought
along younger sisters, small nieces and kilted
nephews. Urchins, at present, are debarred till a
comparatively mature age from the dignity of out-
ward and visible pants, with the accompanying
delights of trouser-pockets. When too elderly for
belted tunics, kilts answer well to stave off as long
as possible the unfashionable knicker.
“Mr. and Mrs. Vallange chose honeymoonish
pieces to act both times, so they’re not a bashful
couple, evidently. In ‘ A Breezy Morning ’ the
bride wore white blouse and skirt, while her lord
affected tweeds and ostentatious up-stream boots ;
the black skirt in conjunction with shirt bodices
has had its day. But to return to the honey-
mqoners. They quarrelled, and the bride invited
a widow with cats and parrots to come and stay
with her, engaging the only spare room on her be-
half. Hubby meantime wires on the quiet to a
brutal bachelor-chum addicted to bull-dogs to run
down and occupy said guest-chamber. Conjugal
recriminations ! Bride weeps at thought of mad
baying of dogs and death-shrieks of cats. Her
tyrant uses big d’s and swears expected guests will
have to share the contested apartment. All ends
happily.
“In ‘A Cup of Tea’ Miss Vallange’s ivory
satin ball-gown had a deep lace flounce laid on flat,
a black satin waist-band, and scattered black
trimming devices, presumably ‘ lovers’ knots ’
though more suggestive of small pairs of scissors.
Further, her decorations included a big pendant,
a bracelet and unobtrusive hair ornament. No
gloves, of course. Few ‘naicest’ wore them at
Mayor’s ball t’other night. At any time, Mr.
Vallange walks exactly like some bird or other,
with very short stilted steps. As his angry stage
passions rise, his pace grows faster and more
staccato, till you’d swear he was a reincarnated
minah. Neither of the young things attained a
convincing stage laugh. Hubby satisfied himself
with smiles—and such smiles ! Wide white park-
paling affairs, calling up thoughts of dentists and
sich. At the matinee, Julius Knight was on show
in the d.c. after he had done his turn. A fallen
idol these times, perhaps because of that Gil de
Berault costume, which just shows the worst part
of the wearer’s legs—and he always expressed his
stage emotions in those eloquent limbs of his.
“As Gil de Berault, Julius K. affects the funni-
est little mouth. It looks as if he primmed his
lips into a tiny round C in front of the glass, and
then painted the result bright red. Girls rather
like it, I understand, because it enlarges the ap-
parent size of his eyes.
“The splendor of Mayor M‘Eacharn’s hospi-
tality induced an alderman, at his re-election the
other day, to style him M ‘Eacharn the magnificent.
Certainly, the Mayor is ‘ going it. ’ His lavishness
is in such contrast to the way things are done at
Govt. House that even the most loyal make rude
remarks. Anyone can be a ‘ good fellow ’ who
can afford to spend as the Mayor does, but, apart
from that, there is a deal of native goodness and
human kindness about him, and nis wife is a
woman unspoilt by success. Their family are not
all at home to participate in their parent’s enter-
taining. Two daughters are in England wi th
their governess, and are to finish their education
at Girton.
“As an instance of the Mayor’s lavish ness, the
quadrangle of the Town Hall basement, where all
the big ball-suppers are held, has an asphalte floor
covered in time of festivity by carpeting. But
Mayor M‘Eacharn, in his desire to outdo every
predecessor, would have none of asphalte, and had
a wooden flooring laid over the whole area at a
cost (to himself) of nearly £3OO.
“It is said that the ‘Magnificent ’aforesaid will
be a grass-widower before Xmas. The Mayoress
has kept pace with her tireless Donald Malcolm
until she feels ready to drop. Another 12 months’
strain of continual smiling, hand-shaking, and
official graft would be the death of her, therefore,
as she isn’t prepared to perish in duty’s cause, and
throw the City Council into mourning, she intends
to sail away presently to see her boys in England.
“ If the state of the matrimonial market be the
true index to the commercial prosperity of a
country, then it may really be believed that Mel-
bourne is at length emerging from the boom
depression. Miss Madden, Miss Newbigin and
Miss Jenkins are to be the three next fashionable
brides. But stay ! None of the bridegrooms-elect
was found in Victoria. Mr. Osborne is from
N.S.W., Capt. Parker from India, and Mr. Wal-
doclc from Colombo.
“One of Melbourne’s leading lady doctors ap-
pears to be in the way of having ‘physician, heal
thyself,’ written over her portals. She has, per-
haps, the second largest practice among the female
physicians, and, sad to say, is down with tuber-
culosis. She is an energetic, solemn-faced, little
person, who has made rapid headway in her pro-
fession, and has a large run of patients. She was
teaching all the time she worked for her exams.,
and has deserved every bit of her success. And
now the remorseless germ comes along making a
bad choice, for there are plenty of useless women
who could better be spared from the hurly-burly.
. “Ex-Gov. Loch’s only son, who has been dis-
tinguishing himself in the Soudan, is now a lieute-
nant in the Grenadier Guards, 6ft. 4in. tall, broad
in proportion, and further remarkable for his
‘silky’ white hair. The said Douglas, when he
arrived in Melb. with his parents, was an ex-
tremely ‘pretty,’ fragile-looking lad, with his
mother’s delicate complexion and wavy blonde
locks. He was something of a juvenile exquisite,
too, and when he escorted his little sisters and
their governess to prize-distributions, &c., was
adored by young lady pupils for his broad, snowy
collar, trim Eton jacket, flaxen hair brushed
carelessly, off his white forehead, and a languid
way he had of fanning himself with his gloves.
His sisters were more like their father, sturdy
romps with masses of tawny curls and healthy
roundabout faces. Young Loch was in charge of
a tutor in those days, but on certain afternoons he
went by arrangement to play cricket at the C. of
E. Grammar School. Prior to his first visit his
festive companions were instructed by their
masters never to swear in his presenceon pain of you-
know-what. In response, the lads of course went out
of their way to use horrid language in their inter-
course with the vice-regal sprig. But he took it
without winking, and in a week or two the play-
ground vocabulary was back in its ordinary
grooves. When he grew older he joined the
Grammar School cadets, and was present as a pri-
vate in the ranks when his father inspected the
metropolitan battalions, 1887. Later on he joined
the militia at the Cape of Good Hope, and has
been five years in his present regiment.
“Hair has gone down from the crown to the
nape of the neck. It’s still crimped, but quite
smoothly. Some heads look as if they bad been
well oiled before the hot wavers went to work.
For evening wear, blossoms nestle innocently be-
hind the ear, just at the side of the coil. The
Madden girls affect two pink roses, and what with
this touch of color and the abandonment of tbe
Jap top-knot, the debutante daughter promises
fairly to put the beauty of the family on her mettle.
Y ours affectionately,

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The present Premier of an Australian
province was a working shoemaker in Melbourne
over 30 years ago.
Dr Crane, R.C. Bishop of Bendigo (Vic.)
entered his 81st year last week. The Bishop has
for many years been blind.
Mount Barker (S.A.) possesses a lady who
has had 25 children, and who has not necessarily
yet completed her public benefactions.
One Aikenhead, M.P., was lately unseated
in Tasmania on account of “ corrupt practices
his agents having given some electors beer. They
now spell his name Achin head.
Mr. Justice Stephen, senior N.S.W.
Puisne Judge, who had to return from circuit
through ill-health, is within two months of com-
pleting his 70th year, having been born in Hobart
Town in 1828.,
NOT GOOD ENOUGH.
CHOW PATRIOT : “ Here, keep quiet ; that’s a
German.”
CHOW ENTHUSIAST : “ Well, I'm going to have a
cut at him.”
C.P.: “ Don’t be a fool; look at this map—we can’t
spare any more provinces. ”
Vic. Commandant Holled Smith, who will
be 52 this month, served in the Soudan from 1885
to 1887. He is very familiar with the country, and
has naturally followed Kitchener’s advance on
Khartoum with intense interest.
Dr. Crooke, now practising in Darling-
hurst (Sydney) at the venerable age of 86 years,
was the first to introduce into Melbourne the Irish
“ side ”or jaunting car. The doctor’s big fortune
was lost in Melbourne boom-time.
A jack-tar of the Jewish faith is a novelty.
Jack (one of the crew of the Royal Arthur) turned
up at Sydney Synagogue services in uniform, the
other day, and is the only Hebraic man-o’-wars-
man (with bell-bottomed pants and a roll in his
walk) ever heard of locally.
An attorney who had put some mortuary
business in the way of Gus Sleight, Melbourne’s
boss undertaker—now undertaken himself—meet-
ing him soon after, suggested commission. “ Can’t
stand that!’’said Sleight, “but tell you what—
I’ll put you on my free list! ”
Now they are trying to make out that a
curse rests on all associated with the resistance at
law in N.S.W. of the Cyanide co.’s application for
leave to amend its patent. And they point to the
daily-paper’s obituary-list and other circumstances
in confirmation of their theory.
Sydney, Goulburn, Bathurst, and New-
castle have adopted the proposal that C.E. clergy-
men in charge of parishes shall be called rectors.
It is expected that the rest of N.S.W. will follow
suit, and the wives of several parsons will feel
even more respectable than heretofore.
On the (Macleay) coastal steamer. States-
man, sitting on one of the after seats in his normal
condition of doze, periodically emitting spasmodic
grunts. Agricultural passenger, after watching
him long and earnestly, with a sigh, as for some-
thing lost: “Blow me! what great bacon he
would make ! ”
Judge Boucaut was recently described by
an Adelaide lawyer as “S.A.’s wholesale judge.”
He wiped off the October criminal calendar—over
40 cases and the biggest on record—in seven days.
Whereas Way, C. J., makes big and prosy speeches,
Boucaut rarely talks to his juries for more than
20 minutes in a case.
Melb. demise, Kitchen, big soap and
candle-maker, a worthy man and open-handed
Evangelical. He was the very bore of the China
mission, vvith its enthused young women ; some
hold the idea that most of the Providentially-
sent money came out of K’s soap and candles.
If so, there’ll be a squeal, maybe a squash.
The kaleidoscopic character of N.S. Wales
Supreme Court may be gauged from the fact that
four of the eight Q.C’s have been acting-judges,
and of the seven Judges on the Bench three held
acting appointments before gaining the per-
manency. Likewise of the seven D.C. Judges,
four have held acting appointments on the higher
bench.
Only one member of the present N.S.W.
Supreme Court Bench —Justice G. B. Simpson—
has held the office of Attorney-general. Years ago
the Attorney-General was supposed to have a lien
on any Supreme Court Judgeship which fell vacant
during his term of office. The late Parkes in the
Butler, Foster, and other incidents, practically
killed the assumption.
Don “Billy” Lane’s recent mission to
England from Rue Australia was a bad failure.
Great things were expected from William’s jour-
nalistic alchemy. The English press was to be
electrified ; a stream of immigrants was to arrive;
the Cosme treasury was to be filled to overflowing.
Then Lane came back .£2OO in debt; and the
colony sadly shouldered the swag.
The Rev. P. Dunne, now of Albury, added
over 10,000 to the population of Queensland. He
was stationed there about 30 years ago, and spent
all the money he could raise in paying the pas-
sages out of South of Ireland emigrants. In
addition to planking down cash, he wrote thou-
sands of letters urging people to avail themselves
of the free immigration terms that then obtained.
S.A. democrats foretell the early end of
the Kingston Ministry, for the reason that Chief
Sec. O’Loughlin is dallying with an eye-glass.
As politics are in S.A. just now, the least little
thing tells, and the sudden appearance of that
eye-glass (S.A. is just on the eve of a general elec-
tion) may convince the workers that the Kingston
crowd has at last become too arrogant and respect-
able.
Our titled insignificances are forgotten
even in the country that bebaubles them. Here
is London Times, the most accurate of papers,
recording that Mr. Duncan Gillies has been
appointed leader of the Opposition in Victoria in
succession to “Mr. MTntyre.” Not even an
initial, to say nothing of the title. The Times
cable editor had clearly never heard of M'lntyre :
small blame to him.
Young Bernard Ingleby, whose marvellous
adventures and feats of derring-do at Mt. Kos-
ciusko while acting as a meteorological observer
have been so well advertised, has resigned. For
which relief, much thanks. It has not been easy
to pick up Sydney E. News during the past four
or five months without colliding with a neatly-
worded paragraph regarding some thrilling adven-
ture of Mr. Ingleby.
Mrs. West, relict of Major West, died last
week at Bathurst, aged 86 years. She had lived
in the district close on 80 years, having gone over
the mountains with her parents when the century
was in its teens. Her husband was not an army
man, “ Major ” being his Christian name. Very
few of the old Western pioneers now remain,
about the last of the wealthy ones being Charles
M‘Phillamy, of Orton Park.
Paris Nesbit has just been appearing in
his first big law case since his recovery. It is a
family will-suit, in which Mr. Nesbit, for plaintiff,
argues that testator was of unsound mind when
he did things. Only .£5OOO is involved ; yet
along with Mr. Nesbit are John Downer (who
leads) and S. J. Mitchell; on the other side, J. H.
Symon and J. H. Gordon. And the quarrel is
exclusively between brothers and sisters.
HIS TROUBLES.
WILLIE: “Pa, what’s the meaning of a man suffering
from a domestic affliction?”
PA: “It means, my son, keeping two servants to do the
work of one. ” (Goes on drawing cheque for
wages.)
A somewhat unsuccessful “journeyman”
lawyer and politician has just gone over in Mr.
Stuart Cansdell, whose family claimed for him
the position of “ father ”of the N.S. W. bar. Mr.
Cansdell was called at the Inner Temple, Nov.,
1858. In ’6l he arrived in Tasmania, where he
acted as Crown Prosecutor and Solicitor-General.
He was twice member for Hobart in the Leg.
Assembly. He went to Queensland in 1874, and
was Crown Prosecutor at Bowen. In N.S.W.,
for 2J years ('B2 to ’B5) he was an acting D.C.
Judge, but the Govt, would not make the appoint-
ment permanent. One of his great regrets was
that when offered the Chief Justiceship of Wes-
tralia in the sixties he did not accept the offer.
“Melb.”: It is remarked even in religious
papers that not one of the religious bodies was
represented in the miserable tailing at C. H.
James’s obsequies. What is a religious body:
James gave them hard cash, not promissory notes,
like G. W. Taylor. Maybe the bodies were afraid
of being asked for a bit back. One of James’s
chums gave. .£lOOO to thekurruk, and died, leaving
his daughters in dire financial distress. No money
returned —as the profane play-bills say.
Rev. John Watsford, now visiting Syd-
ney, was born in Parramatta in 1820, and is a
living history of the early people and events of
that classic borough. But his description of the
Walkers, the Byrneses, the Hunts, and the other
old identities would lead to the opinion that in
the last 50 or 60 years the world of Parramatta
has gone greatly to the bad. There are no such
men and women now. Mr. Watsford was an
evangelist among the Fijian cannibals 54 years
ago.
A.n Australian politician who has just lost
his seat after having been 12 years in Parliament,
spreads himself out thus in beginning the world
afresh : “ Advice 2/6 by letter or personal—Ac-
countant and Auditor —Parliamentary, Financial
and General Agent—Advice given and questions
answered on matters of business —Law —Politics
or General Information. Private or business
advances arranged promptly and privately.
Dealer in Pianos and Musical Instruments —
Cigars—Wines and Spirits, &c. Bureau of
General Information.”
Old W. J. M. Larnach, who shot himself
in the Wellington House of Representatives last
week, was another of the diminishing band of
politicians who fought with or against Hori Grey
in the old days. As Treasurer under Grey, Lar-
nach went to England to float a loan or something.
Afterwards he joined the second inglorious Stout-
Vogel combination, and helped to burden M.L.
with debt and the Midland land-grant railway
shindykit. Larnach was the only man in Aus-
tralasia who called his residence a “ castle ” the
rival Cargill’s Castle, also in Scottish Dunedin,
having been demolished by fire a few years back.
Dr. Nicholson, baronet, who will attain
his 90th year next month, sent a parcel of valuable
books, last mail, as a gift to Sydney University
Library. The venerable doctor has been making
presents to that University for half a century.
While he has been an absentee, his first thought
on encountering a curio has been to attach it and
transmit it to Sydney. He was University Chan-
cellor in 1854. He was also a politician in his
early Australian life. He was a member of
N.S.W. Leg. Council, and Speaker of Queens-
land’s first Parliament. But the passion of poli-
tics long ago burnt out. On the other hand,
his love for literature, or rather for the curios of
literature, and especially for the advancement of
high-class education, has renewed its youth again
and again.
John Hunter Baillie, whose will
after 44 years comes before N.S.W.
Equity Court for interpretation as to
certain educational bequests, carried
about early Sydney a full charge of
theological odium. His wife survived
him till last year, and as she had a life
interest in the property the question of
turning the bequests to educational pur-
poses did not arise earlier. Now St.
Andrew’s College and Scots College both
claim the money, and the trustees seek
the Court’s advice. But the point that
illustrates the old identity’s dogmatic
disposition is that the will provides that
the professorships are not to be held by
any priest or member of any religious
order of the Church of Rome. Another
part of the will, providing for a ragged
school in or near Sydney, also lays
down that no priest or member of any
religious order of the Church of Rome
shall be eligible to any office in connec-
tion therewith.
The night before the suicide of
Detectiye Lomaine in Melb., a couple of
years back, he was in the muster-room
with several other officers when the
talk turned on the suicide of a constable
who had just been inquested. The old
contention that a man was a coward to
suicide, inasmuch as he feared to face
the music of life, was advanced by one
of the men, and Lomaine immediately
“took him up,” roundly averring that
the suicide was an individual of far
more than average pluck and intelli-
gence. He carried the day by his argu-
ments, and 24 hours later was found
standing dead with a bullet through his
skull against the wall of Russell-street
barracks
A very distinguished N S. Wales
Q.C. not long ago successfully appeared
in Court to demur to a certain declara-
tion. “ But you drew that declaration
yourself ! ” ejaculated an astonished lawyer after-
wards. “ I did,” replied the perky Q.C., “ and
yet it is bad.”
In April last, the train by which Chief-
Justice Darley travelled to the Hay Assizes met
with an obstruction near Junee. In June, a
similar incident in the same quarter took place,
and again the Chief-Justice was a passenger. In-
vestigations on both occasions showed that an
attempt had been made to wreck the train. A week
or so ago, a third attempt was made near Junee,
and a man has been placed under arrest. On this
occasion, the C. J. was expected to be in the train,
but was delayed.
If Premier Braddon takes the Treasury he
will then, it is expected, come under pay from the
Tasmanian exchequer. As Premier he has not
drawn any salary these five years. He is in re-
ceipt of an Imperial pension.
“ Curra Ourra” : Valentine Plattfuss,
who lately died at Echuca from snake-bite, was
always regarded as a dexterous snake-killer. He
often fearlessly seized a venomous snake by the
tail and broke its back by flicking it in the air.
The other Sunday he tried this trick once too
often, was bitten on the arm, and died ere medical
assistance could be obtained.
A characteristic of the Turner Ministry is
its backwardness in turning up to Executive
Councils. Quite recently only one Minister put
in an appearance, and he had the task, no easy
one under the circumstances, of entertaining the
Governor for half-an-hour, after which they gave
up hope. In the time of Gov. Hopetoun it was
remarked that Councils were always fully at-
tended. But then—well, the rest is a State
secret!
“ SAME OLD GEORGE.”
GEORGE (ex-shearer , eloquently) : “ Yes, boys, the

’ and —not one o’ yer little tin trumpets, but a

The Romance-Rougemont started in the
Wide World Magazine with an assurance that
the editor had “absolutely satisfied himself of the
truth of the story,” and now Newnes climbs down
to a contention that, it Rougemont-Grein was lost
for three years in the bush,- the (jroundwork of his
.story must be true. Why must it? Besides, it was
the trimmings of the yarn that got the Wide
World by the wool. According to Melb. Argus, a
lady formerly resident in Australia states that she
was astonished to run against, at Tit Bits office,
in the person of the famous De Rougemont, “ that
busybody, Grein, who was So continually boring
us with his spiritualistic experiences, and who
never returned the photo. I gave him to enlarge.
Some of his professed Australian experiences are
identical with the book I gave him to read on
‘Travels in the South Sea Islands,’ especially the
story of Yamba.”
When Rougemont first started off on his
career of “kid,” his geographical (i.e., his lati-
tude and longitude) facts being accurate gave an
air of verisimilitude to his tale of wonders. His
fount of knowledge has, perhaps, been discovered.
A certain surveyor, with an extensive grip of Aus-
tralia, ranging from Adelaide right across the
continent northerly to Port Darwin, &c., used, ’tis
said, to be constantly in Rougemont’s company
when the great romancer lived out Newtown way.
Said surveyor had been employed by S.A. Govt,
to run the overland cable line, had found gold and
rubies a hundred miles or so from the McDonnell
Ranges, also large mica beds. The mica beds,
anyhow, were all right, and so were his surveys.
He is now in England, and if, as is stated, he and
Rougemont continually met in Sydney, it is not
impossible they have since forgathered in London.
At any rate, whilst Rougemont was gaily panning
out from his inexhaustible mines of romance, said
surveyor could easily supply him with necessary
local color, and likewise lats. and longs, beyond
refutation.
Old Tom Monahan, who made and left be-
hind him the huge estate concerning the adminis-
tration of which they have been battling in the Vic.
Courts, was typical of the bard, grasping, Irish
money-grubber. He started in Melbourne as
keeper of the livery-stables attached to the old
Port Phillip Hotel, and saved every cent he could
lay hands on. This he invested in land close to
his stables. He got hold of a big frontage in
Swanston-street, and a good slice in Flinders-lane.
The writer once rented an iron store from him at
a good deal per month, and a creepy feeling
comes over him, to this day, when he recalls
the hideous punctuality with which Monahan
used to ride up to the door on the 3rd of
every month. If the 3rd happened on Sun-
day, he was there on Saturday. Rode a good
horse, and had a neat seat on the saddle.
Wouldn’t take a drink, and wouldn’t offer one.
The piling-up of coin was the one pleasure of his
life. It is a sort of satisfaction for his erstwhile
tenant to reflect that, though old Tom succeeded
in his ambition, he is probably no better off now
than the man who didn’t.

Aboriginalities.
Tassy ” : Near Lake Darldt, not far from
Cue (W.A.), two white women are living, ap-
parently in contentment, with a tribe of abori-
ginals. They are, presumably, “ gropers,” for the
Jarrah seems to have a great fondness for black
beauty, hardly ( week passing without some of
bhem getting “sent up” for camping with the nigs.
First time I have ever known white women volun-
tarily to live with blacks, though it is common
enough with men in all the Australias. Up near
Coolgardie there was, a short time ago, a Chow
travelling round with a tribe.

who has been given a Bible to swear on, attempts
to bite a bit out of the sacred volume. P.M. : —■
“ You savee God ? ” Tommy Tanna :—“ Me no

“ Edsor ” : Glad to see that Vic. Educa-
tion Minister Peacock ukases that sewing be
taught to male State-school pupils. Every boy
who is born to battle should be taught the noble
art of stitching. He’ll find it useful when he gets
out-back, where skirts are scarce. Should also be
taught washing, darning, and elementary cook-
ing. For honors he might qualify to half-sole
boots, make cabbage-tree or kurrajong-bark hats,
and roll up a swag to best advantage. Many
men, after years in the bush, can’t put a patch
on their pants. Pretty tough, too, when a man
THE FOOTBALL FIENDS—THEIR LAST APPEARANCE IN ’98.
can’t make his own ration-bags, and has to take a
tied-up old sock for his pinch o’ tea.
* * * *
“ Piripi ” : Let us have a little talk about
edible grubs. Don’t know what the Australian
variety tastes like, but I can answer for Maori-
land huhu, which, the human repugnance to in-
sect-eating having been overcome, is a perfect
gastronomic delight. A good eatable huhu must
be a finger-length, and should be spitted on a
finely-sharpened ti-tree stick, and gently roasted
before the embers. It tastes like barcelona-nuts
boiled in cream.

“Timi”: Was once seated in Stewart
Bates’ store, at Opotiki, East Coast, M.L., study-
ing the idiosyncrasies of a recently-arrived Mile-
sian cow-puncher, when a Maori beachcomber en-
tered and offered for sale a few ounces of amber-
gris which he had been so fortunate as to find on
the beach. “Poor sample,” said the storeman ;
“how much you want, Zhack?” “Rua pouna
tekau herene,” said the Maori. “ How much did
the naygur ax fur the sthuff ? ” asked the new-
chum. “Two-pounds-ten,” said I. “Fur a
ton ? ” further queried Pat. “ No, for an ounce,”
said I. “An’ pfhwat is the sthuff at all at all ? ”
said Pat, strolling over to the counter to obtain a
closer view of the mysterious substance. I ex-
plained in a manner suited to his comprehension
that ambergris was understood to be a biliary
secretion of the sperm whale. He followed my
explanation with widening eyes, and at last
gasped out : “ Howly fly ? Two-pound-tin an
ounce—an ounce, moind ye fur MANURE !
Lookit here, sir—an’ pfhwat price do they be
chargin’ fur praties in this counthry ? ”

“ Fivecorner ” : “ Warrigal’s ” four pet
birds (8., 10/9/’9B), which are “only capable of
getting outside of an Bin. lizard,” are not jack-
asses, but very ordinary johnny-bottoms, and have
no right to be introduced under the heading of
“ Aboriginalities.” When Eurumtah station (Q.)
was changing hands, some years ago, the seller
laid the buyer a fiver that during the last hour
before sundown he would kill IQO snakes, and did,
in the horse-paddock between the river and the
lagoon (of barramunyip fame), kill 86 black snakes
in the given time, thus losing his bet by only 14.

“ R.W. : Re Poynton, M.P’s, assertion, at
Adelaide Interprovincial Labor Conference, that
he knew 28 men to eat and sleep in a 28ft. x 14ft.
building. If you arrange the berths three deep
round the walls, and put table in centre, you have
it. Three berths deep is not uncommon ; Burra-
wang (Lachlan’s biggest station) has a hut so
arranged. Wasn’t The Bulletin ever in the rush
for a “ bottom bunk ” after a shearers’ roll-call ?
* * * *
A canny N.S.W. station-holder tells how
he was getting a demijohn of brandy with other
loading, and, fearing the teamsters might sweat it
en route, had it invoiced as vinegar. On the track,
the bullockies ran short of vinegar, and, thinking
a little would not be missed, broached the demi-
john, with the result that they emptied it, re
filled it at a wayside store, and delivered it as per
invoice.

“Jack No. 1 ” : How is this for a cham-
pion grafter? Runs a store six days a week;
works a sawmill on Sunday. Has this year 600
acres under wheat, all ploughed and sown by
moonlight and lamplight. Is also fulfilling the
residence-clause on a selection. Sawmill ten miles
from store. Farm three miles from selection.

“P.G.S.”: “Steele
Rudd’s ” assertion (“ 8.,”
10/9/’9B) that kangaroo
doesn't wallop ground
with tail will be contra-
dicted by any bushman
who knows a ’roo from a
hollow log. A’roo, when
travelling, will almost
always vary his jump—
say, first hop 16ft., 2nd
about 9ft., and, on the
first, always grounds tail.
Let “S.R.” track ’roos
over sandy ridges or
spewey ground and he’ll know better; though
about broken tail he is right. ’Roo can’t get a lift
on the spring, and hence can’t travel so fast.

“You can’t eat your cake and have it.”
Writer did once, though. Took up a sack of
potatoes for a winter’s chopping; pared them
pretty thick (bush fashion), and threw the peelings
away. Next autumn went up to burn bush,
noticed a lot of shaughs in ground around whare,
kicked loose earth away, and took up a sack of
potatoes.

“R.E.D.” : One Q. squatter had for ear-
mark, an equilateral triangle ; his next neighbor
a circle, and the man beyond him a square. In-
crease of B’s flock was beyond the average, and
C’s also throve apace, till, on one of C’s “strag-
glers ” B found the arc of a circle chorded by the
side of a square ; and on another, A (called in to
adjudicate) found the apex of a triangle project-
ing from the arc of a circle which subtended the
side of a square. Moral: Always use the ear-clip
yourself !—“ station-hands are so careless.”

“Milky White”: That Wodonga “egg-
offering ” item (l/10/’9B) brings recollections.
Years ago, in an out-of-the-way Vic. township, a,
similar offer was made to the slenderly-stipended
parson. The first eggs deposited in the receiving
vessel came from the X s—a well-to-do farm-
ing family, twelve in number. In packing the
eggs in the basket for the home-taking these
naturally were the uppermost. I was in the habit
of haying tea with the parson every Sunday, and
on this particular evening some of the “ offering ”
boiled formed portion of the repast. The cleric
was the first to chip an egg. He chipped and—
paused. The others of us at the table chipped
and—followed suit. Then we looked at each other
anxiously and the silence couldn’t have been lifted
with a crowbar. But the preacher was equal to
the occasion. “Verily,” he said, “I asked them
for eggs and they have given me meat.” There
was a chick in every one of them. It remained
with the cleric’s wife to put on the finishing touch,
however, with the naive remark, “Mrs. X
told me this morning that she had had such bad
luck with her setting hens this season.” How is
this for a champion mean-man yarn, anyhow ?

“ J.E.”: Self and two others once came on
an old man kangaroo in a paddock with dog-proof
fence with barbed-wire on top. The ’roo hopped
away along the fence till he came to a corner ;
when, expecting him to jump, we were astonished
to see him spring up on the barb wire, stand erect
on it and calmly survey us, till we came close,
when he jumped down and made off. Anybody
else ever heard of a kangaroo acting Blondin ?

“Richard,” on animals’ methods of inter-
communication
White cockatoos, when feeding on a wheat-field, never
fail to post a sentinel on an adjacent tree to give due
screech of approaching danger, and the common rooster
anxiously summons his wives to his side at sight of a
hawk. Following experiment also has convinced me that
fishes are able to warn their fellows of danger. Fishing
once on the Darling, I found a hole abounding with
small bream, which I was able to pull out as fast as I
could bait my hook, but after one had contrived to get off
the hook and wriggle back into the water I never got
another “ bite ” that night. Next day, in same place,
they were biting as freely as ever, but as soon as I had
caught enough for the party’s breakfast I put a just-
bream back into the river, and not another
“ bite ” could I get at that place, though further up and
down I could get plenty. Tried same experiment again
and again, with same result. The liberated fishes had
evidently warned their comrades away from the endan-
gered hole. What have your fish-liars to say to this?

Yon Kotze protests on behalf of the poor
Far Northerner’s stomach :
While on the subject of diseased meat and similar
horrors, I should like to draw attention to that tin of
kippered herrings. It was manufactured sometime in the
middle ages, and after a varied and eventful career and
many ignominious ejections from more particular pre-
mises, turns up invariably in North Queensland. Every
bushman knows it, also its mate, the ancient pot of con-
densed milk, the contents of whioh many summers and
many climes have turned a rich, fruity-brown color. A
distant cousin, the antediluvian luncheon-beef, has lent
a hand to ruin Westralia. It is not always the fault of
the storekeepers who retail this refuse from the table of
civilisation. The tins bear the labels of first-class firms :
but the stuff has been lying about in different wholesale
stores till it has gone bad. Tinned food, especially milk
and fish, will not keep long in a hot climate ; and so,
when a parcel has been left on hand, it gets shunted to
N.Q. “ Good enough for the bush 1 They know no better!”
There is a simple, a very simple, remedy against this evil,
and which I ask you to kindly patent for me. A Bill
should be passed compelling the manufacturers to
plainly dote all their preserves on the label with the year
of bottling or tinning. [When it got old, of course, a fresh
label of recent date would be put on.—Ed. B.] Such a
measure would not only safeguard the consumer and the
retail-merchant against buying a deteriorated article, but
it would also protect the reputation of the producer, who
in many cases loses considerable custom through no
fault of his own. The only people who would object to
thi3 measure would be the wholesale houses. Somehow,
every progressive and desirable law seems to hurt the
middle-man. At times, there lurks a blasphemous sus-
picion through my brain that the middle-man is an
abomination and a curse, and ought to be sat upon.
But, of course, that is only a temporary aberration !

“ Blister " ; Have never seen an authentic
portrait of a bunyip, but the apparition I recently
met one morning on edge of a little reedy bay in
Lake Wellington (Gippsland) was unlike any
beast or bird I ever saw before. I was sneaking
on to duoks, and was about 30yds. in among the
reeds (up to my waist in water) when I nearly
ran against the stranger. Could not see his feet,
but he obliged me with the view of a most con-
vincing set of teeth. He’d had a rough time some-
where, for one eye was gone, his ears (long as a
spaniel’s, and quite hairless) were badly chawed,
and his long, pointed nose was severely torn.
Piebald he was, and a repulsive, mangy-looking
brute. Couldn’t swear to size of his body, but the
long hair floating out from his sides and back
made him look as broad as a house. He was about
4ft. long, and stood comfortably enough in 3ft. of
water. I suppose to him I was a still more fear-
some object, as, before I recovered enough to flee,
he disappeared under water (I found a deep
channel there later on by falling into it) waving a
farewell with his tail, a flat, hairless handle which
I was very pleased to see. When I reached open
water he had come to the surface, and was swim-
ming off and making very good time. Evidently
a resident, too, for my ducks took no notice of
him, though he passed close to them. He was no
liquor-bred beast, for it was last day but one of a
three weeks’ camp, and all our fire-water had been
finished long before. I can’t place him among
crossbred dogs ; too big for any known breed, and
his long coat doesn’t match with his hairless ears
and tail and pig-jawed head.

“ E.S.S.” writes re Australian birds’ nests :
The exposed eggs of Australian birds are never inter-
fered with by crows. The reason is because they are
always under the watchful eye of the parent bird. 1 n the
laying season such birds as the curlew, plover, emu, &c.,
iro in pairs, and may always be found feeding about in
the vicinity of the nest, which is of the most primitive
kind. The bird simply scratches away the few leaves
there may be on a spot the size of a dessert-plate, and
deposits the eggs on the bare ground. But there is this
much in favor of self-protection : The emu’s egg is dark-
green in color, and the prevailing hue of the surround-
ings where laid is the same ; while the brown spots on the
eggs of the other birds resemble the russet leaves and
herbage around the nest. So, though no pains are
taken in nest-making, there is sense displayed in choosing
a laying-place. I have frequently been attacked by
plovers when I have come, unconsciously, near the pre-
cious eggs; and it is no laughing matter to get a blow
from that formidable horn on the point of its wing. The
magpie is another bird that will fight a horseman under
the same circumstances. The “black our” has little
chance against these, when almost any harmless little
bird can drive him away. Coastal settlers erroneously
believe that a crow’s nest is seldom, if ever, seen, and
that it is built in the loneliest wilds and at the top of the
loftiest tree. In reality there are snores of nests in sight
of western homesteads, built in mulga trees. And where,
in all Australia, is there a mulga tree 50ft. high ? lie Mrs.
Foott’s poem, “ Where the Pelican Builds Her Nest,” one
hasn’t to cross the bounds of the Never-Never to find out
that. There is a pelican’s nest on Woodlands estate, two
miles N. of Casino, N.S.W. It is in a small tea-tree at
the end of a reedy swamp, and is composed of a fairly-
large dray-load of sticks and twigs. There were two
young ones in it when I found it, and they pecked at me
so viciously that I very quickly retired.
* * * *
Scattered remarks concerning aboriginal
mothers-in-law, by F. J. Gillen, natives’ protector
of Centralia (the references are mostly to the
Arunta tribe, now about 1000 strong) The
members of all Australian tribes, or nearly all, are
divided up into two or more groups, to which dis-
trict names are given ; and the organisation is
such that the men of one particular group may
only marry the women of another particular group,
while the children belong in some cases to the
mother’s group and in others to the father’s. In
the Urapunna tribe descent is counted in the
mother’s line ; in the Arunta descent is counted
in the father’s. In the Arunta tribe if a man is a
Pinunga his wife must be a Parula woman, and
the relationship between them is called unawa,
which includes the English terms husband and
wife. Not only does he cal) a particular woman
unawa, but he so calls every woman belonging to
her group—that is, every woman whom he may
lawfully marry. All women whose daughters it is
lawful for him to marry he calls mothers-in-
law. If a father is anxious to get a wife
assigned to his son when the latter shall be grown
up lie goes to some man who has a daughter stand-
ing in the relationship of possible mother-in-law
to that son. The son and his possible mother-
in-law are approximately the same age. The two
men arrange matters, and in future years, if the
female child when grown up and married has a
daughter, then that daughter—the one first born—
becomes the wife of the boy. The boy’s particular
mother-in-law (of approximately his own age, as
aforesaid) is his tualcha mara. He may never
speak to his mother-in-law (tualcha mara), nor she
to him, but she has to periodically present him
with hair cut from her head, with which to manu-
facture a waist-girdle—mostly, with a few head-
THE LATE REV. ZACHARY BARRY.
Photo., J. Hubert Newman.
bands, armlets and necklets, the only clothing
worn, winter or summer. On the other hand, he
has to keep on good terms with father-in-law—not
only with the particular one whose daughter he
is to marry, but with all his tribal fathers-in-law,
any one of whom has the power, in punishment of
any infringement of tribal custom, to take away
the wife-that-was-to-be and present her to some
other man to whom he is also tribal father-in-law.
Also, the son-in-law has to provide the father-in-
law with food.

“ Rick,” on cows :
Have been told by cow experts that they (cows, not ex-
perts) will eat anything. I believe it. About a fortnight
ago there was not a blade of grass to be seen on the pad-
docks along the Williamstown line, and have seen cows
lick the rocks till they were polished and worn away.
Will undertake to say that one cow I noticed particularly
lived on rock for a fortnight, but was in very poor con-
dition at the end of that time. I saw one enterprising
dairyman put his cattle in on to the Melbourne city tip,
at North Melbourne, and, being of an enquiring turn of
mind, I went over and examined the shepherd in charge
as to what they ate. He said they would eat anything,
and just bit into things as they came, making no assort-
ment.

The murderous whip-the-cat economy of
Westralia, the boom-country. From a letter
from one of the Bangemall (W.A.) gold centres :
A good many natives have come into the camp, and a
more wretched lot of scarecrows I never saw . . . They
are principally old men and women. All the younger
and stronger natives have either been sent to gaol or are
working on the stations. A lot of these poor wretches,
until the new regulations were issued, were being fed by
one of the storekeepers on the field (on 9d. a day), but
now amount has been reduced to 3d., for which no one
would pretend to feed them. Instructions have been
:nt to the police to drive them all away to the bush,
where, it is said, they could get any amount of bush food.
But the only part these natives could be driven to has
not had any rain on it for three years, so what chance
have the wretches, too old to hunt, of procuring seeds or
roots !
♦ * * m
“ Wallaroo” : The morbid-mindedness of
Australian bush-dwellers is most apparent in for-
lorn, stranger-shunned districts where curlews and
other night-birds make night ghostly with wail-
ings and screechings. The least hint of pathos in
the voice works on the dog-like sensibilities of
such people, and, dog-like, they whine without
questioning the wherefore. They weep over their
quarrels, arguments, births, deaths, marriages,
farm-troubles—everything and anything. The
unfortunate finder of the victim of a recent shoot-
ing accident has “acted queer” ever since. A
young fellow who watched through a window the
exit of an old fellow into eternity has since been
gazing stonily into space, unconscious of all
around him. Another young teamster went, full
of love and grog, to see his girl, but found his
rival flashing his teeth and wit with evident suc-
cess, and sank a moaning wreck to the ground.
When he was found, whisky was sent for, but
meanwhile the sufferer mysteriously disappeared,
and it was not until they investigated the source
of a weird, dog-like howling that they discovered
the stricken one thus venting his feelings under
the shelter of a big log.

Department
of ’ Htiuxliation and Prayeb.— At a
■ meeting of the Executive Council held yesterday
the matter of the drought was debated. It was';
, eventually resolved to set aside a day as oue of
humiliation and prayer. A proclamation to that]
effect wap issued, and it was resojyed that the
attention of the clergy and ministers of . religion
should be called to the same. Mr. Brunker,
Colonial Secretary, states that he had no doubt
that the intimation would be readily aeoepted by
the different churches. The proclamation is as
follows Whereas in consideration (?f tha
very serious calamities from which the colony is
now suffering, by reason of the protracted
drought, it is proper that a day should be set
apart on which all persons may unite in hurailia*
tion and prayer to Almighty God to obtain,
through His divine mercy, a mitigation or re*
moval of these calamities : And whereas it i#
right and expedient that an opportunity should
be given to the clergy and ministers of religion of
all denominations, and all others her Majesty’*
subjects in the colony of New South Wales, to
observe Buch a day of humiliation and prayer ;
Now, therefore, I, the Governor of New South
Wales, with the advice of the Executive Council,']
'do hereby direct that Sunday, the 23rd day of,
October instant, he appointed for the purpose
aforesaid, and express the earnest, hope fiat all
classes of the community wiU join with reverence
and humility jn tbie_aolemn__appeal the Divio#
mercy.*’

MELBOURNIANA.


The championship tourney just concluded,
after many months, at Sydney Chess Club, has
resulted in a brilliant win for E. N. Wallace, for-
mer Australian champion.
Mrs. Maclurcau’s cookery book is having
an excellent sale, and the first edition is almost
exhausted. The book, which can be bought from
all the local stationers, only costs 2/9, and as it is
suited to Queensland and contains over 750 recipes,
the value given far exceeds the sum necessary for
its purchase.*
Line upon line. A common remark on entering
a well-stocked ironmongery store is—■“ What a number
of lines you keep.” This is true, but the art in keeping
a stock is just to keep the very lizie your customer wants
at the price he wants. In McLEAN BROS. & RIGG
LTD. Melbourne and Sydney warehouses you will find
the very article you are looking for. Implements for the
farm, ironmongery for the house, guns for the sportsman,
fishing tackle for the fisherman at eye-opener prices. If
you can’t inspect write for quotations.*
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously-, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the.result of years of
experience, can be had in 3 sizes, by post 1/4, 2/6,
3/8., in N.S. W. only. To all the other colonies
1/8, 2/8, 3/8. Beware of imitations. Only address:
Senior, chemist, 246 George-street Sydney.*

A Voice from the Yukon.
Prospector ” writes from the Yukon:—
Dear Bulletin.— ■lt’s 11 months since I left Westralia
for the Yukon, arriving at Vancouver in 0ct.,’97, since
when I have not seen a Bulletin, though I have tried to
get it. B.’ was my bread-and-butter 18 years ao-o
when, as a youngster I sold it on Sydney streets. For
13 years since then I have lived almost entirely in the
back countries of the various provinces, mining and well-
P° r ‘ n f >?'!■?/ which time I have never been without The
Bulletin till my arrival in Vancouver.
Those Australians here who are now waiting for
the Yukon to open for navigation will never forget
their experience from the time they left Ska-
gway or Dyea and crossed the White or the Chil-
koot Pass. A little of my own experience.
After paying our last dollar to the American customs
ivJu 6 ™ . on Canadian-bought goods, we (3)started
March to hand-sleigh our outfits, aggregating close
on 50001b5., from Skagway and did not reach our present
camp till May 20-71 days doing 60 miles! When we
reached the summit of the White Pass, after pack-
ing our outfits over Porcupine Hill, we were dis-
gusted to be informed by the Canadian police that our
outfits, though purchased in Canadian territory, and
being of Canadian manufacture, would have to pay 25 per
cent, ad valorem duty because they had been shipped from
a Canadian port in an American ship. So there was
nothing for it but to return to Skagway and earn the
money—over 100 dollars.
From “Tagish Lake, where we are at present, we
expect to start in a few days. Have met on the trail
Australians from every province and they—even the
N.Q. s stand the cold as well as anybody ; nor do any of
them seem to regret their venture. Still, apart from the
experience, which is altogether new to the Australian
miner, I think, when the numbers go up, it will be found
that many of us would have done more good with our
energy, time and money, in prospecting various parts of
AUSl)rAll9i>
JOSEPH KNIGHT, Prospector.
John Norton has a Melb. double in
“ Tom ” O’Callaghan, Inspecting Superintendent
of Police and Chief Commissioner presumptive.
“C.S.” : In the bad old days when 400
Chows was a common shipment to Australia, and
Spring Cove, Sydney, looked lonely without a
yellow flag flying on the Faraway hulk, slant-
eyes with their piles were ever journeying home-
wards, and every China steamer carried its com-
plement of coffins in readiness for celestial occu-
pants. The manner of preserving the rice-fed
remains was effective, if crude. Also it was
lucrative to the doctor. The sea-Galen—often a
hospital hard bargain—having disembowelled the
corpse, stuffed the emptiness with tow saturated
with carbolic acid and jammed the body into its
Chow casket, which was filled up with more car-
bolic and closed with pitch seams. Queer tales were
told, too, of some of these embalmings(?) One doctor
became beastly and unaccountably affluent after a
return voyage. All the home-going Chows had
sovereigns, and those from Queensland gold-dust
as well, concealed next their hides. Ah Hing,
say, would one morning feel poorly—a sort of beri-
beri was rife amongst those who ‘had been on the
Palmer—and Ah Hing would see the “ doctor,”
who would kindly allow the invalid to remain aft,
away from his noisy fantan-playing compatriots.
Then Ah Hing would get worse, and do the rest of
the voyage dead, and in pickle for planting. And,
somehow, when Ah Hing’s sorrowing friends came
aft to view the departed, his bowelless cadaver was
always minus the gold-belt. And such a hustler
did the eminent scientist become at packing away
his lost patients that an envious skipper and chief-
officer insisted upon coming in for shares. For, as
they said, what was the good of turning over the
estate to Chinese officials, when such deserving
residuary legatees were on hand.
A working man in China earns not more
than 3d. a day, but on that he can marry and live
as well as a married man and family can in Aus-
tralia on the lowest living wage. Thus, if he can
make the lowest living wage for a single man in
Australia, plus Is. per day, for each year he keeps
at it, and saves that Is., he acquires enough to
keep himself and family for four years in China,
whereas if the single white man works for the
same wage and saves the Is. per day, at the end
of the year he has =£lB 5s. —not more than lowest
living wage for a man and family in Australia for
12 weeks. And it is this fact, independently of the
Chinaman’s personal characteristics, that makes
him and his kind such undesirable immigrants.
The Government of the egg-bald Dickson
is starting badly in Queensland. When the Bill
abolishing attachment of wages got into commit-
tee, Drake’s amendment extending the operation
of the measure to both sexes, and to clerical as
well as manual workers, was carried by 35 to 8.
Then the Government’s Officials in Parliament
Bill was thrown out by a majority of seven.
Dickson goes right on despite these defeats, but,
obviously, the Continuous Ministry which has
been bossed successively by Griffith, MTlwraith,
Nelson, Byrnes, and Dickson is growing shaky at
last. It is now in its ninth year, and a Govern-
ment is usually very ancient and toothless at that
age.
Mrs. MACLURCAN , Queen's Hotel, Townsville , authoress of “ Mrs. Madurcan's Cookery Book.’
The Papuan Land Steal.
A WELL-informed correspondent re the Papuan
land grab :
Tub Bulletin (24/9/’9B) tears that it may cost the Ad-
ministration of British Papua more to acquire the 250,000
acres for the Somers Vine crowd than the contract price
of 2s. lam sure it will, and I claim to have some know-
ledge of Papua and its inhabitants, notwithstanding the
Governor’s self-sufficient utterances in Brisbane recently.
Moreover, my Papuan experience extends over the whole
of the island, and for several years, with survey and
exploration parties, I was brought into close con-
tact with the natives. I am certain, as far as the
coastal regions and lowlands —especially well fitted for
tropical cultivation—are concerned, the land will
come to very much more than 2s. per acre. The
whole coast is densely populated, and the Papuans are
not only an agricultural people, but their laws of land-
ownership are well defined. No chief can sign : w i}', in a
state of cheap trade-gin elation, whole kingd mi. The
aboriginals are a very democratic set, and spin up into
numberless small tribes, the whole available ground is
divided and sub-divided into countless tiny blocks, the
owners of which will have to be individually satisfied —or
despatched. The latter alternative has been a very good,
time-honored South Sea fashion, and 1 cannot disabuse my
mind of the suspicion that the expediency of this scheme
has recommended ilself already to the grahsters. There
are so many and quite respectable ways of despatching
the savage. Where there are eoenanut-groves or planta-
tions the land will be doubly dear, in many oases abso-
lutely unprocurable. The large valleys in the interior
are also densely populated, and the uninhabited back
country is too unspeakably vertical, and inaccessible
generally, to be of any use at present, while the eleva-
tion will not allow of cultivation of high-class tropical
produce. The cost of transport, moreover, would compel
the syndicate to stick to the coast. The whole scheme —
from the ethical point of view Gov. MacGregor claims to
regard it from—spells simply wholesale eviction and intro-
duction of forced hard labor among the lazy, easy-going
Papuans. (By-the-bye, they do remind one of the Irish,
with their insular proximity, their little potato-patches,
their thriftlessness, their fighti ig proclivities—and then-
probable fate shortly). More or less compulsory baptism,
I suppose, will also be thrown in as a sop to the mission-
ary influence.


The Boston Shower.
The greatest luxury one can wish for after a day’s
work on the farm or among stock is a good bath.
Unfortunately, in few rural dwelllings is the water
laid on so that on returning from the fields at
night the wished-for bath may be indulged in.
The Boston Shower has been invented to meet
this want, and it has many and great recom-
mendations. It is ready at a moment’s notice;
easily put in operation ; can be used, with either
cold or hot water, and, above all, it is extremely
cheap. The Boston Shower (of which Messrs. M.
Moss & Co., the old established merchants of
Sydney and Melbourne, are the proprietors) may
be set up by anyone, and any corner in a shed
will serve for the purpose if there is no bathroom
on the place. All that is wanted is a bucket of
77T
...
cold or warm water, and the bath is ready. The
block showing this patent shower illustrates how
it is set up. The patent consists in a most in-
genious pumping arrangement, enclosed in a metal
disc about 4ft. 6in. from the ground. A short
handle projects from this disc, and by working
this backwards and forwards a continuous shower
is produced. The action of the pump is remark-
ably easy, and requires scarcely an effort to force
the water into the shower ; at the same time it is
so powerful that it will throw water 150 ft. This
means to comfort and health is within the reach
of the smallest selector in the country. The price
of the “Boston Shower” is only 375. 6d., and
when packed weighs 451bs. Messrs. M. Moss and
Co. will, on application, be pleased to forward
further particulars.

MELBOURNE RACES.
Holiday excursion tickets to
Melbourne will be issued at Sydney by the
ordinary 9 p.m. Mail Train from MONDAY/
24th October, to FRIDAY, 4th November, in-
clusive, available for return within three months
from date of issue by Mail Train leaving Mel«
bourne at 6.15 a.m.
FARES:
First-class Return, £5 | Second-class Return, £3lss
These Tickets will not be available for return
by Express Train, except by payment of the
difference between the Ordinary and Holiday
Excursion Rates, viz., 21s. 6d. First-class and 16s.
Second-class.
At all other stations Holiday Excursion Tickets
will be issued from MONDAY, 24th October, to
FRIDAY, 4th November, inclusive, available for
return for three months, at the local Holiday Ex-
cursion Rate to Albury, plus the Excursion Fare
Albury to Melbourne, viz., 38s. 7d. First and
255. Bd. Second-class.
On the Southern Line, in cases where the two
local fares (viz., to Albury and thence to Mel-
bourne) amount to more than the through rate
from Sydney to Melbourne, the Sydney rate only,
viz., £5 First-class and £3 15s. Second-class, will
be charged.
On the Western Line passengers will be charged
the Holiday Excursion Rate (via Blayney) to
Albury and the Local Excursion Fare Albury to
Melbourne (as shown above) added ; but when
the passengers from the Western Line travel via
Sydney Holiday Excursion Fare to Sydney plus
the Holiday Excursion Fare Sydney to Mel-
bourne will be charged.
On the South Coast and Northern Lines
passengers will be charged the Local Holiday
Excursion Fare to Sydney, plus the Holiday
Excursion Fare Sydney to Melbourne.
BY ORDINARY EXPRESS AND SPECIAL
EAST EXPRESS TRAINS the Fares from
Sydney to Melbourne will be <£6 Is. 6d. First-
class and £4 11s. Second-class, available for re-
turn by any train within six months from date of
issue.

TWENTY-FIRST REPORT, PRESENTED TO THE SHAREHOLDERS at the ORDINARY
GENERAL MEETING on 28th OCTOBER, 1898.
The Directors have pleasure in placing before Shareholders the annexed Balance-Sheet and State-
ment of Accounts to 30th September, duly audited and certified.
The net profits (including £4137 14s. brought from last half-year) amounted to <£8226 35., of which
the usual dividend at the rate of 3 per cent, per annum will absorb £2250, leaving a balance of £5976
3s. to be carried forward.
. The Directors desire to express their thanks to the Shareholders who have assisted them by bring-
ing deposits and influencing other desirable business to the Bank. With a continuance of their
valuable aid, the Directors feel confident that a steadily progressive course can be maintained on safe
lines, with a corresponding increase in profits, when the present keen competition in rates abates.
By Order of the Board,
H. T. WILSON, Manager.
