POLITICAL
POINTS
No sooner is France free than there is no
more France. It recalls the parson who said,
‘ As soon as a church gets out of debt it begins to
wane.”
“ Bildad ” : What is the sex of the sjngle-
tax? Though embraced by Ashton and other
Ms.P., its ansemic condition indicates it a con-
firmed old maid.
Let it be remembered that Kingston and
Cockburn were the only members of the first
Federal Convention who voted with Hori Grey, to
make the Governor elective.
The hilarious humor of Vic. Leg. Council.
Hon. Mick Fitzgerald in reply to Wrixon’s pro-
posals for the reform of the Council:
If you make the Second Chamber—the Chamber of
Review—a body with a basis nearly as wide as that of
another place, you encourage the revising Chamber,
because of its very strength, to be obstructive.
Fancy Vic. Council needing any encouragement
to be obstructive !
During the exciting Stout-Vogel
regime in Maoriland in the mid-eighties
the competition of the press correspond-
ents at Wellington for news led to some
amusing contretemps. Vogel, struggling
for a second lease of power, and know-
ing that the depression following on his
borrowing had greatly discredited him,
saw that a policy of sensation was his
only hope. He took extraordinary
measures to prevent forecasts of his
contemplated coups reaching the press,
and, being an old journalist himself,
was up to most of the tricks of the
trade. His financial statement, for in-
stance, was set up at the Govt. Printing
Office by comps, who were kept under
strict surveillance and were threatened
with all sorts of pains and penalties if
they gave “ the show ” away ; and
Vogel insulted the pressmen by pad-
ding the ceiling of the Cabinet room
(it was immediately under the press-
room) with sawdust, to prevent eaves-
dropping. The correspondents took up
the implied challenge, and all . sorts of
devices were used to get to windward
of the astute Treasurer. A Wellington
scribe made a fair “scoop ” by surrepti-
tiously entering the cabinet room just
before the Ministers met and, connect-
ing the telephone with his office, left
the receiver unhooked and was thus
enabled to catch a few echoes of some
very private matters. But Vogel was
completely out-generalled by the clever
ruse of an Auckland journalist on the
very day on which the mysterious fin-
ancial statement was to be delivered.
The correspondent, playing on Vogel’s
weakness for posing as an Australasian
statesman, wrote the Treasurer a polite
note asking for a 100-word forecast
“ for Melbourne Akgus.” Vogel, keen-
ly watched from the press-gallery, hesi-
tated, read and re-read the missive, and then, with
pathetic reluctance, wrote a note and despatched
it to the anxious scribe in the gallery. Vogel's
reply was, “ Impossible until after delivery in the
House to-night.” But the pressman, having ob-
served the pantomimic irresolution, reckoned that
his fish would rise to a second cast, so he wrote
back : “ Argus wishes to write leader on state-
ment. Please write synopsis on enclosed blank
telegraph-form, which I have signed, and des-
patch yourself.” Vogel this time gulped the bait
whole, wrote the forecast, and sent it by his per-
sonal attendant to the telegraph-room. But the
pressman was there too, and a whispered instruc-
tion to send “ that Akgus wire to my paper in
Auckland ” enabled one happy editor to beat his
competitors by many hours.
Fysh goes to England, after all, as Tas.
Agent-General. As a Treasurer he will be difficult
to replace. As a hard worker and an honest
financial bullock for an uphill road Tasmania has
few like him.
As most of the Australians reckon their
public servants superannuated —if not useless —
after the age of 60, why not apply the principle to
legislators? Victoria might begin with the old
men of the Upper House.
Drake, M.L.A., recently told his Enoggera
constituents that he always tried to avoid treading
on people’s toes if he could possibly manage it.
That’s just what is the matter with Drake—he
doesn’t offend half enough people.
THE VOTE FOR THRIFT.
By law-reports is often shown
The Fat Man’s patriotic drift—
He gets a vote, not for his own,
But for the other fellow’s thrift.
Premier Reid claims to have favourably
considered the sentiments of the publican class by
taking into his cabinet Hogue, Lee and Parkes
instead of Garrard, Gould and Syd. Smith. Bung
will judge his pudding by his eating, however.
Chinese cabinet-makers are not averse to
the special stamping of Chinese-made furniture.
Said one, “Supposin’ Misser Leid, Misser Barton,
stamp tea packet ‘this tea glow in China’ you
think Eulopean no drink tea? Hy yah ? All a
same furniture.”
_ According to a statement by M‘Coll, in
Vio. Assembly, during the Land Bill discussion,
'<!• Government is offering to convey Vic. farmers
and their families and belongings to Leperland, if
they will consent to go there and take up land.
The luck of Hoolan, the loquacious Queens-
land M.L.A., is dead out. For seven weeks of
the present session he kept silent, and refused to
be ‘ drawn ” by friend or foe. When at last he
broke the ice, he was forthwith suspended by the
Speaker for seven days.
Next Q. elections will probably prove that
Premier Byrnes’ death was the biggest slice of
luck ever thrown to Q. Labor-party. Thousands
i j V u* ;es would have been cast for the party
led by a native-born and Irish-Australian Premier
will now go in favor of the Opposition.
Brisbane Courier pours out columns of
wrath on the recalcitrant Q. Labor-members, but
in the matter of “ language ” Battersby it main-
tains a squid-like silence. But, then, Battersby
once saved the Government by tumbling inside
the bar and^ equalising the votes. The “right-
thinking ” Speaker Cowley gave the necessary
majority.
The motion of Labor-member Hughes, in
N.S.W. Assembly, that Sydney should be the
Federal capital, was supported by 12 votes, of
which the Labor-party supplied six. The Federal
capital will bring with it much extra population
and wealth, which means increased land values,
and in Sydney these would all be snavelled by
the private landlord. WherCas, if the Common-
wealth made a fresh start in a new locality, it
could be its own ground landlord on pure Land
Nationalisation principles. This makes the situa-
tion of one or two Land Nationalisers among the
six Labor-men look rather curious.
UP TO DATE.
MR. HANMEDOWN ( to customer) : “I’m afraid we're just
out of our Yes-No size !”
The Kingston Ministry’s only railway
scheme, a line to Innamincka on Q. border to
divert the stock trade, was rejected by three
votes last week. The line which was fathered by
railways commissioner Pendleton and guaranteed
by him to pay from the start, was described by an
opponent as “ commencing in Purgatory and end-
ing in Hell.”
Queensland Speaker recently “ sent up”
some half-dozen members for seven days apiece.
Apropos of which Q. Hansakd breaks out thusly :
“Mr. Hoolan thereupon proceeded to the table,
and, filling a glass with water, said ‘ Here’s luck
all round.’ The hon. member then left the
Chamber, raising his coat-tails as he retired.”
Now this is picturesque !
Had Downing-street incapacity not driven
Hori Grey from his South African satrapy, it is
probable that the race bitterness now existing
there would have been much ameliorated. Froude,
for some time at the Cape as British Commissioner,
has left it on record that all the peoples there—
Hottentots, Boers, Britishers and Kaffirs said to
him, “ Send us back Grey ; he understands us.”
The numerous pocket-handkerchief-allot-
ment owner is not the only objectionable pluralist
in Victoria. M’Culloeh, a Minister supporting the
Plural Voting Abolition Bill, stated in the Leg.
Council, the other day,---
I, myself, because I happen to be a member of several
companies, have votes for 15 or 16 different constituencies
round Melbourne, although I do not own a piece of land
within the city or suburbs.
A list of road-and-bridge grants made be-
tween March and July, 1898, shows that, of all the
N.S.W. electorates, Hastings and Macleay re-
ceived the biggest allowance. And yet when
Potato Young appealed wildly to the electorate to
show its gratitude and its sense of more roads
and bridges to come by electing Pumpkin Smith,
the pampered district refused even to look at
Smith.
A return laid before Q. Assembly shows
that “free” and O.H.M.S. telegrams in the
northern province totalled ,£73,000 for previous
year. Weather-prophet Wragge claimed <£25,000
as his share. If some of the “free” messages
could have been laid on the table as well, it would
have been very instructive. No wonder certain of
the Opposition got weak in their political knees at
critical periods.
The division by the N.S.W. elections com-
mittee as to the legality of Mudgee election is an
interesting study. The four Reidites, Ashton,
Neild, Mahony and Whiddon, all voted for up-
setting the election ; the four Opposition mem-
bers plumped just as unanimously on the other
side. It was left to Labor member J. H. Cann,
to virtually decide the matter, and he saved the
seat for Mr. Richards.
W. J. Denny, editor of the S.A. Catholic
paper, talks of standing for West Adelaide against
Premier Kingston and Labor-Leader Batohelor at
the general election next April. A large propor-
tion of local residents are Irish, and the Arch-
bishop’s palace with the headquarters of most of
the priests, is also within its limits. Denny s
brother is now studying for the priesthood in St.
Mary’s College, Manly.
When little Dr. Maloney introduced hia
clauses for allowing the father of an illegitimate
child to register it in his own name, I rank
Madden enunciated a most astonishing argu-
ment: “ The intentions of the hon. member who
moved the clauses are, no doubt, of the very best,
but when he is making these efforts to remove
some of the stigma attaching to illegitimacy he
has to recollect that a greater than he ” [presum-
ably the Almighty] “ has ordained that all chil-
dren should be born in original sin.”
Tasmania has at last decided to centralise
its police-force. Hitherto, every little rural
council has appointed its own solitary bobby, and
each copper had to have his own officer to look
after him because the officer in the next district
could not interfere. One result was that the force
was wretchedly paid, undisciplined, and greatly
over-officered. Another was that no policeman
dared to seriously enforce the law against any
local dignitary, else he inevitably lost his billet.
Tasmania stood this system for a long time, but
the isle has at long last revolted.
Melbourne Australasian, by way of
showing the utter needlessness of the clause in the
new Vic. Land Bill which provides for the com-
pulsory resumption of large estates by Govt., says
that in M.L., where Govt, has this power, it has
only been used once. Which is quite true. But
then it is only because the State has the power of
oompulsory purchase that the owner generally
comes to terms. The fact that it has only once
been necessary to use the power doesn’t prove that
it should be abolished, any more than the fact that
Australia only once had an armed insurrection on
hand is proof that the laws against armed insur-
rection should be repealed.
N.S.W. should note that it is the dark
Protectionist places that almost monopolise the
W.A. food supply, as witness the labels in Kal-
goorlie stores. Jams, vegetables, fruits, butter,
biscuits, cakes, puddings, oatmeal, dried fruits,
fish and meat almost entirely from Vic., Tas., S.A.
and M.L. ; California and Europe contribute in
fruit, vegetables, fish and jam. Out of <£3,000,000
worth of Australasian goods imported into W. A.
in ’9O (the last year shown by W.A. statistics)
Freetrade N.S.W. supplied only <£287,000 worth.
The Freetrade enthusiast might explain how it
comes about that though tea enters W.A. free of
impost, there appears to be hardly an ounce of
even moderately good tea for sale in that thirsty
land.
Carruthers on the Federal resolution de-
manding increased control over “our” rivers:
“Of 240,000,000 acres in N.S.W. 142,000,000 are
watered by the Dari in g and Murray River systems. ”
Cohen : “ Yes, and in all those constituencies the
Bill was accepted by large majorities.” No-Yes
and co. and the Sydney D.T. tried to discount
this at the late elections by saying these border
voters were ex-Victorians who had come to
N.S.W. Yet the same God-fearing crowd,
backed by the same organ of sudden variations,
has drearily reiterated for years that the farmers
of Victoria, ruined by Protection, were swarming
to N.S.W. to enjoy the blessings of Freetrade.
And these alleged Victorian refugees are voting
enthusiastically for a policy which will probably
bring Protection to N.S.W.
All the arguments advanced against Barton
acting in the M‘Sharry case while a sitting mem-
ber of N.S.W. Leg. Assembly, avail much more
powerfully against a host of other practices in-
dulged in with impunity by Ms.L. A. and Minis-
ters of the Crown. For example, during last
general elections one of Reid’s Ministers was sole
owner of one newspaper and part owner of another,
and while m these relations to them the papers
accepted contracts for Govt, advts and duly
executed them. MsL.A., who are newspaper
proprietors, invariably accept engagements to
advertise for a quarter or half-year Govt, notices.
The Parliamentary law dealing with the subject
of Ms.L. A. working in their private capacities for
the Crown needs modern interpretation, but the
directing of all its extreme pains at Barton, while
cases like those mentioned pass without comment,
proves the virulent animosity of party spirit.

Storey, M.L.A., in N.S.W. Assembly :
“ Suppose the capital of United Australia is
located at Albury, Wagga, Cootamundra, or some
other place 200 miles from Sydney, how will it
be possible for any man to be a member unless he
is a purely professional politician ?” If the capi-
tal is.to be at Sydney it will still be just as im-
possible for any man from Queensland, S.A.,
Tasmania or Westralia to be a member unless he
is a professional politician. (Storey himself, by
the way, is a lovely specimen of the amateur poli-
tician). The trouble .with the Storey kind of
parochialist is that he can’t remember that there
is any place but N.S.W. in the Federation.

With much pressure on their bald pates,
the Tas. Upper House ancients took the tax off
meat; and, though the consumers bet on a drop,
and the butchers shed blood - and - salt tears
in sympathy, beef and mutton are as high-
priced as before. As sweet consolation for the
oppressed poor, the ’aughty Hinglish trout anglers
are advancing stringent measures for running-in
the unlicensed fishermen ; and Govt, is enforcing
the vermin close-season and rabbit poisoning.
Back-country spud-cookies are now spending all
their spare days either stalking protected ’roos or
tickling trout. The great wonder is that there is
not more sheep-stealing.
The story of a round-robin signed by a
number of Q. Govt, supporters against J. R.
Dickson’s assumption of the Premiership. Firstly,
a certain legislator hawked the document round
and touted for signatures. Seventeen members
put down their names, and two of them found (so
they say) shortly after, that their names figured
at the bottom of a paper differing considerably
from the one they signed. Then the gentleman
who had organised the protest backed down and
became a Dicksonite, leaving his supporters to
kick themselves vigorously for a pack of fools,
while the outside public grinned delightedly in
the back ground.
If the Federal capital is fixed in N.S.W.
The Bulletin suggests, as a few promising jilaces,
Ben Lomond (4471 ft. high), Armidale (3264 ft.),
Kiandra (4640 ft.), Cooma (2657 ft.), also Hillgrove,
Bombala and Nimitybelle. In these bracing,
elevated regions the Federal Parliament wouldn’t
suffer from that Tired Feeling. It would be
bright and vigorous. When the inevitable
struggle for supremacy arose between it and the
State Parliaments a Legislature that hung out at
Ben Lomond or Kiandra ought to simply walk
over the tired, perspiring-Parliaments that swelter
in Brisbane, Sydney, Melbourne, Adelaide and
Perth. The Bulletin wants to see the Federal
Parliament win every time, and the first essential
to that end is to plant it in a bracing atmosphere.
Besides, the Federal city should speedily attract
50,000 people at least, and in these regions 50,000
robust fighting Australians could be grown —not
50,000 pallid weeds leaning up against a public-
house corner.
“ Tay Way ” to The Bulletin :
What is there against Wilcannia as a site for the
Federal capital ? I put the case for it thus :Itis in N.S.
Wales. Inland. On the Darling-. Nearly the same dis-
tance from three of the four capitals. It could be in
direct railway connection with all four in six months by
extending the lines from Cobar and Broken Hill a little
over 100 miles each. And by a direct line from Swan Hill
and a loop from Nyngan to Werris Creek the railway
connection could be made almost perfect in a year or two,
and at the same time open up good country ; and when
the two great lines from Perth and Port Darwin come to
hand pass- -rig-era would rea'-h the Federal capital sooner
than Adelaide, and he in Sydney or Brisbane before they
could get to Melbourne at present. Is it too soon to
speak of another great line of the future! From Nor-
mant.on by way of Gilherton, Longreaeh, Charleville, and
so on to Wilcannia—The Federal Capital. Now let us
hear the other side.
[The main argument against making Wilcannia
the Federal capital is the one which people would
chiefly use against making Hell the Federal capi-
tal. The climate.—Fid. B.]
Re the “ striking-off” of that hatch of
would-be voters at Isisford, Q. Here is the situa-
tion as explained at length in Q. Leg. Assembly.
According to the form issued to voters the attestor
has to sign his name and qualification (J.P., or as
the case may be) three times on each form. (The
Home Secretary issued instructions to Electoral
Registrars that any trifling error should not in-
validate the claim). Well, on these claims the
attestor omitted to add J.P. after the third signa-
ture when attesting. In the interval an intend-
ing opponent of the sitting (Labor) member had
the cast-iron cheek to issue instructions to all
J.sP. in this electorate to be careful as to what
names were added to the roll. This gave the
needed cue, and when the claims came up for
registration the chairman of the bench, the
attestor aforesaid, disqualified the claims he had
witnessed himself owin'l to his own last signature
not havin'i J.P. after it! What can happen to a
man in Queensland if be tries to be an elector.

A Plaint from Rue Australia.
The following letter, written on both sides of
the paper, reaches The Bulletin from Rue
Australia, Paraguay. A similar one, which
struck the office in one of its tired moments,
was dropped in the basket some weeks or
months ago :
To the Sydney Bulletin.—
Sir,—l have been imprisoned falsely by the Paraguan
Government. I asked Mr. Holmes, the British Consult,
Asuncion, for protection on March 2 last, on the 3rd
prox. I wrote Mr. Holmes the following- letter :—“ Sir, —I
called at your office yesterday, and asked you, sir, as the
British Consult, for protection from the Paraguan Govern-
ment. As lam a British subject, with hearing my side
of the case you told me that you knew all about
it, and directed me to go down this street, turn to
my right, keep the market on my left, and it would
bring me to an English Bazaar kept by a man
who could speak English and he might be able
to tell me where I could get a lawyer. You
also asked me if I could speak Spanish. I told you
No. I then asked if that was all the British protection
I would get. You said Yes, that is all the British pro-
tection you will get. You would' oblige me by answering
this in writing and let me know if you have any more
protection to offer.” Mr. Holmes said that I could not
compell him to answer this letter in writing. And that I
was an New Australian and had throwing up all alligiance
to the British Government. I told him I had done no
such thing, as I was only a recent arrival in Paraguay.
Charge one of assault on a pretty woman age 32." In-
tendente King laid a secret charge against me to the
Minister of the Interior, and he without making further
inquiries (although the woman was present) sent a war-
rant for my arrest to the Geppe-politico Ajos, a
villiage 130 miles from Asunsion. I was arrested and
taking to Ajos. The prosecutrix had six witnesses
The Judge held an inquirie to see if it was worth a trial.
When he had spoken to four witnesses he dismissed the
case without trial and said I should not have been in-
teredfeared with. The Giffe politico said the warrent
was that stiff that he went himself to arrest me so that I
would not be ill-treated by his men. I was three day
under arrest, and I live ten miles from Ajos (Protarcos),
and the pigs I had fastened up got neithir food or water
whilst I was away. I seen Dr. Aceval, a lawyer and
brother to the minister of the iriteriorir. He told me
there was no such thing as sueing the Paraguan Govern-
ment for false imprisonment. The United States Consult
told me had I been a United States subject things would
be different, but he could not interfear because there
was a British Consul to go too.
Dear Bulletin, I wrote you a letter like this, also one
to the Home Secretary, London, on last March 9th, and I
believe both went astray. Would you please publish what
you think proper of the above if this should reach you.—
Yours truly, Robert Prior.
The Bulletin asks this wild Rue Australian
what he is grumbling about? He was arrested
(apparently on the information of one of his own
countrymen) on a charge of assaulting the nice-
looking brown woman—which is liable to happen
to a man anywhere. This paper doesn’t profess
to know whether he assaulted the nice-looking
brown woman or not, but apparently the
charge wasn’t proved. Anyhow, Paraguayan
justice was so prompt and so fair that he was
acquitted in three days and got back home
again before the pigs were dead. In Australia
it would have taken him at least a month to
get an acquittal, and the pigs would have been
quite defunct before he got back. Moreover,
it is 1000 to 1 that he wouldn’t have been
acquitted in Australia ; the mere say-so of any
nice-looking brown woman in this country, let
alone a nice-looking brown woman with six
witnesses, would have got him seven years and
very likely two floggings as well. In Suf-
field’s case the bare assertion of the
woman, with all the evidence on the side
of the defence, got the accused ten years.
And if the man who writes on both sides of
the paper had been acquitted in Australia, he
wouldn t have got a farthing damages against
the Government. Anyhow, the person who
goes to live in Paraguay, on the land benevo-
lently provided by the Paraguayan Govern-
ment, can’t complain if he has to submit to
Paraguayan law. And Paraguayan law seems
so eminently just and honest, and so prompt in
its dealings, that the complainant ought to feel
more like presenting it with a bouquet than
wanting damages. If a Paraguayan got into a
similar difficulty in Australia— God help him •
the law wouldn’t help him much, and indi-
viduals like the present complainant on both
sides of the paper would remark that it served
the blasted foreigner right. What strikes Thk
Bulletin most was the inhumanity of the Rue
Australian who went away leaving the pigs
locked up. It feels sorry for the pigs.

The recent demise of medico-clerical Dr.
Zachary Barry recalls the days when an Orange
was an orange in N.S.W.—not a boiled potato !
and when Drs. Barry and M'Gibbon (each worthy
ot each) ran the Protestant Standard with a
blood-curdhng ferocity at singular variance with
the personal dispositions of two specially genial
and kind-hearted men. Their bigotry, in a
word, was purely perfunctory, and official, and
distressed “green” stood as good a chance of
assistance from them as did any other color. Dr.
Barry in particular was, off paper, the mildest of
men, and was simple—despite his portentous
knowledge to the point of credulousness. When
he took over the Standard the doctor already
saw himself another Luther, but climate and
cricket were too strong for him, ahd with charac-
teristic candour he admitted that he would die a
fizzle.”
C. H. James, the “father of the land-
boom,” who died at Burwood, Sydney, the other
day, kept a little “grocery” in W. Melb. in the
late 70 s. About ’B5, Mrs. James inherited some
money, which her husband invested in the pur-
chase of a paddock at Alphington. He promptly
laid out the paddock with streets of high-sounding
names, cut the land into 30ft. allotments, and sold
them, to the accompaniment of free drives and
dinners. James was a long, lanky person, with a
flowing beard, but, despite his appearance, was a
bit good with his hands,” as many know to their
sorrow. When in Queensland, during the shear- ,
ing strike of ’92, he stopped at the house of a big
squatter, but awoke suddenly one night to think
that he was home in his house at Toorak. Hear-
ing a noise in the passage before his door, he
instinctively bounded from bed and, grabbing the
intruder by the throat, dealt out stoush so vigor-
ously that the man fell, “ knocked out.” The
row brought the house out with lights, on which
James realised suddenly where he was, and saw
mthe supposed burglar none other than his host.
The incident was duly published to the world
as an “outrage by unionists,” while the boss
when he came-to, wired all round the Darling
Downs: “Look out for C. H. James; he’s got
burglars on the brain.” James had a let-off
another time. He was ascending the lift at the
Hotel Australia when a beery person lurched in,
and, catching him by the beard, said, “ You want
a shave, sonny ! ” James glared. “ You drunken
scoundrel! ” he roared, “ I’ve a good mind to
knock you down.” The dipso, only laughed, and
when he got out at the first-floor, James asked the
lift-man who he was. “John L. Sullivan,” was
the alarming answer. Personally, James was a I
genial and intelligent man, and, as one who has
had business dealings with him, the writer gives
his opinion that he was the straightest man of the
boom he created. He was a gambler, but not .a
thief, and when one venture, in which he had per-
suaded people to invest, turned up its toes, James
paid everyone his losses in full. Later on his
ventures collapsed so fast that he had to give up
this interesting little habit.

WHAT THE DOCTORS SAY
Doveton-street, Ballarat,
My dear Mr. Malyon,— August 21st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record pro-
gress with regard to my old enemy “ Eczema Capitas,”
from which I sutfered, notwithstanding constant treat-
ment for some years under the prescriptions of Pro-
fessor M'Call Anderson and other eminent derma-
tologists, with only partial and temporary relief.
When I met you I commenced somewhat sceptically
your ointment Ligrnolliene, and after about two months’
treatment I may honestly say my enemy is defeated,
and lam perfectly well. I will only add that since I
commenced the treatment of myself I have prescribed it
to others, and am still doing so with brilliant results.
Please accept my hearty thanks, and remain yours sin-
cerely, THOS. LE GAY HOLTHOUSE, M.R.C.S., L.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
“ The brown bees on Hymettus—”

My Mrs. Browning scholarship grows dim ;
This horse,
Of course,
That won the Caulfield Cup—

But every man agrees
There ain’t-no bloomin’ flies on him l

* • * *
A field of 33 lined up for the Caulfield Cup,
therefore The Bulletin doesn’t offer an abject apology
for having failed to mention the winner in" nine horses,
one of which (Clarion) was a non-starter. The eight
actual goers—The Chief, Grafter, Clarion, Reka, Thunder
Queen, Acton, Koseberry. Bunyan, and Massinissa —-will
probably be mentioned again for the Melbourne Cup, for
none of them disgraced themselves on Saturday. Mas-
sinissa and The Chief finished second and third .; Rose-
bery made all the running for more than a mile; The
Grafter and Thunder Queen came sixth and seventh ;
Reka and Acton were among the seven that fell. Bunyan
did nothing in particular.
Hymettus —and this truth can’t be too vigor-
ously jabbed into the punting mind—had no apparent
chance of winning Caulfield Cup after his performance in
the Caulfield Stakes. The Age report on that perform-
ance will be found amusing and instructive —“Hymettus,
who was running in a foremost position, was the first to
cry enough Hymettus was distinctly disappoint-
ing, as he failed badly just at the point where a Cup
fancy would be expected to show brilliancy.” But
Hymettus, as everybody now knows, did show brilliancy
in the Coongy Handicap four days later, and three days
after that he became a dazzling hero. The explanation
of his absurdly in-and-out running is rheumatism, or
sciatica. Perhaps it was a sudden attack of pains
in the back, or somewhere, that caused Hymettus
to collapse in the Caulfield Stakes, and finish absolutely
last. There is no suspicion of crooked dealing in this
case. The present writer was not thinking of “ reversals
of form ” through natural causes when he wrote, oh Oct.
8th, “ the orse that runs nowhere in Caulfield Stakes is
quite likely to be a prominent performer in the Cup
race.” But it’s all the same to the public whether
sciatica or get-atiea is the cause of a horse running badly
a week before he wins a great race. In either case the
confusion of “ form ” is the same. And what doth it pro-
fit the public to back horses which come to grief in a
scrimmage, as Reka, Acton, Robin Hood, Majestic, and
others did last Saturday? In England it is a common
prac.ice to follow a jockey’s mounts. In Australia a
jockey runs a considerable risk of being dismounted, if
not dead, before the numbers go up.
Caulfield takes the cake for bloodshed in flat-
races. In ’85, when a mob of 40 Cup cattle came jostling
round the last turn towards home, the central portion of
the mob suddenly dropped out of the running. Seen
from the Grand St md, it was as though the earth had
opened to swallow the oncoming herd. Fifteen or more
horses had fallen. After the race was won the public
found entertainment in watching a procession of hurdle-
bearers down the course. On each hurdle was a battered
jockey, one of them, Donald Nicholson, done to death.
And, as The Bulletin remarked at the time, the bell rang
out whilst the corpse was being carried into the stewards’
enclosure. It was the saddling bell for the next race. A
race-meeting is a show where the death of a leading per-
former makes no difference.
Last Saturday the horror of the hippodrome
occurred in the straight, just past the judge’s box, and
was not due to the sharp turn of the rail that threatens
danger thereabouts. A disturbance amidst the rush —a
shout of “Look out!” —a few screams from women—a
general anxiety to learn which horses had “disappointed
their backers,” followed by a pretty general turning of
attention to the race—Ikey Mo shocked to discover that
Massinissa, Hymettus and other favorites were still alive
and going well. The voice of a metallician remarked
ruefully—“ Did you see Cocos save ’isself by jumping
over some of’em !” Perhaps the gentleman had laid a
lot of money against Cocos. Meantime, it was necessary
to clear the course, for the field would be round there
again in less than two minutes. Two of the fallen jockeys
wi re evidently oblivious to this fact. One of them, in
the black and yellow diamonds jacket of “ Bravo” Jones,
was hastily picked up and carried away by some people
who had ventured upon the field of battle. His limp
form looked as if ithad been trampled upon by many hoofs.
The first man to reach the other motionless jockey re-
coiled at the thought of touching him. Poor little
Flanagan's brains were protruding from his skull. How-
ever, the body was duly shifted before the shouts of the
crowd hailed Hymettus as winner. Then cheers for the
owner.
Caulfield is not the only place where Cup trage-
dies have happened. Aleck Robertson, who won Melb.
Cup on Malua, was afterwards killed in a Sydney Cup
race, in the days when Raridwick course had a name for
accidents. The very mare, Grace Darling, who won the
’85 Cup at Caulfield, got a nasty fall in the Veteran Stakes
at Flemingt.on a little while afterwards, and was no more
good for racing. Her subsequent failures accounted in a
measure for ihe death of her owner. There have been
several falls at Flemington—in Cup races, too—but some-
how they didn’t produce shocking results. On the
slippery occasion of Glenloth’s victory, Portsea came to
grief, but the little horse fell out of harm’s way. The
great Carnival Smash is a thing of the future, and it will
be brought about probably by the foul tactics of some
reckless young blackguard who accepts a commission to
interfere with the favorite, or by the groggy condition of
a rank outsider which goes to the post for the sake of the
“ starter” money in Tatts.’ sweep.
A rather ghastly reminder of the Cup catas-
trophe cropped up a couple of hours later, when Morgan,
the rider of Superb, took the mount on Stand Off for the
Windsor Handicap. His face was damaged in about six
places, and one nasty wound on his forehead appeared to
trouble the lad considerably.
Australian broke down in the Caulfield Cup,
and the winner was dotty on a foreleg as he stood to be
photographed in the Birdcage. Likewise Massinissa had
seemingly knocked himself up, and Reka, in sundry
places, looked the worse for his tumble.
* * * *
Men and women who levelled their glasses on
poor, mangled, 13-year-old Flanagan, turned sick at the
sight. Few backers or bookmakers thus troubled them-
selves. The joy of one bookmaker at the fall of Reka
caused him to see how high he could jump, and another
threw his hat in the air the moment the winner was no
longer in doubt. “My blanky troubles ! ” said another of
the brotherhood in reply to “How many are killed?’*
Nor could many of the backers of Hymettus restrain
their jubilation. “ Put it there !” cried onebell-toppered
Johnnie; and the two richly-dressed “ladies” accom-
panying him made pump-handles of the gloved paws.
Many men were heard to curse Massinissa for not getting
home, and one man explained to the writer that he knew
blanky well, and said so before the race, that “Reka
would either win this time or break his blanky neck, and
it is like my blooming luck.” Anyhow, the race was fol-
lowed by more curses than champagne.
At the inquest on the unfortunate jockey
Flanagan, Coroner Morrison, of Melb., intends going into
the whole question of bantam-weight riders. It’s about
time. Out of the 33 starters in Caulfield Cup, there were
20 whose weight was listed as not over 7st. 71b., and 10 of
these 7st. or under. Which means that either lately-
weaned mannikins are put up to manage big, skittish,
and occasionally 7 nasty-tempered horses, or that the same
animals are entrusted to .youths who have had to sweat
down to a weight that leaves them destitute of strength.
It isn’t as if the 6st. rider is the one who always comes to
grief; as often as not, his horse swerves and plays up,
bringing down a dozen behind.
* * * *
The second (extra) day’s racing at Caulfield gave
punters something to chew. In the Eclipse w.f.a. Stakes
(1 mile), The Chief was backed at even money to beat
Cocos, Wait-a-bit, Lelamine, and others. The son of
Gozo had run clean away from Wait-a-bit at the finish of
Caulfield Stakes (9 furlongs) four days previously 7 , and as
they were now meeting on the same terms as before he
seemed certain (as racing “certainties” go) to score
again. Cocos was penalised 71b., and hadn’t a fairchance
on collateral “form” to heat the favorite. Yet Cocos
won, after a determined struggle, by a neck from Wait-a-
bit and The Chief, who ran a dead-heat for second place.
The distance being a furlong shorter than in the Caulfield
Stakes, would presumably account for some of the
reversal of “ form ” between The Chief and Wait-a-bit.
Cocos’ victory, perhaps, was due in a great measure to
jockeyship. Bob Lewis, a comparatively clever jockey,
rode the youngster on this occasion.
But where does Lelamine come in ? At the
A.J.C. meeting, in Sept., Lelamine heat Cocos for the
New Stakes (IJ mile) bv 2J lengths. They met again on
same terms for the Eclipse Stakes aforesaid, and Lelamine
didn’t make a show against Cocos.
Punters may as yvell note that owner W. R.
Wilson didn’t care to pit Bobadil against The Chief and
The Grafter in the Eclipse Stakes last week, although it
was worth more money than one or two of the soft
things which have been good enough for the Derby
favorite to go for this season. The Grafter would h: ve
gone to the post if “ Bobbie ” had put in an appearance.
But the little ’un was kept out of danger, waiting for the
Derby, presumably, wherein he should only have one for-
midable opponent—Cocos. FairDerhybetting(thepresent
writer reckons) would be 5 to 4 on Bobadil, 3 to 1 against
Cocos, and various long odds against any of the others.
• * * *
At least onoe in each year Melbourne possesses
a fascination that attracts visitors from almost every
quarter of Australia. The season for the annual exodus
is now close at hand, and, within the next week or two,
thousands of persons will, somehow or other, so time
their visit as to be present in the metropolis during
race-week, which opens with the V.R..C. Derby on 29th
instant. Indications point to the present occasion wit-
nessing one of the largest gatherings that has assembled
at Fleroington for some time past.
* * * •
The rush for tickets in Tatt.’s new sweep, draw-
able on Melb. Cup morning, is conclusive evidence of how
the public feels re the “ owners’bounty 7 ” arrangement,
which has come to be regarded as an almost inevitable
feature of the ordinary sweep system. Tatt. questioned
the genuineness of the demand for eleventh-hour draw-
ings, and the answer is an emphatic approval of The Bul-
letin’s suggestion. Or call it a hearty protest against
horse-owners’ claims to “stand in” gratis with those
lucky ticket-holders who may happen to have drawn
valuable chances in a racing lottery 7. Anyone risking ss.
or more in a horsey 7 speculation would prefer to know
before the event the name of- the animal which he hopes
to have going for him, but the result of Tatt.’s “ special ”
sweep experiment shows that thousands of investors would
rather sacrifice their sporting interest in a race than agree
to a 33J per cent, taxation of prizes by owners. For that is
how the system pans out in the ordinary 7 way. Every 7 starter
represents, on paper, three chances of winning big sums.
When you have won your starter through a lottery
wherein the owner of the horse has no honest concern,
you are persuaded to give the grasping stranger a third
share in your paper prospects. It doesn’t matter whether
he persuades you, or you persuade yourself without wait-
ing to hear from the gentleman. He gets there either
way.
Ticket-holders in general begin to feel deeply
that the owner has no right to get there at all, conse-
quently the sweep which doesn’t give him a show to in-
terlope is a popular novelty. Its financial and moral as-
pects are alike beautiful and soothing. And presently,
when the Public learn that the lucky 7 ticket-holders in
Tatt.’s ordinary ss. sweeps on Melb. Cup paid thousands
of pounds to “ensure a run for their money 7,” whereas
the special ticket-holders got the same run for nothing-
well, the Public will reckon that a whole loaf, coming as
a surprise, is better than the two-thirds of a loaf which
was expected. Therefore, the sweep drawn on the morn-
ing of the race is a speculation that has come, t.o stay in
this country, otherwise Common Sense will want to know
the reason why. “ Rough on owners,” The Bulletin
hears somebody say. Rough on rats !
* * » *
Some time back a Riverina resident set several
hens on eggs, having previously 7 written on each egg the
name of a horse for coming Melb. Cup. Out of 117 eggs
so set, the only one not hatched was the one inscribed
“Trapper.” “There,” said this ingenious tipster—
“ Trapper’s the ‘ dead bird.’ I’ll back him ! ”
* * * *
The action of the A.J.C. in resolving that
“ after the end of the year any person employed as an
official in connection with an unregistered race-club shall
not be employed in any capacity whatever by a club
registered under A.J.C. rules,” is a cruel one, inasmuch
as it strikes one man only, Mr. P. O Mara, secretary of
Rosehill and Kensington race-clubs. Moorefield, Canter-
bury, and Warwick Farm easily dropped into the wishes
of the A.J.C., as it has been an open secret for some time
since that the pony-meetings of these clubs were not
financial successes. Having closed the big suburban
courses for pony-racing, the A.J.C. should have lain low,
and if only in the interests of breeders in-making a mar-
ket for their undersized stock have winked at Mr.
O’Mara’s excellent dual management of Rosehill and
Kensington. In the past, Rosehill and Kensington have
been the only clubs, Randwick not excepted, with pluck
or inclination to tackle the “ influential ” owner, and
racing habitues say there is less crooked running at pony-
racing Kensington than on lordly Randwick. The A.J.C.
has absolutely no show of killing Kensington, the only 7
result of its resolution being to hamper and increase
the expense of management of the two favorite courses,
Rosehill and Kensington, and deprive Mr. O’Mara of a
portion of his livelihood. In their own interests, Rand-
wick had better go slow in tackling Kensington, for if it
comes to a battle royal, and Kensington opposes Rand-
wick on the senior club’s “off” Saturdays, which even
now do not find favor with race-goers, Kensington and its
lower tariff will assuredly come off best.
» * * *
Bookmaker “ Miok ” Moloney, of Sydney, who
was one of the victims of the gambling-raid, last Satur-
day, is a very “ slick ” man. A friend who went round to
the lock-up to see if he wanted bail remarked subse-
quently : “ There was Mick looking through a little hole.
Says l to him : ‘ Is there anything I can do for you, old
man ? ’ ‘ No,’ says he ; ‘ but is ihere anything I can do
for you?’ ‘Well,’ says I, ‘what can you lay me The
Grafter?’ ‘Ten to one,’ says he, and booked it right
there.”
• * * *
Barnett and Grant, bookmakers at Christchurch
(M.L.), have published a pamphlet in defence Of Ikey
Mo, as a reply to a vigorous eulogy of the tote by horse-
owner G. G. Stead. The reply is weak in the back and
gone at the knees, as might be expected. B. and G.
cannot quote one instance in which they have offered the
public temptation to turn from the tote and bet straight
out with its natural enemies. A large slice of the
pamphlet is devoted to an analysis of the betting on the
English Derby won by Jeddah, from which it appears
that a layer getting round on his book at published
starting-prices would not make more than 3J per cent.
profit upon his receipts. Jeddah’s Derby was remarkable
for the liberality of the odds at the post, and it goes
without saying that B. and G. quote the longest odds,
which were to be obtained only from the biggest book-
makers, all of whom had, doubtless, a lot of “dead-meat”
money in hand before the day of the race.
But this is not a point to discuss, anyway.
Barnett and Grant do business in Maoriland, where the
tote charges 10 per cent. com. If they can pay longer
prices, on an average, than the machine pays, their busi-
ness will advertise itself ; the fact that B. and G. are
eclipsing their deadly rival will become generally known,
and they will have good reason to bless the tote for keep-
ing private competition out of the field. Meanwhile,
B. and G. are cursing the tote, and the public draw their
own conclusions. The Bulletin hasn’t space or inclina-
tion to deal with all the fallacies in the pamphlet to hand,
but the following bit of protest deserves to be published
—“Tlie strongest argument in favor of the totalisator
is that it supplies big stakes. Why should the public sup-
ply the stakes (and why shouldn’t they?—Ed. B.) any
more than they should provide the pool at aristocratic
clubs for a game of nap among the members, &c., &c. ?
(Rats ! Under the old system of betting the public pro-
vided stakes for Ikey Mo.) It is surely a gross infringe-
ment of a person’s liberty to dictate to him that he must
do his betting through the tote, whether he likes it or
not, and irrespective of the fact that he might invest his
money to better advantage with a bookmaker.” Well,
the M.L. public are allowed to “ invest to better advant-
age with the bookmaker,” if they want to. The existence
of B. and G. proves it. But B. and G. want to grossly
infringe the public’s liberty by dictating to them that
they must do their betting with the bookmaker, even
though they might invest to better advantage with the
tote. That’s what’s the matter.

Sample letters received by a big sweep-pro-
moter : “ We are very poor, and have started a Wesleyan
school, and have only got 20 scholars as yet. Do please
send us a prize.” Another: “Am taking this ticket in
the name of my little boy ; he is very anxious to buy a
pony ; please send him a horse.”
* * * *
Adelaide sporting men are so wroth with the
local Council of Churches, because of its crusade against
lotteries, and so much public indignation has been caused
by the suppression of the charity carnival art.-union, that
private detectives have been tolel off to watch the pro-
ceedings at church bazaars and get up cases against par-
sons and their lady admirers who may so far lapse from
virtue as to promote raffles.
* * * *
In Perth (W.A.), lately, after a man had been
fined for running an illegal “ tote” the presiding magis-
trates announced that “ any charitable institution apply-
ing for the implements, seized tote-tickets, &c., could
have them.” Truly, charity does cover a multitude of
sins !
• * * •
How would the N.S.W. lottery-law affect this
case? A number of Sydney residents have lately been
the surprised recipients of blocks of art-union tickets
from a M.L. town, of which some of them never heard
previously. Arc-union is in aid of a local church, and
prizes range from a gold-vase to “three hundred litho-
graphs of his Grace Archbishop Redwood.” Also, a com-
plimentary ticket, alleged to be worth Is., is placed at the
end of the book for the person who disposes of £1 worth
of art-union. If the Crown Law people are not too
utterly flabbergasted by the audacity of the far-away
cleric responsible for this move, writer expects to hear
that some ignorant and unfortunate enthusiast has been
fined £SO for trying to help along some people he never
knew, in a place he never heard of, and for a cause of
which he knows nothing beyond the fact that it has some-
thing to do with the Chur-r-ch.
* * * *
How is this for gambling luck ! In Bridgetown
(W. A.) district 12 timber-allotments were thrown open,
and, there being 122 applicants, a ballot followed. One
man put ill nine separate applications for as many lots.
The chances were 13 to 1 against his getting a single
allotment. As for his chance of winning the whole nine,
Tiie Bulletin has lost most of its remaining hair trying
to calculate what the odds were. Yet he won the whole
nine ; every one of his numbers was drawn, and the other
113 applications only got three allotments among them ! !
And the deal was presumably quite honest—at least, no-
body said anything to the contrary. A man with a
mozzle like that could lay 10 to 1 against the only horse
in a race, and the animal w’ould be struck dead by a
meteor at the starting-post.
* * *, *
Wellington (M.L.) has anew kind of horse-
crank. This individual breaks into stables in the early
morning, cleans out the place, grooms the horses, feeds
them if he can get at the provender, and then departs as
unobtrusively as he came. And as he steals nothing he
is probably a humble, genuine philanthropist. The
species is so rare that when a specimen does come along
he is pretty' sure to be set down as mad.
’* * * *
In Tasmania, a horse (Kimberley) being schooled
over hurdles, developed a habit of baulking, and if
whipped or spurred would lie down. All the jockeys
who took him in hand were beaten, till one Harry Roles,
a well-known cross country rider, one day tied the horse
down and left him so for two days. That cured him !
**‘ * *
After the high-jump at Echuca Show, when
Fairfield and Strathmore tied, the officials made a care-
ful measurement, when it was found the take off was 3in.
lower than where the posts were fixed, so that the actual
jump was 6ft. 9in., equalling Spondulix’s best, made in
Queensland in ’92. America’s record is 7ft. 6J4in.

« > * *
Sydney Opera House was packed last Wednes-
day night, on the occasion of the Hajek-Lucifer weight-
lifting and wrestling match. The supply of heavy dumb-
bells was not what it should have been, and trouble with
the audience was only averted by the ready wit of referee
Foley. Hajek looked burly enough to eat the slender
Lucifer, but he lacked condition, and the latter won the
two lifting events. The wrestling was not what Sydney
was used to in the days of Clarence Whistler and Connors,
Lucifer putting a bit more acrobatic work in than is
usually seen in a bout of Graeco-Roman. Lucifer had the
best of the work throughout, and, eventually getting a
half-Nelson on, slowly but surely turned the heavy Hajek
over on his back, and won the deciding event.
There were fully 1000 onlookers, and the pair of
men of brawn must have netted over £IOO. But what a
display ! “ Jack ” Connors could have settled the two of
them inside five minutes, The powerful Hajek knew
absolutely nothing about the game, while the winner
knew very little more. Until Lucifer got on the half-
Nelson and armlock that gave him the fall, not one legiti-
mate hold was secured throughout the 22 minutes.

Tom Hope, who aspires to fighting Tweedie fot
the feather-weight championship of Australia, was seen
out, after a long spell, on Saturday night last, when, at
the Golden Gate Club, Sydney, he beat “ Chummy ”
Brown in five rounds. Unless Tom improves some, he
has no hope of hitting Tweedie, and much less of beating
him.
• • * *
Lucifer relates a Charters Towers experi-
ence of his. For advertising purposes he used
to leave his heaviest dumb-bell outside the hall
wherein the company was appearing, and a printed
notice suspended above the implement intimated that £5
would be given anyone who could lift the weight.
Several tried and failed, but one day, much to the
owner’s surprise, the dumb-bell disappeared altogether.
A diligent search of the town was made without success.
Eventually it proved that a young miner had not only
lifted the weight, but had actually carried it away and
planted it, and, what was more, refused to bring it back
again. Lucifer threatened, but the miner defied him,
and a match resulted. The American won after a brief
but hot struggle, and the dumb-bell was returned.

under contract to the Queensland Athletic Club, to meet
Jack Tuckwell.

Cable tells that the Corbett-M‘Coy affair is off,
and a go between Corbett and Sharkey is on. Corbett
would have had a better thing on with M'Co.v, as Sharkey,
a rushing, hard-hitting fighter with as hard a chivvy as
our own Ironbark Jemmy Burge, is always a bad sort of
an opponent for a good-looking, scientific fighter of the
Corbett stamp. Unless Sharkey throws the fight away
on a foul, he should win.
* • • •
Strange that Fitzsimmons and Corbett have not
come together again. The pair would draw a bigger
crowd than any other two, and if Fitz. really thinks it a
good thing he is scarcely the man to throw a hatful of
dollars away out of spite. Can it be that Fitz.’s “solar
plexus ” whack was a lucky one, and he doesn’t think the
chance of repeating it any too good ?
* * * *
A Bulletin writer attributes Premier Byrnes’s
death to young Australia’s want of vitality. Here are
instances from the world of sport : Searle, in the first
flush of youth, succumbs to illness. Stanbury, in bis
prime, is beaten by old man Gaudaur, who was rowing
when the former was in petticoats. See how the Irish-
born Hanlan and the English-born Beach lasted—till
middle-age. Look at the Australian-born boxers : Jem
Hall, Griffo, Slavic, Jack Molloy, &c., dead or gone to
pieces. Yet old man Fitzsimmons is champion ; and,
despite age and fast living, which tells more on colored
gentlemen, Peter Jackson can still put up a fight. Con-
trast Tom Hales’s retirement with Ford ham’s or John
Osborne’s. Pedestrianisin furnishes best instances of all.
Of the many hundreds of peds. around Sydney some ten
years ago, not one can raise a trot. The present cham-
pions—Rogers, Cummins, the Marshes, &c.—belong to a
younger generation. It is over 20 years since Harry
Hutchens won a couple of handicaps, and proved himself
an athletic wonder. Hutchens is still running so well that
the handieappers keep an eye on him, and no one will be
surprised if he wins next Shrovetide —“21 ypars after.”
Our “ veteran ” cricketer, Blackball), retired years ago,
but Grace, who began at least a decade earlier, is still
going strong.

The second reading in S.A. Assembly of
King O’Malley’s Bill providing forthelegitimatisa-
tion of children born out of wedlock, where parents
subsequently marry, caused little debate, and it
seems certain that the measure will be passed
through both Houses. Despite the Premier’s op-
position there is a majority in the Assembly, too,
in favor of the Bill for securing a fair share of a
testator’s estate to the widow and children, but
it will not get through this session.
Dowell O’Reilly was beaten at Parramatta
by six votes only and is petitioning against the
return of Major Ferris. During the general
election Mr. Neild was ill in bed and Mr. O’Reilly
syent some time in Paddington pleading for
Jawbone. Hid he stopped at home h'e'mighthave
won over those six votes and so saved his own seat
in the House. Mr. Neild is on the Elections and
Qualifications Committee. However honest he
may be, he should not. under the circumstances
have been chosen to adjudicate on the Parramatta
case.
A very old settler died at Singleton
(N.S.W.), on 23rd Sept., in the person of Peter
M‘Alpine, aged 89i years. This hoary veteran
and his brother, William M'Alpine (18 months
younger), who survives him, arrived in N.S.W. as
children in 1812. Their people settled on the
Hawkesbury, and are related to the Onus family
Peter remembers Muswellbrook in 1825, and saw
some “life” in Melbourne in the forties and
fifties. His last ad venture in the land of Gillies
was a fall of 50ft. down a shaft, accompanied by a
huge inebriated Highlander, who characteristic-
ally was on top. Peter’s legs were shaky ever
after, but his brain was very bright to the last.
There are few older colonists alive than his brother
William, who resides at Bulga, near Singleton
and still actively attends to his farm. ’

“ In chat with the two Australians,” continues
our correspondent, “ each expressed his intention of
‘loafing’ the whole winter. Trott has had six seasons
of cricket in succession and will not go to South Africa
this year, whilst Roche fears the cold months, and, sick of
the ‘ national game,’ wants to go to bed and hibernate.
In reply to congratulations on a smartly hit-up 49 in a
recent match, Roche said, ‘ Oh, that’s not much use to me.
I’m there to get men out, and a score —unless at a pinch—is
rather resented. Cricket here is very' different to cricket
in Australia.’ The prospects of Major Wardill’s team
were rather discounted, and Trott thinks Howell and O.
McLeod certainties and the former the best bowler in
Australia, Both players look the better for their English
season.’

S.A.’s crack batsmen have begun the season
badly; Darling, Giffen and Lyons made 18 runs among
them on Oct. 8. Jones, however, took 8 wickets for 46
runs. The latter is in wonderful form.
• * * *
Melb. Cricket Club will probably come to an
amicable arrangement with the ruffled Cricket Council re
the control of future Australian Elevens. The players,
anyway', ought to understand that the Council is an essen-
tially unselfish institution, and the only form of Federal
government that Australian cricket, can get—therefore a
Council being very desirable, why not make the best of
this Council ? But it takes the average “ representative ”
cricketer a long time to understand anything except his
own importance. A man who has a beautiful leg-stroke,
or a powerful break on a medium-paced ball, is a painful
tm-god to reason with.

The 485 mentioned in The Bulletin’s “ Sport-
ing Notions ” of 15 10/’9B was made by A. E. Stoddard for
the Hampstead Club v. Stoics in August, ’B6, and is the
highest individual score ever made in any match. A. C.
Maclaren’s 424 for Lancashire v. Somerset in July, ’95, is
the highest individual score oil record in a first-class
match; and Murdoch’s 321 for N.S.VV. v. Victoria at
Sydney, in ’B2, is the highest individual score ever
obtained by an Australian in a first-class match. Worrall’s
(not out) for Carlton v. Melb. University, in Feb., ’96,
is the highest individual score ever obtained by an Aus-
tralian cricketer in any match.

The booklet that is being got out dealing with
M Donald s trip is likely to prove of exceptional interest,
as it not only.describes the overlander’s journey', but gives
a number of illustrations of Central Australia that have
never been published before. Those people who would
like to know what Central Australia really is like should
send at once to any of the Austral Cycle Agency’s Depfits
for copies of the work, and their names will be booked
and dealt with as soon as the little booklet is published.
The A.C.A. has decided to make no charge for this very
interesting work.

The longest distance ever ridden on the road in
24 hours is 428 miles. This was accomplished by F. R.
Goodwin, who thus beat George Hunt’s previous record
by miles ; his mount was a “ Swift.”

and pleasure trip, is back in Melbourne with full particu-
lars of the new Dunlop tyre. Proctor’s arrival is quite an
event, as the question of the form and nature of the new
tyre is of great interest to every cyclist in Australasia.*

For the tenth time, or thereabouts, Yic.
Government is considering the advisability of
building the Mildura railway. There is every
reason to believe it would lie a paying speculation
—all the cheap lines which the Turner Govt, has
built out on the western plains where construc-
tion costs next to nothing per mile, have paid
reasonably well. But Victoria has been afraid of
railw.iy-building ever since its dreadful experi-
ence in the days of Duncan, the Boomer. It has
had to shut up so many miles of Duncan’s utterly
insane railways that, despite a reasonable amount
of construction, it has seven miles less line open
for traffic now than it had in 1895.
‘‘ An Enforced Patron of Sydney Six-
pennies,” to The Bulletin :
For the past two years this class of lodgings has very
much increased in Sydney, and the legend, “ Good, clean
beds for single men, fid. per night, or 3s. per week,” is
very frequent in the western ends of Elizabeth, Castle-
reagh, and Pitt streets, and in every part of Sussex-
street. This pay-by-the-night system is a convenience to
a good many men who would be quite unable to pay the
3<. in advance. There are two or three classes of this
kind of establishment, and at the present time in Sydney
very few small businesses are as sound as this. The capi-
tal invested is trifling, and the spot-cash system is
strictly adhered to, except that in rare cases patrons
are allowed a two-nights’ account, but no more.
I have known this sort of trade to be begun
with £lO capital, and as many as 20 beds erected, a
few of which might be simply a spring-mattress resting
on a couple of fruit-cases. However, the whole thing
was a going concern before the tenner was exhausted ;
and, once started, there isn’t much expense. The wash-
ing is the biggest item ; that with soap, lights and black-
ing, would amount to about 10s. per week; which, with
rent added, would make the outgo about 255. Thus, as
the rooms are, as a rule, fully occupied, a profit is left of
about £2 a week. The Castlereagli and Pitt street houses
are run on a more extensive scale. Usually a former
warehouse is selected, with as many as five flats con-
verted into dormitories containing perhaps 40 beds. No
doubt if a little more privacy could be provided, and a
little more fuel in winter, these establishments could be
made to pay even better. The Sussex-street houses are
numerous, but are on a smaller scale ; in many instances
combined with some other small business, and usually
frequented by men employed in the locality. In the
Elizabeth, Castlereagh, and Pitt-street houses I have met
doctors, lawyers, journalists ; and, in fact, every higher
walk in life is at one time or another represented. The
man who has formerly been running a paying business is
to be met there. The clerk, the shopman, and the third-
rate actor are in evidence ; also, as a matter of course,
the boozing remittance-man who supplements a brief
season of joy with a long wait of enforced penury. You
will also meet the young man with no particular liking for
any steady occupation, and with probably a weakness for
pony-racing. The man who assists at auction-sales is
also there, as is the tradesman who only gets a job occa-
sionally ; and the young man from the cofintrv finds out
these places even before his cheque is done. Often on a
Monday night you have the young man from (say)
Botany, who has been at the Tivoli and missed his tram,
but must be out earfy next morning to work. Pages
might be written about the deadbeats, broken-downs,
and waifs and strays generally, who frequent these places.
The Sussex-street. cribs are also largely kept going by
“ beam-ended ” sailors, who get an occasional job on the
wharf.

“M‘G.” jumps with all his feet upon a
distinguished bard :
How Bedford the minester has spoilt the idea of Bed-
ford the poet in “The Star of Clontarf Bill” ! This has
been a marked characteristic of the man since he went
into the share-biz. ; in the old da,\ s, when he hustled
around journalistically, his aggression was devoted
to his work, and the'result of his vehement battling
with an idea resulted in the production of such fine
things as “The Cry of a Clod” and “The Christs of
Trade.” To-day Randolph is no longer the Reckless, ex-
cept in regard to his verse, which is turned out anyhow
with a few excrescences like “ shoon,” “ noctivagant ”
(sic), “doxydaw,” “incension,” “ perpendicles,” “ iso-
toned,” “altisonant,” “ concrescent,” and “ Trisalgon,”
to give it an air of profundity. How many readers of
average education know the meanings of these extra-
ordinary words without consulting a dictionary, and how
many are able to find them there? As “Clontarf Bill”
stands, it is simply a strong idea concealed in a mass of
unmusical lines that bump iike a punctured bicycle on a
corduroy road.
“ A genius is a man with an idea usually
himself.
Death—the distant hill that does not look green.
Self-Interest —-the card we always keep up our
sleeve.
Experience—what you lost everything else in
gaining.

gentleman who writes to The Bulletin saying
that it is a case of no Chinaman no vegetables
might inquire into this.

The stalwart Kins Hedley, now performing at Melb.
Royal, was originally a Bairnsdale (Vic.) boy. Growing
up, he entered N.S.W. mounted police, and was under
fire at the capture of Capt,. Moonlight, at> Wantabadgery.
Afterwards, he left the police to journalise in Gippsland,
abd eventually departed for Yankeeland, to return to
Australia as the “ Great American Actor.”
Carl Hertz on the Indian fakirs “ After I got my
reputation among them the Indians trotted out the best
they could do. I found out that the mango trick is done
by substitution. As I have said, they are far ahead of us
in ’sleight of hand. Then they have cloths that help
them, as they are much bigger than the handkerchiefs
used by our They begin by planting a mango
seed and covering it with a cloth. They work under
the cloth with their hands, and then remove the cloth at
intervals, showing you the mango bush growing larger and
larger. At last they show you a large bush that has
fruit on it. It appears to have grown from the seed, but
it is all a trick, done by substitution. The mango is as
tough as whalebone, and can be crumpled up in any-
way and packed into very small compass. Working
under the cloth with his hands, the magician straightens
the leaves out.
Mr. Rodda Perrv, the phenomenal male contralto whom
Mr. Newbury “ discovered ” in the wilds of Bendigo, is
certainly astonishing, but his voice already sounds as if
it were going to crack. Cyril Monk, who went to Europe
to be trained for a soprano, has suddenly developed a rich
tenor with which he is shortly to paralyse London audi-
ences
L. L. Lewis, who makes a hobby of charity-amateur-
theatrical work, paid most of the expenses incidental to
the matinee at Melb. Princess’s, in aid of the Hospital for
Sick Children. The best kind of benefit-promoter is the
man who burdens himself with a particular anxiety to
keep the exes, as low as possible.
“ Curra Curra ” : At Echuca Show I saw snake-charmer
Davis allow one of his tiger-snakes to bite a chicken in
the breast. The chicken went “groggy” at once and
soon died. Davis says the Australian tiger is the most
venomous snake in the world.
“P.A.” : A true story of the stage. Heard, a vocalist
render. 11 My Old Dutch” with tremendous pathos; lots
of people wept, and the mummer himself shed a few
drops. Next day, while his wife was at her lodging-house
window, nursing her first baby, a smart drag whipped
past. It contained her husband, a male friend, and two
Flossies, who, with a well-filled hamper, were going to
the races. They laughed loudly as they passed the lonely
woman at the window, and the vocalist yelled, “Hello !
there’s my old Dutch,” and the whole four roared out the
chorus as they sped on. 1 Then, strange to say, the woman
at the window cried.
The prettiest, most brilliant, most fascinating actresses,
whoever they may be, are preparing their usual annual
lies for the social-paper gobemouches about winning
immense sums by miraculously spotting the Melb. Cup
horse. They all do it.
The latest wizardry to puzzle England and America is
the cricket-bat trick. The operator gets three ladies to
pick cards out of a pack. Takes them back. Then a
gentleman throws all the cards at a cricket-bat in the
wizard’s hand : the picked cards alone stick to the bat.
Explanation : The cards are forced on the ladies by a
device familiar to conjurors. Similar cards are face-
downwards on the green cloth of a table, their backs
being undistinguishable from (he cloth. , They have
sticking stuff on the backs, and adhere to the bat, casually
laid on them. The cards thrown at the bat are a substi-
tuted pack. The wizard turns the bat round while meet-
ing the cards.
“H.M.”: Too sweeping is “P.A.’s” assertion that
Sig. Hazon can conduct everything. What about a
Beethoven symphony or a Wagner masterpiece ? Ilazon’s
style of conducting is thoroughly Italian—always directed
to effect upon the beholders. Just the same as if a stage-
manager should work up his supers to a sensational
climax in sight of the audience. Both conductor and
stage-manager should achieve required result in rehearsal-
room. Manns, Sullivan, Moskowski, Richter and Nitsche
follow this method, and, beyond an occasional vigor
for a crescendo, nothing is perceptible save the plain
beat. Costa, who made the “Tell” overture a great
speciality, was absolutely passive throughout, but the
effect was there—so much so that Rossini rushed up once
and, bowing to the orchestra, exclaimed, “ Merci! Merei!
C’est magnifique.” Let “P.A.” read, in Mackenzie’s
“ Art of Conducting,” his abhorrence of what he calls the
clap-trap style.
“ P.A.” : This business of making people laugh from the
stage is only a fool’s game, anyhow. I saw a bill-poster
put on the stage in an emergency. He was made up as
a juggins and had to say a few words, but he never said
them—firstly, because he forgot them ; and, secondly,
because the audience roared so at his appearance that no-
thing else could be heard. The performers were equally
affected, and he literally broke up the show. A man
with brains enough to learn to talk a comic song would
have made a pile vvith such a start, but our man never
got beyond it, though for years he boasted of his success
on his first appearance. With such a look of woebegone
idiocy, it’s hard lines that he should have had such a
narrow escape from being a “ great ” comedian.

The famous wethers which the late Prof.
Pearson asked Yic. Parliament to import for
breeding purposes, were repeated in Melb. Age

The Alba Studios, in the Strand, Syd-
ney, with Bert Levy, the well-known black-
and-white artist, in charge, are a new de-
parture in photography. The word “photo-
graphy,” however, falls very far short of what
the Alba Studios- set themselves to do. Their
aim is to produce art-studies in character and in-
dividuality, and to altogether dispense with
theatrical accessories and devices of background.
The new studios have caught on with signal
success, and celebrities who are anxious to appear
at their best in the public eye have had themselves
“studied” in innumerable poses. The proper
course to procure an “ Alba ” portrait is to make
an appointment for a sitting at least three days
previously. Ladies are simply charmed with the
exquisite work of these studios.*

After a blow-out on chicken and cham-
pagne at Athol Gardens (the Sydney picnic
ground of some Irish societies) the other Satur-
day, some ultra-loyal yachting johnnies had their
patriotic susceptibilities wounded by the presence
of a green bannerette, which they promptly hauled
down from the cross-beam in the pavilion, on the
ground that it was “the rebel flag of Ireland.”
Curiously enough, at the luncheon immediately
preceding this injudicious exhibition of Jingoism
a M.L.C. had fired off a martial speech at Gov.
Hampden, in which reference was made to Kip-
ling’s poem about the flag of “England”—the
poem that was inspired by the cheering of an Irish
crowd when the Union Jack on top of Cork
fourthouse waa burnt in a big fire.
W.A. Government’s vigorous attempt to
gaol the owners of Kalgoorlie Miner for reporting
an alleged fight between two legislators at the
back of Parliament House fell through badly,
the result being an acquittal. Certain Ministerial
papers published the same cheerful yarn, but
none of them were interfered with. The Miner
is a somewhat independent and disrespectful
organ, and if it could have been gaoled there
would have been joy in many affluent circles out
West.
Mr. Nelson Illingworth has done in terra-
cotta a bust of Edmund Barton, Q.C., which is
undoubtedly one of the most successful hitherto
essayed by its author. It is an admirably “con-
densed ’’ portrait of the subject. Mr. Barton is a
difficult man to pourtray. His right and left pro-
files are utterly dissimilar, the eyes are unlike in
expression, and even the lips are “out of draw-
ing.” Nevertheless, the full face is strong and
harmonious in character, and gives one the im-
pression of complete unity. It is not too much to
say that Mr. Illingworth has seized upon the
veritable Bartonian characteristics of poise, fea-
tures, and expression, and that the bust repre-
sents a truly faithful portrait. The composition
embraces on the pediment a scroll on which is in-
scribed “ The Federal Leader.”
Ridiculous as is the cry of “ Vote for the
Native Born,” that political slogan got the dapper
little lawyer, Davenport Hoggins, into Tas. As-
sembly. At the recent bye-election one of his
opponents was the present Mayor of Hobart, a
harmless old grocer - person known locally as
‘ ‘ Honest George. ” The barraclcers were on the
job at the door of the booth. “ Give Honest
George a vote,” pleaded a committeeman to an in-
telligent elector. “ Not me,” was the reply,
“ I’ve ’ad enough of ’Onesty ; I’m goin’ fer ’Og-
gins. ” And ’Oggins got in.
The most humorous feature about Mel-
bourne, now that the Presbyterian Assembly isn’t
sitting, is the great anxiety of the gipsies showing
in Exhibition-street to be regarded as Greeks, and
the pains taken by the old oyster-shop Athenians
to prove that they aren’t. The Greeks swear
roundly that the gipsies are rank impostors. The
oystermen of Melb. turned up at the Greek church
last Sunday to find that the unspeakable gipsies
had jumped all the reserved seats, and were re-
garding the words of the priest with rapt atten-
tion. This was more than Hellenic blood could
stand. The Greeks left the building, consulted
one of their number, who consented to act as
oracle, and when the gipsies came out of church
full of pious musings on the hereafter, a dark,
thick-set gentleman named Deepoulopolottsco, or
words to that effect, gave a war-cry, and the
Greeks swept u pon the interlopers. The conflict
was short and decisive the descendants of
Leonidas “ dealt it out ” to the unskilled progeny
of Rameses in a way that caused the crowd from
St. Pat’s to grab its bit av a shtick and evince a
desire to chip in. Eventually a horde of battered
gipsies struggled in one by one to the ground
where they are showing, and sent out for huge
pieces of steak.
From S.M. Herald, Oct. 4 :
THE REPUBLIC RETURNS TO PORT.
Newcastle, Monday.
The ship Republic, which left for Honolulu on the Ist
instant, with a cargo of 1580 tons East Greta coal, re-
turned to port to-day in an apparently sound condition.
An air of mystery has been adopted by all those con-
cerned, and the real cause of the vessel’s return cannot
be definitely ascertained. The press representatives were
debarred from boarding the ship, and the officers refused
to be interviewed. It has, however, transpired that Cap-
tain Jones informed the authorities of the port that when
the vessel rolled she made a little water, and that it was
considered advisable to return. The Republic had pro-
ceeded 300 miles on her voyage when the decision to
again make Newcastle was arrived at, and she was picked
up by a tug 35 miles off the coast. Anxious inquiries
were made as to her condition, and, in reply to questions
put to the captain as she entered Nobbys, the fact was
elicited that she was practically dry, the pumps having
sucked. The water was never at any time beyond con-
trol, and, judging from the information at present
obtainable, it would appear that there was never
any need for apprehending danger. It seems that in
smooth water the vessel was dry, and that it was only
when rolling that she made a few inches of water. It is
pointed out that the majority of wooden ships leak to a
slight extent, and that masters generally expect to find a
little water in the hold. The fact that the ship is in a
safe condition may be judged from the fact that the
harbor-master allotted her a buoy in deep water in North
Harbor, and that the pumps were not used in port. It is
understood that arrangements have been made for an
official inspection of the Republic by Lloyd’s surveyor
to-morrow. The prevailing opinion here is that other
causes, as yet unknown, contributed to the decision to
return to Newcastle.
The Republic was originally owned in Chili, and was
recently sold at Port Pirie by order of the authorities for
port dues. She was knocked down at auction for £350, and
was privately re-sold a few hours afterwards for the sum
of £450.
Evidently, the sooner Premier Reid hurries up
with that reform of N.S.W. Marine Board which
is to establish supervision over vessels leaving
Newcastle, the better. The Bulletin will watch
with interest what happens in the case of the
Republic.
Solicitor P. A. 0. O’Farrell is dying in
Melb. Hospital. What a story of legal, judicial,
ecclesiastical and political high and low life in
Victoria his memoirs could reveal if he had
written and any publisher dared print them !
Once the solicitor for all the ‘ ‘ Irish business ” in
the province and the legal confidant of Robert
Moles worth in the famous divorce-case; now poor,
almost friendless, helpless in a hospital. What a
type of much of the rushing glamor of pioneer
life in a new continent ! O'Farrell is the wild
Irishman who created so much excitement in
Melbourne a few years back by shooting at R.C.
Bishop Goold, and he is a brother to the crank
who was hanged for shooting the Duke of Edin-
burgh at Clontarf. In the early days of Melb.
the massive attorney was one of the biggest land-
owners in the town, but in a few years all
had left him, and he has since lived on
his previously neglected law-practice. O’Far-
rell accused Bishop Goold of having swindled
him, and from being a dutiful R.C. he became
a most bitter opponent of the priesthood.
He entered into extensive litigation against' the
Bishop, but had not a legal leg to stand on—
because, he averred, he had trusted the head of
the church over-much. Failing in the courts,
O’Farrell took the law into his own hands, and,
meeting Bishop Goold in a quiet suburban street
one day, he drew his “ gun ” and waded in. For
this he was sent to gaol for an indeterminate
period, as he could not find bondsmen to guarantee
that he would not repeat his attempt; and it was
only when the Bishop placed himself beyond the
range of O’Farrell’s revolver by dying that the soli-
tor was released. A glance at any of the maps
of the Lands Dept, which show the original
holders of tho different lands surrounding Melb.
gives startling evidence as to his position in the
early times, for a straight line can hardly be
drawn for half-a-mile in any direction without
cutting, (iome block marked “P. A. C. O’Farrell.”
The value of the land he owned would
pay perhaps a quarter of the Vic. public debt.
Yet the erstwhile owner has been found penniless
and taken to a hospital to die
One of the respites Premier Reid finds in
censure motions is that from the time they are
tabled till they are determined aspirants for em-
ployment at the forthcoming Paris Exhibition lie
quiet. The wise billet-hunters cautiously with-
hold applying influential pressure till they find how
the cat jumps.
