Idyllic—and Otherwise.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
Idyllic.
Charles Biedermann rose, as Helena —the peer-
less, spotless Helena—propounded her question.
“You ask me, Helena, if there is anything in
my past I could not tell you ; if I can honestly
come to you and offer you a life as untarnished as
your own has been ; if I can he as perfectly candid
with you as you have heen with me.”
“Yes, Charles, I think I have a right to ask
the question. My friend, Miss Virginal, for
whom I have the highest respect, has talked the
matter over with me, and I have come to the con-
clusion that I am perfectly justified in asking the
question.”
Biedermann compressed his lips “The truth
is,” he painfully beg ui, “my life has not been so
good as it might have been. I have heen tempted
and I have fallen. I have struggled after the fall
and have risen again, a better man for both fall
and struggle Your case is entirely
different. Absence of temptation, a life passed
almost in seclusion, have made virtue compara-
tively easy, and I protest against your assumption
of a right to judge me by a feminine standard.’
He looked at her anxiously. Her face was
averted. His doom was sealed. He turned to go,
butjustashe was reaching the threshold of the
cottage a tender voice rang out behind him, all
tearfully dulcet: —
“Come back, Charles; come back! Who,
indeed, am I that I should judge you 1 ”
* * • *
And so it ran, a little Nineteenth Century
Idyl, tenderly,touchingly idealistic. The variant
subjoined, however, is painfully, practically,
realistically Otherwise.
Othenvise I
A dark-eyed, handsome, full-lipped woman, all
gracious curves and feline lissomeness, stood
regarding a tall, square-built, somewhat pallid
young man, whose physiognomy, whatever else it
wanted, certainly did not lack intellectuality.
“ You want to know, Margaret, have I been
perfectly frank and confiding with you. Have I
not, after the manner of my sex, maintained a
politic reticence towards you regarding my pmt, in
certain particulars. ... I am proud to be able to
say I can bare my bosom for all men to read. . . .
I have striven to keep the narrow path of purity.
I have always treated the women of my acquaint-
ance with chivalrous respect, and can honestly say
that, in this matter at least, I have kept myself
‘unspotted from the world.’ ”
He looked up. A curious, little disdainful
smile was cur Lug her lips—her eyes laughed out
in limpid amusement She turned away and
muttered quietly, “ What a milksop it is ! ”
The Immaculate rose slowly, left the room, took
down his hat, found the street door and departed.
Nobody called him back!
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
On the subject of women workers (in
other than the matrimonial sphere) a solemn daily
journal reckons that “the triumph which leaves
them at life’s close without the love and sympathy
of husband and children has the taste of Dead
Sea fruit.*’ The fruits of marriage, of course, are
invariably sweet. No woman at life’s close ever
has Cause to regret the drunkeii husband who
whops her, or did whop her until she got her
divorce. And children, the dear children ! —they
are unmixed blessihgs in every case.

SYDNEY.
Mr. W. T. ROSE
DOES NOT THINK, HE IS SURE.
HE CANNOT PROPERLY PICTURE
The Horrors of his Illness.
The Case of
Mr. William Thomas Rose
[From the “Nepean (Penrith, N.S. W.)
Times.”)
(BY OCR SPECIAL REPORTER.)
A report having reached this office to the
effect that Mr. W. T. Rose, of High-street,
Penrith, had some time since established
himself in firm health by the opportune use
of a certain remedy after suffering for a long
time from a stubborn and dangerous illness,
our reporter called at Mr. Rose’s house, and
asked him what ailed him some time back.
“ Well,” replied that gentleman, “Iwas
afflicted with
A Terrible Nervous Complaint.
It came upon me when I was weakened, and
my Constitution shattered by a long con-
tinued internal complaint. I caught cold
when out on an all-night fishing excursion,
and on returning home was seized with
griping cramps at the bottom of the
stomach. While it lasted the pain was
simply excruciating. I was bent double
with pain, with my two hands pressed upon
my stomach. I changed to a deadly colour,
and couldn’t utter a word. It felt as though
my bowels were tied in knots. Gradually
the cramps subsided, and left me free for
two or three days. Then the spasms came
on again. The torment was worse than
before. Once more I slowly recovered, but
only to be seized again and again with these
racking spasms. Often in the height of the '
anguish I positively thought I should be
snuffed out. ‘One minute more of this
agony,’ I said to myself, ‘ and I shall be a
dead man.’ I slowly got weaker and weaker.
Every seizure left me worse.”
“ Did you get no advice ? ”
Mr. Rose laughed at the question. “ I
should think I did,” he replied. “ I tried
everything, but nothing I used at that time
could ward off the cramps, or even give me
relief.” . 1
“ I suppose you had other symptoms ?”
“ Yes, my one-time splendid appetite
deserted me, and whatever I did eat gave
me a
Painful Feeling of Oppression
right here (Mr. Rose placed his hand on
the lower portion of his chest to indicate
the spot). Wearisome headaches troubled
me, and a sharp, twinging pain between the
shoulders frequently made itself felt. In
the morning I found my tongue thickly
coated with a disagreeable substance. My
breath was foul, and after every attempt to
eat, wind would generate in my stomach.”
“ Your liver was affected, perhaps ?”
“ Yes, very badly; but I hardly know
what portion of my body was not afflicted.
Then my nerves began to go. That was the
last straw. I had battled as cheerfully as
possible against my disease, but now this
new infliction made me
Downhearted Beyond
Expression.
When I stood up I was so giddy and shaky
that I had to take hold of something to
steady myself. I was frightened to walk
across the road. All night long I lay in bed
all of a shake, and twitching frightfully. All -
sorts of
Foolish and Unwarrantable
Terrors
Would crowd into my mind. I had to sit up
in bed, I really dare not lie down. My
despondency increased day by day. I
can’t describe the awful nervousness that
was upon me. I went into a shaking fit
through nothing at all. The blessing of
sleep was denied me. My nights were one
long misery, and I dreaded the approach of
bed time. Sometimes I was unable to get
out of bed, and at other times I sat up
brooding over my misfortunes, trying to
imagine what it would be like to be well
and strong once more.”
“ I suppose you had the attention of, a
doctor ? ”
“ Yes, I had as many as five different
doctors, but none of them could cure me.
Some slight temporary relief was all that
I ever obtained from their medicines, and
I was soon as bad as ever again, if not
worse. I also obtained all sorts of physic
from the chemists, and my wife bought me
many medicines which were warranted to
cure me straight off.”
“ But they didn’t do it ? ”
*’ They were all rubbish,” said Mr. Rose,
emphatically just new-fangled catch-
pennies; but you know how a sick man
clings to the weakest hope.”
“ I threw them up again often as not;
they wouldn’t stop on my stomach, and I
spent pounds and pounds on them, and all
for no result. When I now look back and
think of the
Miserable State I was in
then I truly wonder I survived. Why, do
you know that I fell away from 11 stone to
8 stone odd. .1 was a mere shadow of my
once strong and robust condition. My
bones were almost coming through my skin.
I was come to the
Last State of Misery.
The present was full of gloom and suffer-
ing, and the future held out to me little
hope of recovery. At last I gave up all
hope of ever getting better. I was as bad
as a man could be and still live. In fact, I
no longer cared what happened to me.
Hope was quite dead. Then my wife came
to me with Clements Tonic. ‘ You must
give this a trial,’ she said. ‘ It’s no use,’ I
said to her, ‘there’s no medicine in the world
that can lift me up again,
I’ve Sunk Too Low.’
She pressed me to try just one dose, and as
it was easier to take a spoonful than to
argue, I took some. My wife then insisted
on me taking the doses regularly.”
“ What effect did Clements Tonic have?”
“ Marvellous—it was like the touch of a
magician’s wand. You never saw a man get
better so quickly as I did. The cramps in
my stomach entirely ceased. My liver
began to resume its proper functions. My
digestion improved, and the food which
formerly caused pain, wind, and distress now
began to properly nourish
My Worn Out Frame.
A new hope sprang up. I began to bestir
myself, for I felt I had found a remedy to
which I could pin my faith. My nerves
became stronger, and I had no more nervous
twitchings. I had deeper and longer sleep
every night. Under the invigorating in-
fluence of Clements Tonic
I Threw Off My Nervousness
altogether. It was just like getting rid of
a heap of cankering chains. Instead of
being despondent I was now hopeful, if not
cheerful. I came along astonishingly. I
was always hungry. I never seemed able
to get enough to eat, and, naturally enough,
I began to put on flesh. My nerves con-
tinually improved. I laughed at being
nervous now. I soon regained my lost
weight, and was able to go about my work
again as well as ever. It was as though
years had been put on my life. ”
“ You think Clements Tonic did this for
you ? ”
“I Don’t Think—l’m Sure.
Plainly speaking, Clements Tonic dragged
me back out of the very jaws of death.”
“ About publishing this—you don’t
object?”
“No, on the contrary, I wish it to be made
public, and if you hadn’t called upon me, it
was my intention to make a special journey
to Sydney to put my case in the papers. I
wish to make the means of my miraculous
recovery public as a duty
I Owe to Mankind,
and may it bring hope and health to others
as it did to me.”
As our reporter was turning away,
Mr. Rose called after him :—“Now, don’t
you imagine I’ve exaggerated my sufferings
as some people think I do, for the fact is
that 'I was
Worse Than I’ve Described,
and lack of words alone has prevented me
from properly picturing to you the
Horrors of“ My illness,
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, William Thomas Rose, of High-street, Penrith,
in the colony of New South Wales, do solemnly and
sincerely declare that I have carefully read the an-
nexed document, co sisting of eleven folios and
consecutively numbered front one to eleven, and that
it contains and is a true and faithful account of my
illness ami cure by Clements Tonic, and also contains
my full permission to publish the same in any way;
and I make this solemn declaration conscientiously
believing the same to be true, and by virtue of the
- provisions of an Act made and passed in the ninth
year of the reign of her present Majesty, intituled
' “An Act for the more effec ual abolition of Oaths
■ and Affirmations taken and made in the various
Departments of the Government of New South Wales,
: and to substitute Declarations in lieu thereof, and for
the suppression of voluntary and extra-judicial Oaths
and Affidavits. ’’

Stolen Kisses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
Yes, so I imagined !
And still all is silence—
Not a board creaks now;
It seemed but a while since
We, listening intently,
Heard soft-footed noises,
The sound of doors swinging,
And whisper of voices.
Guilt made us sensitive :
Bodies a-quiver
With passionate lightnings
Made every nerve shiver ;
You breathed and I shuddered,
I sighed and you started,
We dreaded no dagger,
But feared to be parted.
How shall I meet him,
At breakfast behold him ?
With inward derision—
A white cheek uphold him,
Dissembling, as though his
Were welcome caresses:
But all the while thinking
Of you and your kisses 1
What shall I tell him,
If he discover ?
Why, flirt a fan—this wise—
And own you my lover !
But let us be secret
And hide our love beautiful:
You, the friend Faithful,
I, the wife Dutiful.
A sin—nay, to beauty
All tribute is given,
The right to fall sweetly
And still be forgiven : .
The Innermost Voices
I mock with light laughter,
My heaven’s my Lover :
For fools a Hereafter.
A truce to your banter ;
Love swells in a tidal
Resurgence, and all but
Love’s converse is idle :
My lips have a question,
Regards stolen kisses,
And yours—can they ansvver?
And—Love is delicious.
RODERIC QUINN.
Delightful notice in a Thursday Island
paper:—
“Please, gentlemen, anyone who wants to get printer
man, who has just come from Port Darwin, boat Captain
Rana Roderico, she leaves Port Darwin- 21st July and
arrived here on 19 August, and brought a printer man :
who is able to do printer your name in any letters on
your window or sign,’.’ &c., &c.
And this is how they babble in the Northern
Babel.
Cooktown.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
This is a description of the far Northern capi-
tal, as it was evolved from my mind. I have often
visited Cooktown, and here is the result of a num-
ber of aggravated impressions—or, better, depres-
sions.
■ • » • •
After the wet season, Cooktown is rather a
pretty place—a white patch nestling between
green hills and blue water, overshadowed by the
dark grandeur of Mt. Cook. The latitude is indi-
cated by here and there a graceful cocoa-palm
(strange how a single palm will give the most
commonplace landscape a dreamy tropical appear-
ance !) But, in summer, the town is truly awful
—a mere colony of redhot iron boilers, that have
scorched the vegetation off the surrounding coun-
try, The nakedness of the hills reveals only
empty jam-tins and lean goats, the two main
characteristics of a N.Q. settlement. During the
midday heat, the goats take their siesta on the
verandah of the local bank.
• * * •
Cooktown lives by spurts. It was born with a
spurt; and the Palmer, the Annan, and Papua
have at different times provided the “ready ” for
a royal burst. But between spurts Cooktown lan-
guishes exceedingly, and lives only by wharf-
lumping and playing in the Chinese lottery. A
stranger can find his way into town simply fol-
lowing the track of ancient tickets to windward.
Cooktown’s glories are its harbor and the Cook
monument. A monument in a galvanised-iron
town looks about as relevant as a crinoline in
Heaven. As for the harbor—the stranger has to
acquire a taste for it. But, after that, like man-
Soes, it is very nice. My first experience of the
arbor was on a fishing expedition. I sailed right
into the.centre of the basin at high tide and cast
my line. I had to walk back, and the boat was
pulled into the channel by a couple of horses. It
was very depressing.
* m m *
Ever since the days of the Palmer, Cooktown
has been the head-quarters of Queensland-China.
Cairns isn’t in it! There is a Chinese Consul,
and a brass joss of very considerable sanctity—
for an outside place like Australia. When this
eminent idol arrived in town, some years ago, the
slant-eyed residents celebrated the event with
much pomp and a champagne banquet. All the
local white celebrities were invited, and turned up
punctually. The time was Sunday, during church -
service, and the Christian temples were commen-
surately short of elders and wardens. Which
appeared quite a matter of course to the inhabi-
tants, and would never have penetrated to the
outside world but for the editor of the local paper.
He, being an enemy of the Chinese, wasn’t in-
vited.
■ * . * * ‘ * ’
There is a railway to the Laura. This terminus
consists of the station-shed and a small bush pub.
Some miles further on there is an iron bridge ;
but they forgot to lay the rails up to that. It does
not matter, anyhow. Nobody ever travels on the
line. When people are in a hurry they walk. I
made one trip up with the bi-weekly mail-train.
At the 7-mile a vegetable-Chow stopped the en-
gine and got on board with: his baskets. ' Ten
miles further on there was a 15-minutes wait,
while the driver and the stoker went to stalk a
plain turkey. They bagged it, and for the next
few miles the feathers whirled into my compart-
ment as they plucked the bird on the tender. So
I shut my window and went to sleep. It was
very depressing.
• • • *
Cooktown possesses 23 pubs., nearly all in the
main street. The landlord generally stands in the
bar-door. He fixes his eyes sleepily on you as you
come up from the wharf. Then he recognises
you as a stranger, and his face lights up.
His hospitable smile broadens in a reverse
ratio to the distance between you and his
pub. As you pass, his countenance lengthens
and his scorn for your meanness increases accord-
ing to the natural law aforementioned. And then
you approach the next hostelry. You cannot
bear . these reiterated scenes of hope and dis-
appointment very long. If you are soft-hearted,
you’ll break down at the third pub. I got to the
sixth. “ Please give me some whisky! ” “ Ain’t
got no whisky. Nobody drinks no whisky.”
“ Brandy ?”“No ! ” “ Beer ?—claret ? ’’—not
in stock. And so you must swallow your nip of
fiery draught rum. If you don’t finish it, the
landlord pours back the remains into the bottle
and returns to his station at the door.
* • • •
The only amusement is the nightly meeting of
the Salvation Army. Every citizen has been a
member, once at least. When there is cash about
he slides back ; but after the spree he comes up
again for Repentance. All the town knows the
favorite hymns by heart, and the spirit of the
Army seems to have soaked into the municipal
character. It is a sort of sub-conscious, everlast-
ing whipping of the cat. And it is very de-
pressing.
VON KOTZE.
There are 1700 persons employed by the
Wallaroo and Moonta Mining Co. (S.A.), but
practically none of the miners' have a vote for the
Leg. Council, as they are not freeholders and they
don’t pay rent. The co. lets them build on the
mining lease, and when a .miner leaves he is at.
liberty to sell his house if he can find a buyer.
Kingston’s Household Suffrage Bill, designed to
give a Council vote to every householder and his'
wife, would have enfranchised all these people—
who have been knocked out, however, by the sub-
stitution of a small rental qualification.
Artist Furniss must have had “ liver ”
when he was at Albany, en route to England. He
took a very yellow view of-the place, and, when he
got home, caricatured it unmercifully. Yet there
are few more picturesque.places in Australia than
Albany, which is also scrupulously clean and is an
ideal summer and health resort. But its fine
public buildings, its eight hotels, and 3000 of a
population make every new-comer ask “ How is it
done?” And the inhabitants.themselves dunno.
Albany has been a bit of a mystery for 40 years.
Tradition says it was once the home port of
numerous whalers, for \tfhose use small stores
sprang up, and one or two pubs, put in an appear-
ance. Then the ocean-going boats began to :call
in for coal (brought from various countries, and.
stowed in hulks) ; and, after that, sandalwood
broke out and brought a few ships to take it away.
But Albany’s streaks of luck have all been very
fitful and evanescent. Yetifc pays its debts, liveS'
on hope, and doesn’t worry.
The Biggest Rogue Wins.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
They reckoned they had a good thing on at
Bunyipvale with the =650 handicap. “They”
were Thompson, ot Nowlahmine, and Cleaby, of
Brown Creek, up the river.
Thompson’s Flo was a red-hot favorite at small
odds. She was a local mare ; all knew her, or
thought they did, and “ went on ” heavily.
Cleaby’s Neddy was unknown, save to a few,
among whom were Thompson. The latter was
uncertain how the race would end ; there was no
doubt it lay between Flo and Neddy, but that was
all Thompson knew about it. As the same thing
was giving Cleaby no little anxiety, these two
honest sports soon formed an acquaintance, each
determining to “ pump ” the other.
That was no go. But after- some fencing, they
understood each other so well that times, weights,
&c., were compared, and they found that the
favorite, Flo, could win, with a little to spare-
very little. And Flo was at even money. Not
much in that to encourage true sports.
The result was simple. Neddy suddenly jumped
in the betting from six to even money, and the
favorite went back to threes. Cleaby and Thomp-
son had not met for nothing ! An hour before the
race Thompson’s jockey got his orders “not to
win.” And Cleaby and Thompson wandered
about the course with the vacant expression of
men who didn’t know each other from Adam.
Next, the jockey saw a pal in a retired corner
behind a gum-tree, and the betting changed again.
Both horses started at evens, and the favorite won.
Neddy second.
*'• * *
Immediately after the race Cleaby (owner of
Neddy) protested. Flo had won a <£lo race at an
unregistered meeting. And Cleaby wore so bland
an expression that the idea of his having got his
information from- Thompson was too preposterous
for : belief, especially as Thompson was very angry,
and wanted to fight Cleaby on the spot.
Usual decision of country meetings—referred to
A.J.C.
. , i.j. • • *
Decision: Pay second horse. Thompson and
Cleaby jubilant. Go to draw their winnings from
the bookie, but—
< “Oh ! you can’t come that game on me ; I’m an
unregistered bookmaker, always pays first horse
past the post! you know.” He was a big, strong
brute,- and his fighting reputation extended from
river to river. What could they do ? what they
did—nothing, bar a little choice cursing. But
that’s a mere detail in racing circles.
• • *
Theqockey heard of this,, and suddenly realised
that the money he had instructed liis mate to put
on Flo that time they held counsel behind the
gum-tree wasn’t lost, after all. And he went to
collect his dividend on the horse that was first
past the post.
; “ What! pay on Flo. Why d me eyes, the
mare was disqualified. You don’t expect me to
pay against the A. J.C. decisions, do you ? What
do you take me for, a hinnocent kid, or a rogue?
come now ! ”
Mote cursing.

Bill, the Ventriloquist Rooster.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
We had a rooster at our place, named Bill, said
Mitchell. A big 1 mongrel of no particular breed ;
though the old lady said he was a “ brammer ”
and many an argument she had with the old man
about it too ; she was just as stubborn and obstin-
ate in her opinion as the governor was in his.
But, anyway, we called him Bill and didn’t take
no particular notice of him till a cousin of some of
us came from Sydney on a visit to the country,
and stayed at our place because it was cheaper
than stopping at a pub. Well, somehow this chap
got interested in Bill and studied him for two or
three days, and at last he says :
“ Why, that rooster's a ventriloquist! ”
“ A what ?”
“A ventriloquist ! ”
“ Go along with yer ! ”
“ But he is. I’ve heard of cases like this before;
but this is the first I’ve come across. Bill’s a
ventriloquist, right enough.”
And so he was, and, as it turned out, he was an
unconscious one at that, as the Sydney jackeroo
said. Then we, remembered that there wasn t
another rooster within five miles —our only neigh-
bor, an Irishman named Page, didn’t have onb at
the time—and we’d often heard another cock crow,
but didn t think to take any notice of it. We
watched Bill, and sure enough he was a ventrilo-
quist. The “ ka-coeka ” would come all right, but
the co*ka-koo-oi-uo ” seemed to come from a
distance. And sometimes the Whole' crow would
go wrong and come back like an echo that had
been lost for a year. Bill would stand on tiptoe,
and hold his elbows out, and curve his neck, and
go two or three times as if he was swallowing
nest-eggs, and nearly break his neck and burst his
gizzard; and then there’d be no sound at all
where he was—only a cock crowing' iri the dis-
tance.
And pretty soon we could see that Bill was
troubled about it. He was in great trouble about it.
You see, he didn’t know it was himself—thought
it was another rooster challenging him, and he
wanted badly to find that other bird. He would
get up on the wood-heap, and crow and listen-
crow and listen again— ero.w and listen, and then
he’d go up to the top of the paddock, and get upon
the stack, and crow and listen there. Then down
to the other end of the paddock, and get up on a
mullock-heap, and crow and listen there; then
across to the other side and up on a log among the
saplings, and crow ’n’ listen some more. He
searched all over the place for that other rooster,
but, of course, he couldn’t find him. Sometimes
he’d be out all day crowing and listening all over
the country, and then come home dead tired, and
rest and cool off in a hole that the hens had
scratched for him in a damp place under the
water-cask sledge.
Well, one day Page brought home a big white
rooster, and when he let it go it climbed up on
Page’s stack and crowed, to see if there was any
more roosters round there. Bill had come home
tired ; it was a hot day, and he’d rooted out the
hens, and was having a “spell-ho” under the
cask when the white rooster crowed. Bill, didn’t
lose any time getting out and on to the wood-heap
and then he waited till he heard the crow again ;
then he crowed, and the other rooster crowed’
again, and they crowed at each other for three
days, and called each other all the wretches
they could lay their tongues to, and after that
they implored each other to come out and be made
into chicken - soup and feather pillows. But
neither d come. You see, there was three crows—
there was Bill’s Crow, and the ventriloquist crow
and the white rooster’s crow—and each rooster
thought that there was two roosters in the opposi-
tion camp, and that he mightn’t get fair play, and
consequently both were afraid to put up their
hands.
But at last Bill couldn’t stand it any longer.
He made up his mind to go and have it out, even
if there was a whole agricultural show of prize
and honorable-mention fighting-cocks. in Page’s
yard. He got down from the wood-heap and
started off across the ploughed field, his head
down* his elbows out, and his thick, awkward
legs prodding away at the furrows behind for all
they were worth.
I wanted, to go down badly and see the fight,
and barrack for Bill. But I daren’t, because I’d
been coming up the road late the night before
with my brother Joe, and there was about three
panels of turkeys roosting along on the top rail of
Page’s front fence, and we brushed ’em with a
bough, and they got up such a blessed gobbling
fuss about it that Page came out in his shirt and
seen us running away; and I knew he was laying
for us with a bullock whip. Besides, there was
friction between the two families on account of a
thoroughbred bull that Page borrowed and
wouldn’t lend to us, and that got into our pad-
dock on account of me mending a panel in the
party fence, and carelessly leaving the top rail
down after sundown while our cows was moving
round' there in the saplings.
So there was too much friction for me to go
down ; but I climbed a tree as near the fence as I
could and watched. Bill reckoned he’d found
that rooster at last. The white rooster wouldn’t
come down from the stack, so Bill went up to him,
and they fought there till they tumbled down the
other side, and I couldn’t see any more. Wasn’t
I wild? I d have given my dog to have seen the
rest of the fight. I went down to the far side of
Page’s fence and climbed a tree there, but, of
course, I couldn’t see anything, so I came home
the back way. Just as I got home Page came
round to the front and sung out, “ Insoid there ! ”
And me and Jim went under the house like
snakes and looked out round a pile. But Page
was all right—he had a broad grin on his face
and Bdl safe under his arm. He put Bill down
on the ground very carefully, and says he to the
old folks:
“ Yer’e rooster knocked the stuffin’ out o’ my
rooster, but I bear no malice. ’Twas a grand
foight.”
And then the old man and Pago had a yarn ,
and got pretty friendly after that. ; And Bill
didn’t seem to bother about any more ventrilo-
quism, but the white rooster spent a lot of time
looking for that other rooster. Perhaps he
thought he’d have better luck with him. But
after that Page was on the look-out to get
a.rooster that would lick our rooster. Did
nothing else for a month but ride round and
enquire about roosters, till at last he borrowed a
game-bird in town, left <£s deposit on him, and
brought him home. And Page and the old man
agreed to have a match—about the only thing
they’d agreed about for five years. And they
fixed it up for a Sunday when the old lady anil
the girls and kids were going on a visit to some
relations about 15 miles away—to stop all night.
The guvnor made me go with them on horseback,
but I knew what was up, and so my pony went
lame about a mile along the road, and I had to
come back and turn him ont in the top paddock,
and hide the saddle and bridle in a hollow log t
and sneak home and climb up on the roof of the
shed. It was a awful hot day, and I had to keep
climbing backward and forward over the ridge-
pole all the morning to keep out of sight of the
old man, for he was moving about a good dead.
Well, after dinner, the fellows from roundabout
began to ride in and hang up their horses round
the place till it looked as if there was going to be
a funeral. Some of the chaps saw me, of course,
but I tipped them the wink, and they gave me
the office whenever the old man happened around.
Well, Page came along with his game-rooster.
Its name was Jim. It wasn’t much to look at,
and it seemed a good deal smaller and weaker
than Bill. Some of the chaps were disgusted, and
said it wasn’t a game-rooster at all. Bill’d settle
it in one lick, and they wouldn't have any fun at
all.
Well, they brought the game one out and put
him down near the woodheap and roused Bill
out from under his cask. He got interested at
once. He looked at Jim, aiid got up on the wood-
heap and crowed and looked at Jim again. He
reckoned this at last was the fowl that had been
humbugging him all along. Presently his trouble
caught him, and then he’d crow and take a squint
at the game ’un and crow again, and have another
squint at gamey, and try to crow and keep his eye
on the game rooster at the same time. But Jim
never committed himself until at last lie happened
to gape just after Bill’s whole crow went wrong,
and Bill spotted him. He reckoned he’d caught
him this time, and he got down off that wood-
heap and went for the foe. But Jim ran away—
and Bill ran after him.
Round and round the wood-heap they went, and
round the shod, and round the house and under it,
and back again, and round the wood-heap and
over it and round the other way, and they kept it
up for close on an hour. Bill’s bill was just within
an inch or so of the game rooster’s tail feathers
most of the time, but he couldn’t get any nearer,
do how he liked. And all the time the fellers
kept chyakin Page and singing out. “ What
price yer game ’un, Page ? Go it, Bill ! Go it
old cock ! ” and all that sort of thing. Well, the
game rooster went as if it was a go-as-you-please,
and he didn’t care if it lasted a year. He didn’t
seem to take any interest in the business, but Bill
got excited, and by-and-by he got mad. He held
his head lower and lower and his wings further
a n further out from his sides, and prodded away
harder and harder afc the ground behind, but it
wasn’t any use. Jim seemed to keep ahead without
trying. They stuck to the woodheap towards the
last. They went round first one way for a while,
and then the other for a change, and now and
then they’d go over the top to break the mono-
tony; and the chaps got more interested in the
race than they would have been in the fight—and
betted on it too. But Bill was handicapped.« ith
his weight. He was done up at last ; he slowed
down till he couldn’t waddle, arid then, when he
was thoroughly knocked up, that game rooster
turned on him and gave him* the father of a
hiding.
And my father caught me when I’d got down
in' the excitement, and wasn’t thinking, and he
gave me the step father of a hiding. But he had
a lively time with the old lady afterwards, over
the cock-fight.
Bill was so disgusted with himself that he went
under the cask and died !
Syd., ’93.
HENRY LAWSON.
At the taking of the last census in F ranee
9,000,000 people declared themselves atheists.
Charles M‘Donald, M.L.A., was strongly
condemned by nearly the whole Queensland press
for opposing the voting of a message of condolence
to the Emperor of Austria on his recent bereave-
ment. The Q. Govt.’s action was prompted (it is
alleged) by “the deep sympathy felt for an un-
fortunate monarch in his hour of trial. ” Bah !
What sympathy did the Q. Govt, wire along to the
city of Milan when its poor, bread-hunting mul-
titudes were shot down by hundreds in the
streets ? This pretence of overwhelming grief for
the sorrows of a man you never met and never
knew is merely an excuse, in most cases, for a
fresh outburst of grovel.
Dear Bulletin.— The ‘‘grocer soul” in
me prompts the weighing of respective merits of
two recently-dead men. Bismarck was a greater
man than Gladstone, because he did not play to
the gallery. The latter , was ever posing to the
small-souled part of the B.P. —the mud-headed
Exeter Hall and Dean Farrar sections. And the
fact that he was their idol is a sufficient indict-
ment of his “greatness.” His Democracy was
merely a political counter, as was his remaining a
commoner. He was a theoretical philanthropist—
of the sort that would rather (jive Bibles to niggers
than a square feed to a tramp. Gladstone seems
never to have been a boy or a young man, and his
lack of humor was phenomenal. Bismarck, who
went through the whole gamut of youth in an
aggressively youthful manner, worked for one
grand idea, carried it out, and he never truckled
to the mob. At the best, Gladstone only anti-
cipated— where Bismarck created—public opinion.
Finally, B. had no cant —G. was cant itself !
Cosmos.
